Division of Compounds into Ensouled and Inanimate
Al-Jurjani introduces the division of compounds with mixture into those with souls and minerals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of compounds with mixture into ensouled (plants and animals) and minerals, and the structure of the upcoming section.

Core Idea
Compounds with mixture are either ensouled or inanimate.

In this passage, al-Jurjani sets the stage for discussing compounds that possess mixture (mizāj). He notes that such compounds form the majority, and they are divided into two fundamental categories: those endowed with a soul (vegetative or animal) and those without a soul, namely minerals. This classification organizes the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get an overall sense of the classification being made.
Notice the use of brackets indicating the commentator's additions.
2
Identify the main division
The passage divides compounds with mixture into two types: those with a soul and those without. Find where this division is stated.
The phrase 'ينقسم إلى ما له نفس... وإلى ما لا نفس له' is the key.
3
Understand the basis
The passage mentions that compounds without mixture are few, so the focus is on the majority with mixture. Why is this distinction important?
It sets the scope for the rest of the section.
4
Note the upcoming chapters
The passage ends by stating that this section contains three chapters. This prepares you for the detailed discussion that follows.
Keep this structure in mind as you proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الأول : الصور الحالة في الجسم [ القسم الرابع في المركبات التي لها مزاج وهي الأكثر ] من المركبات لأن مالا مزاج له منها قليل بالقياس إلى ماله مزاج ( وهو ) أي هذا الأكثر ( ينقسم إلى ما له نفس إما نباتية أو حيوانية ) وإلى ما لا نفس (له وهو المعدنيات ( وفيه ثلاثة فصول ) .
The First Observatory - Fourth Section: First Objective: The Forms Subsisting in the Body. [The fourth section is about compounds that have mixture, and they are the majority] of compounds, because those without mixture are few in comparison to those with mixture. And this majority is divided into that which has a soul, either plant or animal, and that which has no soul, which is minerals. And it contains three chapters.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the fourth section of the first observatory, focusing on compounds that possess mixture (mizāj). The author notes that such compounds are the majority; those without mixture are rare. These majority compounds are then divided into two categories: those with a soul (either vegetative or animal) and those without a soul, i.e., minerals. The section will include three chapters.

Key Terms
المركبات
المزاج
النفس النباتية
النفس الحيوانية
المعدنيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All compounds have mixture.
Correction: The passage states that only the majority of compounds have mixture; those without mixture are few.

Checkpoint
How are the majority of compounds divided according to this passage?
A) Into those with mixture and those without
B) Into those with a soul and those without a soul
C) Into plants and animals
D) Into minerals and elements

Key Takeaway
Compounds with mixture are divided into ensouled (plants and animals) and inanimate minerals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage reminds you that you are not merely a mineral or plant, but an ensouled being with an animal soul and higher faculties. In what ways does your daily life reflect this truth? Are there areas where you live as if you were only physical, ignoring the soul that distinguishes you? What will you change today to honor the soul God has given you?
?
The passage notes that most compounds have mixture, but not all. How does this precise observation of creation deepen your appreciation for the wisdom and order of the Creator? Can you recall a moment when you were struck by the intricate design of a living thing? How might that awe transform your worship?
?
If you truly believed that you are a compound with a soul destined for accountability, would you live differently? What is one habit or thought pattern that contradicts this belief, and what step will you take right now to align your life with the truth that you are not just matter but a living soul?

The Form Acts on Its Own Matter First
Al-Jurjani states the principle of form acting first on its own matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a corporeal form first affects its own matter before affecting adjacent matter, and that this principle applies to all elemental qualities.

Core Idea
A form's first effect is on its own substance, then on its neighbor.

This passage introduces a fundamental principle of physical interaction: every specific form (like heat, cold, moisture, dryness) acts first on the matter it inheres in, then on the matter of adjacent bodies. This establishes the priority of self-effect before external effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the first sentence carefully. The author states that the corporeal form acts first on its own matter, then on the matter of what is adjacent.
Note that 'corporeal form' is glossed as 'specific form'.
2
Examine the example
Look at the example of fire. Fire first heats its own matter (the fiery substance) and then heats adjacent matter.
Consider how this applies to other elements like water or earth.
3
Generalize the principle
The author states that this pattern holds for all qualities (heat, cold, etc.) and all elements.
This is a general law of elemental interaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الصورة الجسمية ) أي الصورة الحالة في الجسم التي هي مبدأ الآثار وهي الصورة النوعية ) تفعل أولاً في (مادتها التي حلّت هي فيها (ثم في مادة ما يجاورها ) . فالصورة النارية تسخن مادتها ثم مادة ما يجاورها، وكذا الحال في سائر الكيفيات وباقي العناصر ) فالمجاورة شرط للتفاعل ) الواقع بين الأجسام. ألا ترى أنّ النار لا تسخن إلا ما له وضع مخصوص وقرب معين بالنسبة إليها فإذا حصلت المجاورة بلا مماسة أمكن التفاعل بين الجسمين ) وأبلغ من ذلك ) التفاعل الحاصل بمجرد المجاورة. ( ما كان أي التفاعل الذي كان ( بالمماسة ) التي هي الغاية في المجاورة ( والمماسة إنما تكون بالسطوح و لا شك في أنه ( كلما كان السطوح أكثر كانت المماسة بها ( أتم، وذلك ) أي تكثر السطوح (إنما هو بحسب تصغر الأجزاء و إذا تحققت ما صورناه لك فنقول : ( العناصر المختلفة الكيفية ) التي هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ( إذا) تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت اختلاطاً تاماً ( حتى حصل التماس الكامل ( بين أجزائها فعل صورة (كل) منها ( في مادة الآخر فكسرت منه سورة كيفية ) المضادة لكيفيتها ( حتى نقص ( العنصر البارد بفعل صورته قوله: فكسرت من سورة (كيفيته هاهنا احتمالان أحدهما وهو الأنسب بظاهر العبارة أن يبقى كيفية كل من العناصر الممتزجة. وإنما تنكسر سورة تلك الكيفية فقط بحيث تلتئم من الكيفيات المنكسرة كيفية متشابهة في جميع الأجزاء. وثانيهما أن ينخلع كيفية كل واحد منها ويفيض على مجموعها من المبدأ الفياض كيفية واحدة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الممتزج ومع كل واحد من الاحتمالين كانت صورها النوعية باقية في أنفسها. هذا ثم إن الاحتمال الأول ينسب إلى الأطباء والاحتمال الثاني ينسب إلى الحكماء.
They said: The corporeal form – i.e., the form subsisting in the body, which is the principle of effects, and it is the specific form – acts first on its matter in which it is subsisting, then on the matter of what is adjacent to it. Thus the fiery form heats its matter, then the matter of what is adjacent to it, and similarly for the other qualities and the remaining elements. Therefore, contiguity is a condition for the interaction occurring between bodies. Do you not see that fire only heats what has a specific position and a certain proximity to it? So when contiguity occurs without contact, interaction between the two bodies is possible. And more powerful than that is the interaction that occurs by mere contiguity, i.e., the interaction that occurs by contact, which is the ultimate degree of contiguity. Contact only occurs by surfaces, and there is no doubt that the more surfaces there are, the more complete the contact by them. That is, the multiplication of surfaces happens only by the smallness of the parts. And when you understand what we have depicted for you, we say: The elements differing in quality – which are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness – when their parts become very small and mix completely until complete contact occurs between their parts, the form of each acts on the matter of the other, and thus the force of the quality opposite to its quality is broken, so that the cold element decreases by the action of its form. His saying: 'the force of its quality is broken' – here there are two probabilities: The first, which is more appropriate to the apparent wording, is that the quality of each of the mixed elements remains, but only the force of that quality is broken, such that from the broken qualities a uniform quality arises in all parts. The second is that the quality of each one is stripped off, and from the Bestowing Principle a single uniform quality flows over the whole aggregate in all parts of that mixture. And with each of the two probabilities, their specific forms remain in themselves. Then note that the first probability is attributed to the physicians, and the second to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanism of how substances interact through their forms. It states that the form (specific form) acts first on its own matter and then on adjacent matter, using the example of fire heating itself then its surroundings. Contiguity is a necessary condition for interaction; even without direct contact, interaction can occur if bodies are contiguous. However, interaction via direct contact (tamās) is stronger. Contact occurs through surfaces, and the more surfaces (i.e., the more finely divided the parts), the more complete the contact. When the elements' qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are mixed, their forms act on each other, weakening the opposing qualities. This is illustrated by the cold element being diminished. Then the commentary discusses two interpretations of the phrase 'the force of its quality is broken'. The first interpretation holds that the original qualities remain but their force is dampened, resulting in a uniform mixture. The second holds that the original qualities are replaced by a new single quality from the divine principle. Both maintain that the specific forms persist. The first view is attributed to physicians, the second to philosophers.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الصورة النوعية
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the corporeal form act on first?
A) Its own matter
B) Adjacent matter
C) Both simultaneously
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The corporeal form first affects its own material substrate before influencing adjacent matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you try to change others (your neighbor), do you honestly examine and address the condition of your own soul (your own matter)? What would it look like today to let the form of faith first transform your own substance before you speak into someone else's life?
?
When you feel the 'heat' of anger or passion, does it first consume you before it affects others? How can you ensure that the first effect of any quality within you is on your own character, not on those around you?
?
If your actions truly begin with your own inner state, what is one habit or attitude you will consciously work on today, knowing that your influence on others depends on it?

Contiguity, Contact, and Surface Area
Al-Jurjani explains contiguity as condition and the role of surface area.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that contiguity is necessary for interaction, contact is more powerful, and smaller parts increase surface area for better contact.

Core Idea
More surfaces mean stronger contact and interaction.

Having established that forms act on adjacent matter, the author now explains the conditions for this interaction. Contiguity (neighboring) is required, even without direct touch, but contact (tamās) is more effective. The extent of contact depends on the surface area, which increases when parts are smaller.

Guided Path
1
Understand contiguity as a condition
The author states that contiguity (jīwār) is necessary for interaction between bodies. Even without direct contact, interaction can occur if bodies are adjacent.
Example: fire heats nearby air without touching it.
2
Distinguish contact from mere contiguity
Interaction via direct contact (tamās) is more powerful than mere proximity. Contact is the highest degree of contiguity.
Contact occurs by surfaces meeting.
3
Relate surface area to smallness
The more surfaces involved, the more complete the contact. This happens when parts become smaller, increasing total surface area.
Smaller particles have more surface area per volume.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الصورة الجسمية ) أي الصورة الحالة في الجسم التي هي مبدأ الآثار وهي الصورة النوعية ) تفعل أولاً في (مادتها التي حلّت هي فيها (ثم في مادة ما يجاورها ) . فالصورة النارية تسخن مادتها ثم مادة ما يجاورها، وكذا الحال في سائر الكيفيات وباقي العناصر ) فالمجاورة شرط للتفاعل ) الواقع بين الأجسام. ألا ترى أنّ النار لا تسخن إلا ما له وضع مخصوص وقرب معين بالنسبة إليها فإذا حصلت المجاورة بلا مماسة أمكن التفاعل بين الجسمين ) وأبلغ من ذلك ) التفاعل الحاصل بمجرد المجاورة. ( ما كان أي التفاعل الذي كان ( بالمماسة ) التي هي الغاية في المجاورة ( والمماسة إنما تكون بالسطوح و لا شك في أنه ( كلما كان السطوح أكثر كانت المماسة بها ( أتم، وذلك ) أي تكثر السطوح (إنما هو بحسب تصغر الأجزاء و إذا تحققت ما صورناه لك فنقول : ( العناصر المختلفة الكيفية ) التي هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ( إذا) تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت اختلاطاً تاماً ( حتى حصل التماس الكامل ( بين أجزائها فعل صورة (كل) منها ( في مادة الآخر فكسرت منه سورة كيفية ) المضادة لكيفيتها ( حتى نقص ( العنصر البارد بفعل صورته قوله: فكسرت من سورة (كيفيته هاهنا احتمالان أحدهما وهو الأنسب بظاهر العبارة أن يبقى كيفية كل من العناصر الممتزجة. وإنما تنكسر سورة تلك الكيفية فقط بحيث تلتئم من الكيفيات المنكسرة كيفية متشابهة في جميع الأجزاء. وثانيهما أن ينخلع كيفية كل واحد منها ويفيض على مجموعها من المبدأ الفياض كيفية واحدة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الممتزج ومع كل واحد من الاحتمالين كانت صورها النوعية باقية في أنفسها. هذا ثم إن الاحتمال الأول ينسب إلى الأطباء والاحتمال الثاني ينسب إلى الحكماء.
They said: The corporeal form – i.e., the form subsisting in the body, which is the principle of effects, and it is the specific form – acts first on its matter in which it is subsisting, then on the matter of what is adjacent to it. Thus the fiery form heats its matter, then the matter of what is adjacent to it, and similarly for the other qualities and the remaining elements. Therefore, contiguity is a condition for the interaction occurring between bodies. Do you not see that fire only heats what has a specific position and a certain proximity to it? So when contiguity occurs without contact, interaction between the two bodies is possible. And more powerful than that is the interaction that occurs by mere contiguity, i.e., the interaction that occurs by contact, which is the ultimate degree of contiguity. Contact only occurs by surfaces, and there is no doubt that the more surfaces there are, the more complete the contact by them. That is, the multiplication of surfaces happens only by the smallness of the parts. And when you understand what we have depicted for you, we say: The elements differing in quality – which are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness – when their parts become very small and mix completely until complete contact occurs between their parts, the form of each acts on the matter of the other, and thus the force of the quality opposite to its quality is broken, so that the cold element decreases by the action of its form. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that fire only heats objects in a specific position and proximity. Are you placing yourself in the right spiritual 'position' and 'proximity' to be transformed by divine truth? What one habit could you change this week to increase your 'surface area' of exposure to the Quran?
?

Have you settled for proximity without the depth of direct encounter? What would it take for you to break your heart into smaller, more vulnerable pieces before Him?

Mixing Elements and Two Interpretations
Al-Jurjani explains mixing and two interpretations of quality change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how forms of different elements interact during mixing, and the two scholarly views on whether qualities remain or are replaced.

Core Idea
When elements mix, their forms break each other's opposing qualities, but their specific forms persist.

The final part of the passage deals with the actual mixing of elemental parts. When elements with opposite qualities (e.g., hot and cold) are finely divided and mixed, each form acts on the other's matter, weakening the opposing quality. The passage then presents two interpretations of what exactly happens to the qualities, with physicians and philosophers disagreeing.

Guided Path
1
Read the mixing scenario
The author describes elements with different qualities (heat, cold, etc.) being divided into fine parts and thoroughly mixed until complete contact occurs.
This results in each form acting on the matter of the other.
2
Understand the effect on qualities
The action of the form 'breaks the force' of the opposite quality, leading to a balanced mixture. The example says the cold element decreases.
The specific forms themselves are not destroyed.
3
Compare the two probabilities
First view (physicians): The original qualities remain but their force is broken, producing a uniform mixture. Second view (philosophers): The original qualities are stripped off and a new single quality is bestowed by the divine principle.
Both views agree that the specific forms continue to exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الصورة الجسمية ) أي الصورة الحالة في الجسم التي هي مبدأ الآثار وهي الصورة النوعية ) تفعل أولاً في (مادتها التي حلّت هي فيها (ثم في مادة ما يجاورها ) . فالصورة النارية تسخن مادتها ثم مادة ما يجاورها، وكذا الحال في سائر الكيفيات وباقي العناصر ) فالمجاورة شرط للتفاعل ) الواقع بين الأجسام. ألا ترى أنّ النار لا تسخن إلا ما له وضع مخصوص وقرب معين بالنسبة إليها فإذا حصلت المجاورة بلا مماسة أمكن التفاعل بين الجسمين ) وأبلغ من ذلك ) التفاعل الحاصل بمجرد المجاورة. ( ما كان أي التفاعل الذي كان ( بالمماسة ) التي هي الغاية في المجاورة ( والمماسة إنما تكون بالسطوح و لا شك في أنه ( كلما كان السطوح أكثر كانت المماسة بها ( أتم، وذلك ) أي تكثر السطوح (إنما هو بحسب تصغر الأجزاء و إذا تحققت ما صورناه لك فنقول : ( العناصر المختلفة الكيفية ) التي هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ( إذا) تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت اختلاطاً تاماً ( حتى حصل التماس الكامل ( بين أجزائها فعل صورة (كل) منها ( في مادة الآخر فكسرت منه سورة كيفية ) المضادة لكيفيتها ( حتى نقص ( العنصر البارد بفعل صورته قوله: فكسرت من سورة (كيفيته هاهنا احتمالان أحدهما وهو الأنسب بظاهر العبارة أن يبقى كيفية كل من العناصر الممتزجة. وإنما تنكسر سورة تلك الكيفية فقط بحيث تلتئم من الكيفيات المنكسرة كيفية متشابهة في جميع الأجزاء. وثانيهما أن ينخلع كيفية كل واحد منها ويفيض على مجموعها من المبدأ الفياض كيفية واحدة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الممتزج ومع كل واحد من الاحتمالين كانت صورها النوعية باقية في أنفسها. هذا ثم إن الاحتمال الأول ينسب إلى الأطباء والاحتمال الثاني ينسب إلى الحكماء.
They said: The corporeal form – i.e., the form subsisting in the body, which is the principle of effects, and it is the specific form – acts first on its matter in which it is subsisting, then on the matter of what is adjacent to it. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when elements mix, their qualities are completely destroyed and the forms vanish.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that specific forms persist; only the force of qualities is broken or replaced.

The Objection and Answer on the Perfectly Balanced Temperament
Al-Jurjani records and refutes the objection that a perfectly balanced temperament cannot exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection against the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament and how it is refuted by appealing to external causes and the possibility of rare occurrence.

Core Idea
The objection assumes impossibility, but the answer shows rarity—a crucial distinction.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents an objection that a perfectly balanced temperament cannot exist due to the equal resistance of its parts. He then provides a refutation showing that external causes can enable such a temperament, even if it is rare. This lesson walks through the argument and its response, clarifying a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the premise that equal parts cannot force each other to stay together because their natural inclinations drive them apart.
Notice that the objection relies on the assumption of no external interference.
2
Identify the conclusion of the objection
The objection concludes that separation occurs before any interaction, so no temperament can form.
3
Read the answer
Pay attention to the external cause that reverses the positions of the parts, causing them to hinder each other and remain together.
This is the key move in the refutation.
4
Understand the concession and rebuttal
The answer admits such a temperament is rare and impermanent, but denies absolute impossibility.
The cause of persistence may be external, not limited to elemental dominance.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why the objection's claim of impossibility is too strong and how the answer corrects it.
This illustrates a common pattern in kalam: refuting an opponent's claim of impossibility by showing a possible scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قالوا: وإنه لا يوجد) في الخارج (إذ أجزاؤه متساوية) في الميل إلى أحيازها متقاومة (فلا يقسر بعضها بعضاً على الاجتماع لامتناع أن يغلب بعض من الأمور المتساوية المتقاومة بعضاً آخر منها، وطبائعها داعية إلى الافتراق) بالتوجه إلى أحيازها الطبيعية المختلفة (فيحصل الافتراق قبل حصول الفعل والانفعال فإنه حادث يستدعي مدة معتداً بها لأنه حركة من كيفية إلى أخرى بعيدة عنها بخلاف) الافتراق الذي يكفيه أدنى حركة مع كونه موجوداً في كل آن من زمانها (فلا يحصل بينها مزاج) لتوقفه على حصول تلك الحركة وحدوثه عند انقطاعها. (والجواب: أنه ربما تقع الأجزاء لأسباب خارجية (بحيث تكون المائلة إلى العلو) كالنار والهواء (في جهة السفل والعكس. أي وتقع الأجزاء المائلة إلى السفل كالأرض والماء في جهة العلو، فتتمانع) الأجزاء وتتقاوم لتساوي قواها في الميول وتبقى مجتمعة (فيحصل المزاج بتفاعلها. (نعم يندر وجود (ذلك) المعتدل ولا يكون باقياً مستمراً إما لسرعة التحلل أو لسرعة غلبة بعض أجزائه على بعض (وأما الامتناع فلا، كيف وبقاء الاجتماع قد يكون لمنفصل كأصل الاجتماع الذي لا بد له من مقتض سوى الأجزاء (إذ لسبب (لبقاء الاجتماع (غير منحصر في غلبة عنصر) وهو ظاهر.
They said: And it (the perfectly balanced temperament) does not exist in the external world because its parts are equal in inclination to their natural places, mutually resisting. So one part does not compel another to aggregate because it is impossible for one of the equal, mutually resisting things to overcome another. And their natures call for separation by heading to their different natural places. Thus separation occurs before the occurrence of action and passion, for they (action and passion) are an event that requires a considerable duration because they are a movement from one quality to another far from it, unlike separation which is satisfied with the least movement while it exists in every moment of its time. So no temperament occurs between them because it is dependent on the occurrence of that movement and its happening at its termination. The answer: It is possible that the parts, due to external causes, are placed such that those inclining upward (like fire and air) are in the direction of downward, and vice versa. That is, the parts inclining downward (like earth and water) are placed in the direction of upward. So they hinder each other and resist each other due to the equality of their forces in inclination, and they remain aggregated. Thus temperament occurs by their interaction. Indeed, the existence of such a balanced temperament is rare and not permanent, either due to its rapid dissolution or the rapid predominance of some of its parts over others. But impossibility is not the case; how can it be, since the persistence of aggregation may be due to something separate, like the original aggregation which must have a cause other than the parts? For the cause of persistence of aggregation is not limited to the predominance of an element. This is clear.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament (al-mizaj al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi). The objectors argue that such a temperament cannot exist in the external world because its constituent parts are equal in their natural inclinations toward their own spheres, and they mutually resist each other. Due to this equality, no part can overpower another to force them to stay together; rather, their natures drive them to separate, heading to their natural places. Separation occurs before any action and passion (i.e., qualitative change) can take place, because action and passion require a measurable duration, while separation happens instantly. Therefore, they conclude, no temperament can form. The answer counters that external causes can position the parts so that those tending upward (fire, air) are placed downward, and those tending downward (earth, water) are placed upward, causing them to hinder each other. With equal forces, they remain together and interact, producing a temperament. However, it is admitted that such a balanced temperament is rare and impermanent, either dissolving quickly or having one part dominate. But impossibility is denied: the persistence of aggregation may be due to an external cause beyond the parts themselves, and the cause of aggregation is not confined to the dominance of one element. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
مزاج
معتدل
أجزاء
تقاوم
اجتماع
افتراق
فعل وانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that a perfectly balanced temperament is absolutely impossible.
Correction: The answer shows that it is merely rare and not impossible, as external causes can enable it.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the objection against the existence of a perfectly balanced temperament?
A) The parts are exactly equal in strength, so they cannot overcome each other to stay together.
B) The parts are too different to interact.
C) External forces always prevent aggregation.
D) Action and passion occur too quickly for temperament to form.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it ignores external causes and confuses rarity with impossibility.

Classification of Temperaments: Medical Balance and Eightfold Division
Al-Jurjani defines medically balanced temperament and divides all temperaments into eight categories based on four aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of medically balanced temperament and how all temperaments are classified into eight categories through four aspects and two perspectives.

Core Idea
Temperamental balance is about proportional justice, not equal parts.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the medical concept of balanced temperament (al-mu'tadil bihasab al-tibb). Al-Jurjani explains that true balance is not absolute equality but a proportional mix suited to each being's nature. He then presents an eightfold classification of temperaments derived from considering four aspects—type, species, individual, and organ—each evaluated in relation to internal perfection and external existence. This classification provides a sophisticated framework for understanding the natural temperaments of living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall flow. Notice the definition of 'medically balanced' and the subsequent classification.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of medically balanced
Identify that balance here means a predominance of qualities appropriate to the being, not equality. This is distinct from absolute equilibrium.
Derived from 'justice in distribution' (al-'adl), not al-ta'adul.
3
Grasp the eightfold division
Note that both the balanced and unbalanced each divide into eight categories. The eight arise from four aspects (type, species, individual, organ) each considered in two ways (internal and external).
4
Analyze the type equilibrium example
Study how type equilibrium is explained: it has a range (width) with extremes, and an optimum at the center. The external version is necessary for existence; the internal is for best perfection.
This pattern is analogized to species, individual, and organ.
5
Reflect on the classification's practical value
Consider why this classification is important for medicine and natural philosophy: it allows precise discussion of temperament variation.
It shows that balance is relative to context.

Then they said: And what is not truly balanced: if there predominates over it from the parts in quantity and from the qualities in intensity what is appropriate and suitable for it in its properties and effects, like predominant heat in the lion for its courage, and predominant cold in the rabbit for its cowardice, then it is the balanced according to medicine. It exists and is derived not from equilibrium (al-ta'adul) which is equality, but from justice (al-'adl) in distribution, as if the mixture has received the due share of elements appropriate to its temperament. Otherwise, i.e., if it is not predominant over it by what is appropriate, but rather predominates by what is not appropriate, then it is not balanced. And each of the two categories, i.e., the medically balanced and its opposite, divides into eight divisions. For the balanced, because it may be considered relative to four matters: type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw). And each of these four is considered relative to the internal sometimes and to the external other times. So every type of compound temperaments has a temperament without which the specific form cannot exist. And that temperament is not fixed to one limit that it cannot exceed; otherwise all individuals of one type, e.g., man, would be alike in temperament and consequent character and morality. Rather, it has a width between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness with two extremes: excess and deficiency. If the temperament exits that width, it would not be that type. So that is its specific equilibrium (i'tidal naw'i). And the most appropriate of its temperaments relative to the types external to it: the temperament of a human body is appropriate to it insofar as it is human, not the temperament of a horse or donkey. That is because it is suitable for its required effects, so that if it exits to any of those other temperaments, it dies. And for each type there is also a temperament lying within that width, i.e., at the exact middle between the two extremes of the type's width. It is the most appropriate of the temperaments within that width, and through it its state in what it was created for (its specific qualities and effects) is the best possible. That is its specific equilibrium relative to what enters into it of species or individual. So the specific equilibrium measured externally is needed for the type's existence and occurs for every individual of it with varying degrees. The one measured internally is needed for the type's best perfection and occurs only for the most balanced individual of the most balanced species of that type, and also only in its most balanced states. Upon that, analogize the remaining three: the species equilibrium measured externally is that which is appropriate for a species of a type measured against the temperaments of its other species, and it has a width with two extremes that is smaller than the type width, being part of it. If it exits that, it would not be that species. Measured internally, it is the temperament at the exact middle of this width, and it is the most appropriate for the species, because through it its state is best in what it was created for. It occurs only for the most balanced individual of that species in its most balanced states, whether that species is the most balanced or not. The individual equilibrium measured externally is that which the individual needs for its continued healthy existence, and it is appropriate to it measured against the temperaments of individuals of its species. It also has a width that is part of the species width. Measured internally, it is that through which the individual is in its best states. And the organ equilibrium measured externally...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage classifies temperaments. It begins by defining the 'medically balanced' (al-mu'tadil bihasab al-tibb) as a temperament that is not absolutely equal but has a predominance of qualities appropriate to the being's characteristics and effects, like heat in the lion and cold in the rabbit. This balance is derived from justice in distribution, not from mathematical equality. The opposite is the 'not balanced' where inappropriate qualities predominate. Then it states that each of these two main categories divides into eight divisions. This eightfold division arises because the balanced (and similarly the unbalanced) can be considered relative to four aspects: type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw), and each aspect is considered in two ways: relative to the internal (what perfects the thing itself) and relative to the external (what allows it to exist among others). The passage then explains each: for type equilibrium, there is a range (width) within which the type exists; if the temperament falls outside, the type ceases. Within that range, there is an optimum at the exact middle (the most balanced) that gives the best effects. Examples are given for human type. The same logic applies to species, individual, and organ equilibria: each has an external range for existence and an internal optimum for perfection. The passage follows the order: type, species, individual, and organ, and asks the reader to analogize the remaining three from the explanation of type equilibrium.

Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states it is derived from justice in distribution, not equality, and gives examples of lions (hot) and rabbits (cold) as balanced for their species.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The classification of temperaments into eight categories shows that balance is relative to the being's nature and purpose, and each level (type, species, individual, organ) has its own range and optimum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one dominant quality in your own character—something like heat or cold, courage or timidity. Is it appropriate for the roles Allah has placed you in (as a parent, friend, worker, worshipper)? How might you seek justice (not equality) in balancing this quality so it serves your purpose rather than hinders it? What concrete step will you take today to adjust that quality toward the 'just' middle?
?
You are a human being, yet your temperament is not the same as the 'best possible' human temperament. That optimum is rare and belongs to the most balanced individuals in their best states. Does that fact humble you? Or does it drive you to seek a better balance in your inner and outer life? Which state of your soul—anger, desire, intellect—is farthest from that optimum, and what will you do to bring it closer?
?
Al-Jurjani says that if a human were to exit its type's temperament width and take on the temperament of a horse or donkey, it would die. In your spiritual life, have you ever tried to 'be' something you are not—to adopt qualities that are not appropriate for your nature as a human created to know and worship Allah? What 'foreign temperament' have you been imitating, and how can you return to your own fitting balance today?

Generic Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Nawʿī)
Al-Jurjani defines generic moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of generic moderation and its two aspects: external (shared by all individuals of a species) and internal (the most balanced temperament at the midpoint).

Core Idea
Generic moderation is a range, not a fixed point, with two aspects: existence and perfection.

This lesson introduces the concept of generic moderation, which is the temperament necessary for a species to exist. Al-Jurjani explains that it is not a single fixed point but a range between extremes, and distinguishes between the external aspect (which keeps the species alive) and the internal aspect (which enables its best possible state). Understanding this distinction is foundational for the other three types of moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on generic moderation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani first defines generic temperament as a range, then explains its two aspects.
Pay attention to the terms 'عرض' (range) and 'حاق الوسط' (midpoint).
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
In the passage, find where Al-Jurjani discusses the external aspect (مقيس إلى الخارج) and the internal aspect (مقيس إلى الداخل). Note how each is defined and what it requires.
The external aspect is necessary for the species' existence; the internal aspect is for its perfection.
3
Understand the instruction to apply the same logic
The passage ends with 'قس الثلاثة الباقية' — measure the remaining three types similarly. This sets up the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

المزاجية ( مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق ( بل له عرض) فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين)، إفراط وتفريط . ( إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله ) النوعي ( وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه) فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما . وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. (وله) أي ولكل نوع ( أيضاً مزاج واقع فيما بين ذلك العرض) أي يكون في حاق الوسط فيما بين طرفي المزاج العرضي النوعي ( هو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة في ذلك العرض (به وبه يكون حاله فيما خلق له) من صفاته وآثاره المختصة به ( أجود ) ما يتصور منه ( وذلك اعتداله ) النوعي ( بالنسبة إلى ما يدخل فيه من صنف أو ( شخص ) فالاعتدال النوعي المقيس إلى الخارج يحتاج إلى النوع في وجوده ويكون حاصلاً لكل فرد من أفراده على تفاوت مراتبه. والمقيس إلى الداخل يحتاج إليه النوع في أجودية ،كمالاته ولا يكون حاصلاً إلا لأعدل شخص أعدل صنف من ذلك النوع ولا يكون أيضاً حاصلاً له إلا في أعدل حالاته

( وعليه) أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي (قس الثلاثة الباقية ) فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق وسط هذا العرض، وهو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة فيما بين طرفيه بالصنف إذ به يكون حاله أجود فيما خلق لأجله ولا يكون حاصلاً لأعدل شخص منه في أعدل حالاته سواء كان هذا الصنف أعدل الأصناف أو لا. والاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الخارج هو الذي يحتاج إليه الشخص في بقائه موجوداً سليماً وهو اللائق به مقيساً إلى أمزجة الأشخاص صنفه وله أيضاً عرض هو بعض من العرض الصنفي. وبالنسبة إلى الداخل هو الذي يكون به الشخص على أفضل حالاته والاعتدال العضوي مقيساً إلى الخارج

وقوله : إذا خرج عنه أي إذا خرج ذلك المزاج لا عن ذلك العرض. وقوله : لم يكن ذلك النوع فذلك النوع بالنصب على أنه خبر لم يكن ذلك النوع . وقوله : وهو أي ذلك المزاج بقرينة . قوله : وأليق أمزجته وهو أعني قوله : وأليق أمزجته عطف على قوله : اعتداله النوعي. وقوله : ما يدخل فيه أي في ذلك النوع .

ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه )
The first observatory - the fourth section: the second purpose: on the divisions of temperament. Temperamental [temperament] (a temperament in which the specific form cannot exist except with it, and that temperament is not on a single limit that cannot be exceeded; otherwise all individuals of the same species, like man for example, would be uniform in temperament and what follows from it of character and disposition. Rather it has a range) between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness having two extremes), excess and deficiency. (If it departs from it, that species would not exist; therefore it is its) generic (moderation, and the most suitable of its temperaments with respect to the species external to it). So the temperament occurring in the body of any human is the one suitable to him insofar as he is a human, not the temperament of a horse, donkey, or others. That is because it is appropriate for the effects required from him; if he departs to any of these temperaments, he dies. (And it has) i.e., each species (also a temperament lying within that range) i.e., it is at the very midpoint between the two extremes of the generic accidental temperament (which is the most suitable of the temperaments lying within that range (to it, and with it its state concerning what it was created for) of its attributes and effects specific to it (is the best) that can be conceived of it (and that is its) generic (moderation with respect to what enters into it of sub-species or) individual. So the generic moderation measured externally is necessary for the species in its existence and occurs for every individual of its individuals with variation in its degrees. And measured internally, the species needs it for the bestness of its perfections, and it does not occur except for the most moderate individual of the most moderate sub-species of that species, and also does not occur for him except in his most moderate states. (And based on it) i.e., on what we have mentioned of the state of generic moderation (measure the remaining three). So sub-specific moderation in relation to the external is that which is suitable for a sub-species of a species measured against the temperaments of its other sub-species, and it has a range with two extremes that is less than the generic range, since it is a part of it; and if it departs from it, that sub-species would not exist. And in relation to the internal, it is the temperament lying at the very midpoint of this range, and it is the most suitable of the temperaments lying between its two extremes for the sub-species, since with it its state is best concerning what it was created for, and it does not occur for the most moderate individual from it in his most moderate states, whether this sub-species is the most moderate of sub-species or not. And personal moderation in relation to the external is that which the individual needs for his continued existence sound, and it is the one suitable to him measured against the temperaments of the individuals of his sub-species; and it also has a range that is a part of the sub-specific range. And in relation to the internal, it is that by which the individual is in his best states. And organic moderation measured to the external is that by which the existence of the organ is connected to being sound, and it is the one suitable to it apart from the temperaments of the other organs. And it also has a range, but it is not a part of the personal range; and measured to the internal, it is that which is fitting for the organ so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time.)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moderate temperament (al-iʿtidāl) into four types: generic (al-nawʿī), sub-specific (al-ṣinfī), personal (al-shakhṣī), and organic (al-ʿuḍwī). The author first defines generic temperament as that without which the specific form cannot exist, emphasizing that it is not a single fixed point but a range (ʿaraḍ) between the extremes of heat-cold and moisture-dryness. He distinguishes two aspects for each type: the external (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-khārij) and the internal (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-dākhil). The external aspect of generic moderation is the temperament shared by all individuals of a species in relation to other species; if it departs, the species ceases to exist. The internal aspect is the most balanced temperament within that range, which only the most balanced individual of the most balanced sub-species possesses in his most balanced state. The author then instructs to apply the same logic to the remaining three types. For sub-specific moderation, the external aspect distinguishes one sub-species from others; the internal aspect is the temperament at the exact midpoint of its range, achievable only by the most balanced individual. For personal moderation, the external aspect ensures the individual's survival, while the internal aspect brings him to his best state. For organic moderation, the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect is the optimal temperament for that organ in its best state. The glosses clarify grammatical points and ensure the reader understands the referents of pronouns and the structure of the argument.

Key Terms
مزاج
اعتدال
نوعي
عرض
حاق الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all individuals of a species have the exact same temperament.
Correction: The passage explains that there is a range (عرض) and each individual has its own temperament within that range; the internal aspect is only for the most balanced.

Checkpoint
What is the external aspect of generic moderation?
A) The midpoint of the range
B) The range itself necessary for the species' existence
C) The temperament of the most balanced individual
D) The specific form

Key Takeaway
Generic moderation has an external aspect (the range essential for species existence) and an internal aspect (the midpoint for perfection), and this pattern applies to all four types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that within the human range of temperament, there is a midpoint of perfection that only the most balanced individual achieves. In your own character, where are you settling for 'just within the acceptable range' rather than pressing toward that midpoint? What is one concrete change you will make this week to move closer to that perfect balance?
?
Consider your daily habits: your speech, your reactions, your desires. Are they merely within the bounds of what keeps you human, or are they aligned with the most excellent state you were created for? Which single habit, if brought to its midpoint, would most transform your relationship with Allah?
?
The text says the internal moderation occurs only in the most moderate state of the most moderate individual. Are you content with being 'average' in your character, or do you feel the weight of responsibility to pursue the highest excellence? What will you sacrifice today to step toward that peak?

Sub-specific Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Ṣinfī)
Al-Jurjani explains sub-specific moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand sub-specific moderation, its external aspect (distinguishing one sub-species from others) and internal aspect (the most balanced temperament within that sub-species).

Core Idea
Sub-specific moderation is a narrower range within the generic range, with its own midpoint for perfection.

After establishing generic moderation, Al-Jurjani moves to sub-specific moderation, which applies to a sub-group within a species. He explains that it has its own range, smaller than the generic range, and its own internal aspect that only the most balanced individual of that sub-species can achieve. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sub-specific moderation
Read the Arabic and translation for sub-specific moderation. Notice the comparison to generic moderation: the range is smaller and it is a part of the generic range.
The term 'صنف' means sub-species, like a race or variety.
2
Differentiate external and internal aspects
Identify the external aspect (what makes the sub-species distinct from others) and the internal aspect (the midpoint that gives the best state).
The internal aspect only occurs for the most moderate individual, regardless of whether the sub-species is the most moderate overall.
3
Compare with generic moderation
Reflect on how sub-specific moderation parallels generic moderation but at a lower level. The external aspect ensures existence of the sub-species, the internal aspect its perfection.

Source Text

المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

المزاجية ( مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق ( بل له عرض) فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين)، إفراط وتفريط . ( إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله ) النوعي ( وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه) فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما . وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. ( وعليه) أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي (قس الثلاثة الباقية ) فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق وسط هذا العرض، وهو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة فيما بين طرفيه بالصنف إذ به يكون حاله أجود فيما خلق لأجله ولا يكون حاصلاً لأعدل شخص منه في أعدل حالاته سواء كان هذا الصنف أعدل الأصناف أو لا. والاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الخارج هو الذي يحتاج إليه الشخص في بقائه موجوداً سليماً وهو اللائق به مقيساً إلى أمزجة الأشخاص صنفه وله أيضاً عرض هو بعض من العرض الصنفي. وبالنسبة إلى الداخل هو الذي يكون به الشخص على أفضل حالاته والاعتدال العضوي مقيساً إلى الخارج

ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه )

What happens if a sub-species' temperament leaves its sub-specific range?
A) The species ceases to exist

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage distinguishes between the external range that defines your sub-species and the internal midpoint that is only for the most moderate. Where in your life do you confuse the two—thinking that simply being within your community's range is enough, while neglecting the personal midpoint Allah wants for you?
?
The internal midpoint is only attainable by the most moderate individual in his most moderate state. How does that reality affect the way you handle your own spiritual efforts? Do you accept that perfection in a specific trait may be rare, yet still strive without despair?

Personal Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-Shakhṣī)
Al-Jurjani defines personal moderation and its two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand personal moderation, which ensures an individual's survival (external aspect) and optimal state (internal aspect).

Core Idea
Personal moderation is the individual-specific range within the sub-specific range, with its own midpoint for best health and function.

Al-Jurjani now applies the same scheme to the individual person. Personal moderation has an external aspect that keeps the individual alive and healthy, and an internal aspect that brings him to his best possible state. This lesson focuses on how this type of moderation fits within the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on personal moderation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the range is a part of the sub-specific range.
'شخص' means an individual person.
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
The external aspect is for survival and soundness; the internal aspect is for the best state. Note that the internal aspect is defined as being in 'أفضل حالاته' (his best states).
3
Connect to the overall pattern
Observe how this fits the pattern of the previous two types: each has a range and a midpoint, with external for existence and internal for perfection.

المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

( إذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك النوع فهو اعتداله ) النوعي ( وأليق أمزجته بالنسبة إلى الأنواع الخارجة عنه) فالمزاج الحاصل لبدن من أبدان الناس هو اللائق به من حيث إنه إنسان دون مزاج الفرس والحمار وغيرهما . وذلك لأنه المناسب لآثاره المطلوبة منه حتى إذا خرج إلى شيء من هذه الأمزجة مات. (وله) أي ولكل نوع ( أيضاً مزاج واقع فيما بين ذلك العرض) أي يكون في حاق الوسط فيما بين طرفي المزاج العرضي النوعي ( هو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة في ذلك العرض (به وبه يكون حاله فيما خلق له) من صفاته وآثاره المختصة به ( أجود ) ما يتصور منه ( وذلك اعتداله ) النوعي ( بالنسبة إلى ما يدخل فيه من صنف أو ( شخص ) فالاعتدال النوعي المقيس إلى الخارج يحتاج إلى النوع في وجوده ويكون حاصلاً لكل فرد من أفراده على تفاوت مراتبه. والمقيس إلى الداخل يحتاج إليه النوع في أجودية ،كمالاته ولا يكون حاصلاً إلا لأعدل شخص أعدل صنف من ذلك النوع ولا يكون أيضاً حاصلاً له إلا في أعدل حالاته

( وعليه) أي على ما ذكرنا من حال الاعتدال النوعي (قس الثلاثة الباقية ) فالاعتدال الصنفي بالقياس إلى الخارج هو الذي يكون لائقاً بصنف من نوع مقيساً إلى أمزجة سائر أصنافه وله عرض ذو طرفين هو أقل من العرض النوعي إذ هو بعض منه وإذا خرج عنه لم يكن ذلك الصنف وبالقياس إلى الداخل هو المزاج الواقع في حاق وسط هذا العرض، وهو أليق الأمزجة الواقعة فيما بين طرفيه بالصنف إذ به يكون حاله أجود فيما خلق لأجله ولا يكون حاصلاً لأعدل شخص منه في أعدل حالاته سواء كان هذا الصنف أعدل الأصناف أو لا. والاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الخارج هو الذي يحتاج إليه الشخص في بقائه موجوداً سليماً وهو اللائق به مقيساً إلى أمزجة الأشخاص صنفه وله أيضاً عرض هو بعض من العرض الصنفي. وبالنسبة إلى الداخل هو الذي يكون به الشخص على أفضل حالاته والاعتدال العضوي مقيساً إلى الخارج

وقوله : إذا خرج عنه أي إذا خرج ذلك المزاج لا عن ذلك العرض. وقوله : لم يكن ذلك النوع فذلك النوع بالنصب على أنه خبر لم يكن ذلك النوع . وقوله : وهو أي ذلك المزاج بقرينة . قوله : وأليق أمزجته وهو أعني قوله : وأليق أمزجته عطف على قوله : اعتداله النوعي. وقوله : ما يدخل فيه أي في ذلك النوع .

ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه )
The first observatory - the fourth section: the second purpose: on the divisions of temperament. Temperamental [temperament] (a temperament in which the specific form cannot exist except with it, and that temperament is not on a single limit that cannot be exceeded; otherwise all individuals of the same species, like man for example, would be uniform in temperament and what follows from it of character and disposition. Rather it has a range) between heat and cold and between moisture and dryness having two extremes), excess and deficiency. (If it departs from it, that species would not exist; therefore it is its) generic (moderation, and the most suitable of its temperaments with respect to the species external to it). So the temperament occurring in the body of any human is the one suitable to him insofar as he is a human, not the temperament of a horse, donkey, or others. That is because it is appropriate for the effects required from him; if he departs to any of these temperaments, he dies. (And it has) i.e., each species (also a temperament lying within that range) i.e., it is at the very midpoint between the two extremes of the generic accidental temperament (which is the most suitable of the temperaments lying within that range (to it, and with it its state concerning what it was created for) of its attributes and effects specific to it (is the best) that can be conceived of it (and that is its) generic (moderation with respect to what enters into it of sub-species or) individual. So the generic moderation measured externally is necessary for the species in its existence and occurs for every individual of its individuals with variation in its degrees. And measured internally, the species needs it for the bestness of its perfections, and it does not occur except for the most moderate individual of the most moderate sub-species of that species, and also does not occur for him except in his most moderate states. (And based on it) i.e., on what we have mentioned of the state of generic moderation (measure the remaining three). So sub-specific moderation in relation to the external is that which is suitable for a sub-species of a species measured against the temperaments of its other sub-species, and it has a range with two extremes that is less than the generic range, since it is a part of it; and if it departs from it, that sub-species would not exist. And in relation to the internal, it is the temperament lying at the very midpoint of this range, and it is the most suitable of the temperaments lying between its two extremes for the sub-species, since with it its state is best concerning what it was created for, and it does not occur for the most moderate individual from it in his most moderate states, whether this sub-species is the most moderate of sub-species or not. And personal moderation in relation to the external is that which the individual needs for his continued existence sound, and it is the one suitable to him measured against the temperaments of the individuals of his sub-species; and it also has a range that is a part of the sub-specific range. And in relation to the internal, it is that by which the individual is in his best states. And organic moderation measured to the external is that by which the existence of the organ is connected to being sound, and it is the one suitable to it apart from the temperaments of the other organs. And it also has a range, but it is not a part of the personal range; and measured to the internal, it is that which is fitting for the organ so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time.)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moderate temperament (al-iʿtidāl) into four types: generic (al-nawʿī), sub-specific (al-ṣinfī), personal (al-shakhṣī), and organic (al-ʿuḍwī). The author first defines generic temperament as that without which the specific form cannot exist, emphasizing that it is not a single fixed point but a range (ʿaraḍ) between the extremes of heat-cold and moisture-dryness. He distinguishes two aspects for each type: the external (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-khārij) and the internal (bi-l-qiyās ilā al-dākhil). The external aspect of generic moderation is the temperament shared by all individuals of a species in relation to other species; if it departs, the species ceases to exist. The internal aspect is the most balanced temperament within that range, which only the most balanced individual of the most balanced sub-species possesses in his most balanced state. The author then instructs to apply the same logic to the remaining three types. For sub-specific moderation, the external aspect distinguishes one sub-species from others; the internal aspect is the temperament at the exact midpoint of its range, achievable only by the most balanced individual. For personal moderation, the external aspect ensures the individual's survival, while the internal aspect brings him to his best state. For organic moderation, the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect is the optimal temperament for that organ in its best state. The glosses clarify grammatical points and ensure the reader understands the referents of pronouns and the structure of the argument.

Key Terms
اعتدال
شخصي
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the external aspect of personal moderation ensure?
A) The best state of the individual
B) The existence of the sub-species
C) The individual's continued existence and soundness
D) The perfection of the species

Key Takeaway
Personal moderation has an external aspect for survival and an internal aspect for optimal state, following the same structure as generic and sub-specific moderation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently living only for 'continued existence sound' (external moderation), or are you actively seeking your 'best states' (internal moderation)? What is one concrete change you will make today to pursue your best state in a specific area of your life?
?
Which aspect of your personal temperament do you neglect more—the external (health, duties, survival) or the internal (spiritual excellence, emotional well-being)? How can you intentionally balance both starting this week?
?
Have you been treating moderation as a one-size-fits-all generic rule, ignoring your unique individual needs? Name one area where you need to apply personal moderation tailored to you, and commit to a specific step to do so.

Organic Moderation (al-Iʿtidāl al-ʿUḍwī)
Al-Jurjani defines organic moderation and its two aspects, with glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand organic moderation, which applies to individual organs, with an external aspect for soundness and an internal aspect for optimal function.

Core Idea
Organic moderation is the organ-specific temperament range, not part of the personal range, with its own midpoint for best performance.

The final type of moderation is organic moderation, which applies to individual organs. Al-Jurjani explains that the external aspect keeps the organ healthy relative to other organs, and the internal aspect brings it to its best state. Notably, its range is not a part of the personal range; it is independent. This lesson completes the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on organic moderation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the special feature: the range is not part of the personal range.
The glosses after the main text clarify earlier points, but focus on the organic moderation part.
2
Identify the external and internal aspects
The external aspect is for the organ to remain sound; the internal aspect is for it to be in its best state.
3
Contrast with personal moderation
Understand why the organic range is not a part of the personal range. This is because the organ has its own requirements independent of the individual's overall temperament.

Source Text

المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج

المزاجية ( مزاج لايمكن أن توجد فيه الصورة النوعية إلا معه، وليس ذلك المزاج على حد واحد لا يتعداه وإلا كان جميع أفراد النوع الواحد كالإنسان مثلا متوافقة في المزاج وما يتبعه من الخلق والخُلُق ( بل له عرض) فيما بين الحرارة والبرودة وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة ذو طرفين)، إفراط وتفريط . Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that organic moderation's range is part of the personal range, like the other types.
Correction: The text explicitly says: 'ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي' — it is not a part of the personal range.

Checkpoint
What is unique about organic moderation's range compared to the previous types?
A) It is the same as the personal range
B) It is not a part of the personal range
C) It is larger than the generic range
D) It has no external aspect

Key Takeaway
Organic moderation follows the same pattern but its range is independent of the personal range, ensuring each organ has its own optimal temperament.

intermediate

Simple and Compound Non-Moderate Temperaments with Objection and Response
Al-Jurjani divides non-moderate temperaments and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of non-moderate temperaments into simple and compound, and the author's response to an objection about compound temperaments involving opposing qualities.

Core Idea
Non-moderate temperaments are either simple or compound; compound opposites are impossible.

In this passage, al-Jurjani builds on the concept of moderate temperament by examining its opposite. He classifies non-moderate temperaments into two categories and then addresses a clever objection about compound temperaments involving both heat and cold. His response reveals the crucial role of ratio rather than absolute quantities.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Identify the two main types of non-moderate temperaments: simple (departure in one quality) and compound (departure in two non-opposing qualities). Note the four subtypes of each.
Simple: hot, cold, moist, dry. Compound: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry.
2
Understand the objection
The imaginary objector suggests that if a compound should have ten hot and five cold parts for moderation, finding twelve hot and six cold would make it both hotter and colder than moderate. Grasp why this seems plausible.
The objection uses absolute numbers to challenge the impossibility of opposites.
3
Follow the response
Al-Jurjani shifts the criterion from the number of parts to the intermediate quality and its inclination to one side only. The ratio between hot and cold parts determines moderation, not the absolute numbers.
If the ratio (e.g., hot double cold) is maintained, the temperament is moderate regardless of scale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إما أن يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي ( في كيفية) واحدة (ويسمى البسيط، وهو أربعة: حار وبارد ورطب ويابس (أو يكون خارجاً عنه ( في كيفيتين غير متضادتين ويسمى المركب (وهو) أيضاً :( أربعة حار رطب وحار يابس وبارد رطب وبارد يابس . وأما الحار البارد مثلاً أو الرطب اليابس ) أي خروج المركب عما هو حقه في كيفيتين متضادتين ( أو اجتماع ثلاث أو أربع من تلك الكيفيات (فلا يتصور) إذ يلزم اجتماع المتضادين ) لا يقال : إذا كان يجب أن للمركب عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة فوجد اثنا عشر حارة وستة باردة فهو أحر مما ينبغي وأبرد منه ). وقس على ذلك الأجزاء الرطبة واليابسة والازدواجات العقلية ) لأنا نقول : الاعتبار فيما ليس معتدلاً طبياً إنما هو ( بالكيفية المتوسطة وميلها إلى أحد الطرفين) المتضادين وذلك أي ميلها ( لا يكون إلا إلى طرف واحد ( منهما ( ضرورة) أي إذا مالت الكيفية المتوسطة عما ينبغي فإما أن تميل عنه إلى جانب الحرارة فقط أو إلى جانب البرودة فقط إذ ميلانها إليهما معا محال بديهة وكذا الحال في الرطوبة واليبوسة ( وأما الأجزاء فلا عبرة ) فيما نحن فيه ( بعددها ومقدارها بل مداره على النسبة بينهما . ( وإذا كانت ( الأجزاء ( الحارة ضعف الباردة أي عدد كان فالمزاج واحد ) فإذا فرض أن الاعتدال الطبي مبني على هذه النسبة فالأجزاء الحارة إذا كانت عشرة والباردة خمسة كان المركب معتدلاً ، وكذا إذا كانت الحارة عشرين والباردة عشرة إلى غير الأعداد التي توجد فيها هذه النسبة. وما قيل من أن المعتدل هو الذي وفر

قوله : ( وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إلخ) أي وأما انقسام غير المعتدل الطبي إلى ثمانية أقسام فلانه إلخ . وقوله : لا يقال إما معارضة لقوله : فلا يتصور وإما منع له مع السند . وقوله : إذا كان يجب إلخ . أي إذا كان الشأن أنه يجب للمركب في اعتداله الطبي عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة إلخ . وقوله : فوجد أي فإذا خرج عن الاعتدال الطبي حتى وجد اثني عشر جزءاً حارة وستة باردة إلخ فحينئذ يكون الاثنان من اثني عشر خارجاً عما ينبغي من الحرارة، وكذا الواحد من الستة يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي من البرودة. وقوله : فهو أي ذلك المركب. قوله : والازدواجات العقلية إما باجتماع ثلاثة منها فيكون المركب أحر وأبرد وأرطب مما مثلاً. ينبغي وإما باجتماع أربعة منها فيكون أحر وأبرد وأرطب وأيبس مما ينبغي. وقوله بل مداره أي مدار ما نحن فيه . وقوله : مبني على هذه النسبة أي النسبة الضعيفة. فما ذكر آنفاً من قوله : فهو أحر ما ينبغي وأبرد منه باطل قطعاً بل ذلك واقع على ما ينبغي كما لا يخفى
As for the non-moderate, it is because it is either outside what is appropriate (in one quality) (and it is called simple, and it is four: hot, cold, moist, and dry) or it is outside it (in two non-opposing qualities and it is called compound, and it is also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. As for hot-cold for example or moist-dry) i.e., the compound's departure from what is right in two opposing qualities (or the combination of three or four of those qualities (it is inconceivable) because it would necessitate the combination of opposites). It cannot be said: if it is necessary that the compound has ten hot parts and five cold parts, and one finds twelve hot and six cold, then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it. And measure by that the moist and dry parts and the intellectual double-combinations). For we say: the consideration in what is medicinally non-moderate is only (by the intermediate quality and its inclination to one of the two opposing extremes; and that, i.e., its inclination (cannot be except to one side (of them (necessarily)) i.e., if the intermediate quality inclines from what is appropriate, it either inclines to the side of heat only or to the side of cold only, since its inclination to both together is impossible by intuition. The same is the case with moisture and dryness. (As for the parts, there is no consideration) in what we are discussing (of their number and amount; rather the criterion is the ratio between them. (And if the hot parts are double the cold, whatever the number, the temperament is one.) So if it is assumed that medical moderation is based on this ratio, then if the hot parts are ten and the cold five, the compound is moderate; likewise if the hot are twenty and the cold ten, and so on for numbers in which this ratio exists. And what has been said that the moderate is that which...

His statement: (As for the non-moderate, because it is, etc.) i.e., as for the division of the medicinally non-moderate into eight divisions, because it is, etc. And his statement: 'It cannot be said' is either an objection to his statement 'it is inconceivable' or a denial of it with a proof. And his statement: 'if it is necessary, etc.' i.e., if the matter is that it is necessary for the compound in its medical moderation to have ten hot parts and five cold parts, etc. And his statement: 'and one finds' i.e., if it departs from medical moderation so that twelve hot parts and six cold parts are found, etc., then the two out of twelve are outside what is appropriate in heat, and likewise the one out of six is outside what is appropriate in cold. And his statement: 'then it is' i.e., that compound. His statement: 'and the intellectual double-combinations' either by combining three of them, so the compound is hotter, colder, and moister than it should be, for example; or by combining four of them, so it is hotter, colder, moister, and drier than it should be. And his statement: 'rather the criterion' i.e., the criterion of what we are discussing. And his statement: 'based on this ratio' i.e., the ratio of half. So what was mentioned earlier from his statement: 'then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it' is absolutely false; rather that is occurring as it should be, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses non-moderate temperaments and addresses an objection. The author first classifies non-moderate (ghayr al-muʿtadil) temperaments into two kinds: simple (basīṭ) and compound (murakkab). Simple non-moderate temperaments involve departure in one quality only, and they are four: hot, cold, moist, dry. Compound non-moderate temperaments involve departure in two non-opposing qualities, also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. The author then states that compound temperaments involving opposing qualities (like hot-cold or moist-dry) or three or four qualities are inconceivable because they would require the combination of opposites. This is the main argument. Then an objection is raised: if a compound should have, for example, ten hot and five cold parts for moderation, but one finds twelve hot and six cold, it seems to be both hotter and colder than it should be. The author responds by shifting the consideration from the absolute number of parts to the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) and its inclination to one side. He argues that the intermediate quality, when it departs from what is appropriate, can only incline to one side (e.g., heat or cold), not both simultaneously, because that would be impossible. Therefore, the apparent combination of excess in both heat and cold is not a real combination; it is a matter of the ratio between the parts. As long as the ratio (e.g., hot parts double the cold) is maintained, the temperament remains moderate regardless of absolute numbers. Thus, the objection is invalid. The gloss further clarifies that the division of non-moderate is into eight types (four simple and four compound), and that the objection is either a direct contradiction or a denial with evidence, and explains the objection's premise and conclusion, ultimately affirming that the earlier claim of being both hotter and colder is false because the ratio-based determination shows it is actually as it should be.

Key Terms

بسيط
مركب
كيفية
متضاد
كيفية متوسطة
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a compound temperament with an excess in both heat and cold is possible and that this would contradict the impossibility of opposites.
Correction: The author clarifies that the intermediate quality can only incline to one side, and the appearance of both excesses is actually a matter of the ratio; when the ratio remains the same, the temperament is still moderate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of compound non-moderate temperament are there?
A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Non-moderate temperaments are classified as simple or compound, and the criterion for moderation is the ratio between parts, not absolute numbers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own character, do you ever try to hold two opposing attitudes at the same time—like being both harsh and gentle without a clear principle? How does this lesson's truth that the intermediate quality can only incline to one side force you to choose a single, consistent orientation in your dealings? What will you do today to align your heart to one clear quality rather than wavering?
?
When you evaluate your spiritual state, do you count your good deeds (the 'hot parts') and bad deeds (the 'cold parts') as absolute numbers, or do you consider the ratio—the overall direction of your heart? If you had ten good deeds and five bad ones, would you feel moderate? What if the ratio is the same but the numbers are larger? Does your focus on absolute quantities reveal an attachment to appearances rather than the inner proportion that Allah sees? What change in your self-assessment does this demand?
?
Think of a relationship or situation where you felt pulled between two 'compounds'—wanting to be both strict and lenient, or both involved and detached. This lesson says a compound of opposites is impossible. Are you trying to hold an impossible compound? What would it look like to choose a simple or non-opposing compound, accepting the tension of a single inclination? Will you today identify one area of contradiction in your life and resolve to incline to one side sincerely for Allah's sake?
