Classification of Temperaments: Moderate, Non-Moderate, Simple and Compound
Defines moderate and non-moderate temperaments, divides non-moderate into eight types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of temperaments into moderate and non-moderate, and the eight types of non-moderate temperaments according to the simple and compound divisions.

Core Idea
Non-moderate temperaments are exactly eight: four simple and four compound.

In this passage, the author defines the moderate temperament as that which ensures the healthy function of an organ. He then turns to non-moderate temperaments, explaining that they can deviate in one quality (simple) or two non-opposite qualities (compound). This yields exactly eight distinct types. Understanding this classification is fundamental to the study of humoral physiology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
Pay attention to the terms 'moderate' (muʿtadil) and 'non-moderate' (ghayr muʿtadil).
2
Understand the definition of moderate temperament
The moderate temperament is defined as what ensures an organ's healthy existence and is uniquely suited to it. It has a quality but not as part of the personal quality.
This sets the baseline for deviations.
3
Identify the two categories of non-moderate
Non-moderate is either simple (deviation in one quality) or compound (deviation in two non-opposite qualities). Simple yields four types (hot, cold, moist, dry); compound yields another four (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry).
Total eight types.
4
Note the impossibility of opposite combinations
The passage argues that combinations like hot-cold or moist-dry are impossible because they would require opposites to coexist. Similarly, three or four qualities together are impossible.
This limits non-moderate to exactly eight types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الرابع : المقصد الثاني : في أقسام المزاج. ما يتعلق به وجود العضو سالماً، وهو اللائق به دون أمزجة سائر الأعضاء. وله أيضاً عرض إلا أنه ليس بعضاً من العرض الشخصي ومقيسا إلى الداخل. وهو الذي ينبغي للعضو حتى يكون على أحس أحواله وأكمل زمانه. وأما غير المعتدل فلأنه إما أن يكون خارجاً عما ينبغي في كيفية واحدة ويسمى البسيط، وهو أربعة: حار وبارد ورطب ويابس. أو يكون خارجاً عنه في كيفيتين غير متضادتين ويسمى المركب، وهو أيضاً أربعة: حار رطب وحار يابس وبارد رطب وبارد يابس. وأما الحار البارد مثلاً أو الرطب اليابس أي خروج المركب عما هو حقه في كيفيتين متضادتين أو اجتماع ثلاث أو أربع من تلك الكيفيات فلا يتصور إذ يلزم اجتماع المتضادين.
First Observation - Fourth Section: Second Objective: On the Types of Temperament. What pertains to the existence of a member healthy, and it is the one suitable for it to the exclusion of the temperaments of the other members. And it also has a quality but it is not a part of the personal quality and is measured to the interior. And it is what is proper for the member so that it may be in its best states and its most complete time. As for the non-moderate, then it is either outside what is proper in one quality and it is called the simple, and it is four: hot, cold, moist, dry. Or it is outside it in two non-opposite qualities and it is called the compound, and it is also four: hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry. As for hot-cold for example or moist-dry, i.e. the departure of the compound from what is its right in two opposite qualities, or the combination of three or four of those qualities, then it is inconceivable, because the conjunction of opposites would be necessary.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies temperaments. It begins by defining the moderate temperament (al-muʿtadil) as that which ensures the healthy existence of an organ and is uniquely suited to it; it also has a quality but not one that is part of the personal quality. Then it describes the non-moderate (ghayr al-muʿtadil) and divides it into two categories: simple (bāsiṭ), which is deviation in a single quality, yielding four types (hot, cold, moist, dry); and compound (murakkab), which is deviation in two non-opposite qualities, yielding another four types (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry). It then argues that deviation in two opposite qualities (e.g., hot-cold) or in three or four qualities is impossible because it would require the coexistence of opposites. Thus the non-moderate temperaments are limited to exactly eight types.

Misconception: Some think that non-moderate temperaments can include more than eight types by combining opposite qualities (e.g., hot-cold).
Correction: The passage argues that such combinations are impossible because they would require opposites to coexist. Thus, only eight types exist.

Key Takeaway
Non-moderate temperaments are exactly eight: four simple (hot, cold, moist, dry) and four compound (hot-moist, hot-dry, cold-moist, cold-dry); combinations of opposites are impossible.

?
Opposite qualities cannot coexist—but how often do you try to hold contradictory values or desires in your heart? What is one internal conflict you need to resolve by choosing one path over the other to achieve spiritual temperance?

Refuting Dual Deviation in Temperament
Refutes the objection that a temperament can deviate in two opposite qualities simultaneously

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a compound's temperament cannot deviate in two opposite qualities simultaneously, and how the ratio of parts preserves moderation.

Core Idea
Temperament is defined by the intermediate quality's inclination, not by part counts.

This passage addresses a potential objection against the theory of temperaments. The objection claims that a compound could be both hotter and colder than it should be if the number of hot and cold parts changes proportionally. The author explains why this is impossible and clarifies the true criterion for medical moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the scenario: a compound with 10 hot and 5 cold parts (moderate) is found with 12 hot and 6 cold parts. The objection says it becomes both hotter and colder than it should be.
Notice the objection assumes that absolute numbers determine moderation.
2
Identify the first response
The author states that the consideration is based on the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) and its inclination. It can only incline to one extreme, not both simultaneously.
This is a self-evident principle.
3
Understand the second response
The author argues that the ratio of hot to cold parts, not their absolute numbers, determines temperament. As long as the ratio is constant (e.g., double), the temperament remains the same.
Thus 12:6 is the same ratio as 10:5.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The objection is false because it ignores both the principle of single inclination and the role of ratio. The compound in the example is actually moderate, not hotter and colder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : إذا كان يجب أن للمركب عشرة أجزاء حارة وخمسة باردة فوجد اثنا عشر حارة وستة باردة فهو أحر مما ينبغي وأبرد منه. وقس على ذلك الأجزاء الرطبة واليابسة والازدواجات العقلية. لأنا نقول : الاعتبار فيما ليس معتدلاً طبياً إنما هو بالكيفية المتوسطة وميلها إلى أحد الطرفين المتضادين وذلك أي ميلها لا يكون إلا إلى طرف واحد منهما ضرورة. أي إذا مالت الكيفية المتوسطة عما ينبغي فإما أن تميل عنه إلى جانب الحرارة فقط أو إلى جانب البرودة فقط إذ ميلانها إليهما معا محال بديهة وكذا الحال في الرطوبة واليبوسة. وأما الأجزاء فلا عبرة فيما نحن فيه بعددها ومقدارها بل مداره على النسبة بينهما. وإذا كانت الأجزاء الحارة ضعف الباردة أي عدد كان فالمزاج واحد. فإذا فرض أن الاعتدال الطبي مبني على هذه النسبة فالأجزاء الحارة إذا كانت عشرة والباردة خمسة كان المركب معتدلاً، وكذا إذا كانت الحارة عشرين والباردة عشرة إلى غير الأعداد التي توجد فيها هذه النسبة. فما ذكر آنفاً من قوله : فهو أحر ما ينبغي وأبرد منه باطل قطعاً بل ذلك واقع على ما ينبغي كما لا يخفى.
It cannot be said: If it is necessary that for the compound there are ten hot parts and five cold parts, then it is found to have twelve hot and six cold, then it is hotter than it should be and colder than it. And analogize to that the moist and dry parts and the intellectual combinations. For we say: The consideration in what is not medically moderate is only by the intermediate quality and its inclination to one of the two opposite extremes, and that, i.e. its inclination, can only be to one extreme necessarily. That is, if the intermediate quality deviates from what it should be, then it either deviates only toward the side of heat or only toward the side of cold, because its inclination to both is impossible by self-evidence, and similarly for moisture and dryness. As for the parts, there is no consideration in what we are discussing of their number and quantity, rather the basis is on the ratio between them. And if the hot parts are twice the cold parts, whatever number, then the temperament is one. So if it is assumed that medical moderation is built on this ratio, then if the hot parts are ten and the cold are five, the compound is moderate, and likewise if the hot are twenty and the cold are ten, and so on for other numbers where this ratio exists. So what was mentioned previously of his saying: 'It is hotter than it should be and colder than it' is absolutely false; rather that occurs as it should, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that if a compound requires 10 hot and 5 cold parts but is found with 12 hot and 6 cold, it seems both hotter and colder than it should be, implying deviation in two opposite qualities. The answer is twofold. First, the criterion for being non-moderate is the deviation of the intermediate quality (al-kayfiyya al-mutawassiṭa) toward one extreme only; it cannot deviate toward both opposites simultaneously, as that is impossible by self-evidence. Second, regarding the parts, the number of hot or cold parts does not matter; what matters is the ratio between them. As long as the ratio of hot to cold is constant (e.g., double), the temperament remains the same. Therefore, if the ratio is maintained, the compound is actually moderate, not hotter and colder. The claim that it is both hotter and colder is therefore false.

Key Terms
كيفية متوسطة (kayfiyya mutawassiṭa)
نسبة (nisba)
اعتدال طبي (iʿtidāl ṭibbī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that changing the absolute number of hot or cold parts always changes the temperament.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the ratio between hot and cold parts determines temperament, not the absolute numbers.

Checkpoint
What is the primary determinant of whether a compound is medically moderate according to the answer?
A) The total number of hot parts
B) The total number of cold parts
C) The ratio of hot to cold parts
D) The deviation of the intermediate quality

Key Takeaway
A compound's temperament is determined by the intermediate quality's inclination toward one extreme, not by absolute part counts, and the same ratio of parts preserves moderation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pulled in two opposite directions in your spiritual life—like being both too strict and too lenient—do you realize that you can only truly incline to one extreme? Which one will you choose to correct today?
?
The author shows that the same ratio can appear with different numbers. In your character, are you focusing on the 'numbers' (external actions) while ignoring the 'ratio' (intention and balance)? What one adjustment can you make to restore the right proportion?
?
You constantly judge yourself for having too much of one trait and too little of another, but perhaps you are holding the same ratio as before. Can you identify a specific area where you thought you were failing but are actually consistent? How will that change your self-talk?

The Most Balanced Human Temperament: Ibn Sina vs. al-Razi
Presents the consensus on human temperament and the differing views of Ibn Sina and al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that humans have the most balanced temperament and the disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Razi on which subgroup is most balanced.

Core Idea
The most balanced temperament belongs to humans, but scholars differ on which group is ideal.

This note addresses a subtle point in natural philosophy: which beings have the most balanced temperament? All agree it is the human species due to the nobility of the soul and the lack of stinginess in the outpouring from the First Principle. However, Ibn Sina and al-Razi diverge on which human group is most balanced, offering arguments based on geography and climate. The passage presents their reasoning concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Identify the unanimous agreement that humans are the most balanced compound and understand the reasoning: the human soul's nobility and the receptivity of the temperament.
The phrase 'لا بخل في إفاضة المبدأ' emphasizes that the outpouring is not stingy but depends on preparedness.
2
Analyze Ibn Sina's argument
Study the reasons Ibn Sina gives for considering equator dwellers the most balanced: equal day and night, the sun's zenith passage, and moderate temperatures.
He adds a caveat about avoiding opposing earthly causes like mountains and seas.
3
Analyze al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi counters by pointing to the fourth clime's inhabitants, citing empirical observations: fairer color, taller stature, better intellect, and nobler character.
This shows a shift from purely theoretical reasoning to observable qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : اتفقوا على أن أعدل أنواع المركبات أي أقربها بحسب المزاج إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي نوع الإنسان لأن النفس الإنسانية أشرف وأكمل ولا بخل في إفاضة المبدأ بل هي بحسب استعدادات القوابل فاستعداد الإنسان بحسب مزاجه أشد وأقوى فيكون إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي أقرب. واختلفوا في أعدل الأصناف من نوع الإنسان فقال ابن سينا : أعدل أصنافه سكان خط الاستواء لتشابه أحوالهم في الحر والبرد وذلك لتساوي ليلهم ونهارهم أبداً فتكسر كل واحدة من هاتين الكيفيتين الحادثتين منهما بالأخرى. ولأن الشمس تلبث على سمت رؤوسهم كثيراً بل تمر به حال اجتيازها عن إحدى الجهتين إلى الأخرى وهناك حركتها في الميل عن المعدل أسرع ما يكون فلا تشتد حرارة صيفهم ولا تبعد الشمس عن سمت رؤوسهم إلا بمقدار الميل الكلي فلا يكون بردهم أيضاً شديداً، فيكون مزاجهم أقرب إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي إذا لم تعرض هناك أسباب أرضية مضادة كالجبال والبحار. وقال الإمام الرازي : هم سكان الإقليم الرابع لأنا نرى أهله أحسن ألواناً وأطل قدوداً وأجود أذهاناً وأكرم أخلاقاً.
A note: They agreed that the most balanced of the types of compounds, i.e., the closest in terms of temperament to true equilibrium, is the human species, because the human soul is nobler and more perfect and there is no stinginess in the outpouring of the First Principle, rather it is according to the preparedness of the recipients, so the preparedness of the human due to his temperament is stronger and more powerful, so it is closer to true equilibrium. And they differed regarding the most balanced of the varieties of the human species. Ibn Sina said: The most balanced of its varieties are the inhabitants of the equator because their conditions in heat and cold are similar, due to the equality of their night and day always, so each of these two qualities occurring from them breaks the other. And because the sun lingers at their zenith a lot, rather it passes over it during its crossing from one side to the other, and there its motion in inclination from the celestial equator is as fast as it can be, so the heat of their summer does not become intense, and the sun is not distant from their zenith except by the total declination, so their cold also is not intense, so their temperament is closer to true equilibrium if there do not occur there opposing earthly causes like mountains and seas. And Imam al-Razi said: They are the inhabitants of the fourth clime because we see their people as fairer in color, taller in stature, better in intellect, and nobler in character.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) that presents a consensus and a disagreement. All agree that the most balanced of all compound beings, the closest to true equilibrium (al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī), is the human species. The reason is that the human soul is the most noble and perfect, and the outpouring from the First Principle (al-mabdaʾ) is without stinginess; it depends on the receptivity of the recipients. Human receptivity, due to its temperament, is strongest, thus closest to true equilibrium. Then there is disagreement on which specific variety of humans is most balanced. Ibn Sina holds that equator dwellers are most balanced because their day and night are always equal, moderating heat and cold. Moreover, the sun passes over their zenith and its motion in declination is fastest, so neither summer heat nor winter cold is extreme, making their temperament closest to balance, barring opposing earthly factors. Al-Razi argues for inhabitants of the fourth clime (including Khurasan) because they are fairer, taller, more intelligent, and more noble in character.

Key Terms
أعدل (aʿdal)
اعتدال حقيقي (iʿtidāl ḥaqīqī)
نوع الإنسان (nawʿ al-insān)
خط الاستواء (khaṭṭ al-istiwāʾ)
إقليم رابع (iqlim rābiʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the most balanced human type is determined solely by physical appearance, but the passage shows it is based on physiological and environmental reasoning.
Correction: Both Ibn Sina and al-Razi use physical and environmental arguments, not just appearance. Ibn Sina uses climatic theory, al-Razi uses empirical observations including but not limited to appearance.

Checkpoint
Who argued that the inhabitants of the equator have the most balanced temperament?
A) al-Razi
B) Ibn Sina
C) al-Katibi
D) The author of the work

Key Takeaway
Medieval scholars used climatic and empirical reasoning to debate which human group has the most balanced temperament, with Ibn Sina favoring the equator and al-Razi favoring the fourth clime.

?

?
The passage says that the outpouring of the First Principle depends on the preparedness of the recipient. Are you actively preparing your soul to receive divine gifts, or are you waiting passively? What will you do this week to increase your spiritual preparedness?

The Most Balanced Persons and Organs
Defining the most balanced person and organ, with a critique of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the most balanced person and organ are determined in temperament theory, and why the claim about the skin is not certain.

Core Idea
The most balanced organ is the skin, but the proof is persuasive, not certain.

In this passage, the author builds on earlier discussions of balanced types and kinds to specify which individual and which organ are considered most balanced. However, he also warns that the basis for identifying the most balanced organ lacks conclusive evidence. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from type to person to organ.
The passage uses a comparative method (by analogy with the most balanced kind).
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition of the most balanced person and the most balanced organ. Note how each is derived.
The most balanced person is defined in relation to the most balanced kind; the organ is specified as skin, especially the index fingertip.
3
Understand the critique
The author ends with a critique: the claim about the skin is not certain and lacks conclusive proof. Ask why this matters for the theory.
This shows the author's critical approach even to established views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما ذكر أعدل الأنواع وأعدل الأصناف أشار إلى أعدل الأشخاص وأعدل الأعضاء بقوله : ( وتعرف ) أنت على قياس أعدل الأصناف ( إن أعدل الأشخاص ) النوعية ( أعدل شخص من أعدل صنف و أما ) ( أعدل الأعضاء ) فهو ( عندهم الجلد سيما ) الجلد الذي ( للأنملة سيما) الذي للسبابة ولذلك حكم جلد أنملة السبابة أو جلد الأنامل. (طبعاً في الفرق بين الملموسات والحاكم ينبغي أن يكون متساوي الميل إلى الطرفين) ليحكم بالعدل. ) ولا يخفى) على الفطن ( أن شيئا من ذلك الذي ذكروه من حال الجلد (غير يقيني) إذ لا دلالة قاطعة عليه وحديث التحكيم إقناعي.
And when he mentioned the most balanced of types and the most balanced of kinds, he pointed to the most balanced of persons and the most balanced of organs by saying: 'And you know, by analogy with the most balanced of kinds, that the most balanced of persons (specific) is the most balanced person from the most balanced kind. As for the most balanced of organs, it is, according to them, the skin, especially the skin of the fingertip, especially that of the index finger.' Therefore they judged the skin of the fingertip of the index finger or the skin of the fingertips. 'Naturally, in distinguishing between tangible things, the judge should be equally inclined to both extremes in order to judge justly.' And it is not hidden from the intelligent that none of what they mentioned about the state of the skin is certain, for there is no conclusive evidence for it, and the story of arbitration is persuasive.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how to identify the most balanced individual and organ within the context of temperament theory. The author begins by stating that after discussing the most balanced types and kinds, the text turns to the most balanced persons and organs. The most balanced person is defined as the most balanced individual belonging to the most balanced kind. For the most balanced organ, it is specified as the skin, particularly the skin of the fingertip, and more particularly the index finger. This is because the skin is the organ of touch, and to accurately judge differences in tangible qualities, the judge (i.e., the skin) should be equally balanced between extremes. However, the author then critiques this view, stating that this is not certain and lacks conclusive proof; it is merely persuasive.

Correction: The passage defines the most balanced person as the most balanced person from the most balanced kind, not an average across all individuals.

Classification of Imbalanced Temperaments
The author classifies the eight non-moderate temperaments into material/simple and congenital/accidental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eight imbalanced temperaments are classified into material/simple by cause and congenital/accidental by duration.

Core Idea
Imbalanced temperaments arise either from humors or external factors, and can be innate or acquired.

This passage from the text presents a systematic classification of the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. Before we explore the details of each temperament, we must first grasp the author's twofold division: by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental). This classification is essential for understanding how imbalances are analyzed in Islamic medicine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it opens with a classification statement and provides examples for each category.
The passage is concise; read it twice to absorb the structure.
2
Identify the two binary divisions
Look for the two ways each of the eight temperaments is categorized: by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental). Note the examples: phlegm (coldness) and yellow bile (heat) for material; snow and sun for simple.
Material imbalances involve a dominant humor; simple imbalances involve external factors.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters. It shows that not all imbalances are caused by humors; some are due to environment or innate constitution. This affects diagnosis and treatment.
The commentary will clarify these implications further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن كلاً من الأمزجة الثمانية الخارجة عن الاعتدال قد يكون ماديا بأن يغلب على البدن خلط يغلب عليه كيفيه فيخرجه عن الاعتدال الذي هو حقه إلى تلك الكيفية كأن يغلب مثلا عليه البلغم فيخرجه إلى البرودة أو الصفراء فتخرجه إلى الحرارة، وقد يكون ساذجاً بأن يخرج عن الاعتدال لا بمجاورة خلط نافذ فيه بل بأسباب خارجية أوجبت ذلك كالمبرد بالثلج والمسخن بالشمس وقد يكون كل واحد منها جبلياً خلق البدن عليه وعرضياً عرض له بعد اعتداله في جبلته .
And know that each of the eight temperaments outside moderation may be material, by a humor dominating the body that overpowers its quality and takes it out of the moderation that is its due to that quality, e.g., phlegm dominates it and takes it to coldness, or yellow bile takes it to heat; and it may be simple, by leaving moderation not due to the presence of a penetrating humor but due to external causes that necessitate that, like cooling with snow or heating with the sun; and each of them may be congenital, the body being created upon it, or accidental, occurring after its moderation in its constitution.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. The author explains that each of these eight can be categorized in two ways: by cause and by duration. First, by cause: a temperament may be 'material' (māddī), meaning an imbalance arises from a dominant humor (such as phlegm causing coldness or yellow bile causing heat); or it may be 'simple' (sādhdhaj), meaning the imbalance is not due to an internal humor but to external factors like snow or sun. Second, by duration: each type may be 'congenital' (jiblī), i.e., the body was created with that imbalance, or 'accidental' ('araḍī), i.e., it occurs after the body's original balanced constitution. This classification clarifies that not all imbalances are caused by humors, and some are due to environment or innate disposition.

Key Terms
الأمزجة الثمانية
الاعتدال
مادي
ساذج
جبلي
عرضي
البلغم
الصفراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All imbalances are caused by humoral excess.
Correction: Imbalances can also be caused by external factors like snow or sun, not necessarily by a dominant humor.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by a 'simple' (sādhaj) temperament outside moderation?
A) An imbalance caused by a dominant humor
B) An imbalance that is congenital
C) An imbalance due to external causes like snow or sun, not a humor
D) An imbalance that is temporary

Key Takeaway
The eight imbalanced temperaments are classified by cause (material vs. simple) and by duration (congenital vs. accidental), showing that not all imbalances are due to humors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own state: do you tend to blame your imbalances on internal moods (material) or external circumstances (simple)? Which of these is harder to change? What does that reveal about your reliance on your own control versus submission to Allah's decree?
?
This lesson shows that some imbalances are congenital—you were created with them. Is there an aspect of your temperament you have always considered a flaw? How does knowing it is part of your original constitution change how you respond to it? Will you accept it as a test or strive to moderate it?
?
The ideal is moderation (iʿtidāl). Honestly, are you comfortable with your own imbalances? Do you secretly prefer them because they feel familiar? What would it cost you to genuinely seek balance in your spiritual and physical life? Make a concrete plan to address one imbalance this week.

The Most Balanced Individual and the Organ of Touch
Argues about the most balanced person and organ, then states uncertainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the skin, particularly of the fingertip and index finger, is considered the most balanced organ for tactile judgment, and why this claim is not certain.

Core Idea
The skin of the fingertip is the most balanced organ, but its judgment is not certain.

This passage discusses the concept of the most balanced individual and organ. After critiquing an earlier inference about heat and location, the author argues that the most balanced individual comes from the most balanced kind, and the most balanced organ is the skin of the fingertip, especially the index finger, because it should be equally inclined to judge touch. However, he concludes that this is not certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses parentheses to indicate embedded commentary.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The passage first critiques a previous inference about heat, then introduces the possibility of a more balanced temperament, then analogizes to individuals and organs. Finally, it identifies the skin of the fingertip as the most balanced organ and concludes with uncertainty.
3
Reflect on the uncertainty
The author explicitly states that the claim about the skin is not certain and the argument is persuasive, not decisive. This is a key point: even theoretical reasoning can be uncertain in Islamic theology.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and do the activity to solidify your grasp of the passage.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the most balanced human individual and the most balanced organ for tactile judgment. From this, the author moves to the concept of the most balanced individual and organ: the most balanced individual comes from the most balanced kind, and the most balanced organ is the skin, specifically of the finger tip, especially the index finger. The skin is considered the judge of touch because it should be equally inclined to both extremes to judge justly. However, the author concludes that all this is not certain; there is no decisive proof, and the argument is merely persuasive. The passage ends by noting that the eight temperaments outside balance can be either material (due to a dominant humor) or simple (due to external causes), and each can be congenital or accidental.

The Eight Temperaments: Material and Simple, Congenital and Accidental
Classifies the eight temperaments into material/simple and congenital/accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eight temperaments outside balance are categorized as material or simple, and as congenital or accidental.

Core Idea
Every unbalanced temperament is either material (from humors) or simple (from externals), and either congenital or accidental.

This passage classifies the eight temperaments that fall outside the balanced state. It explains that each temperament can be material, caused by a dominant humor, or simple, caused by external factors. Additionally, each can be congenital, meaning the body was created with it, or accidental, occurring after balance.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the two axes
Read the passage and identify the two classification axes: material vs simple (based on the cause), and congenital vs accidental (based on the origin).
The passage first explains material vs simple, then notes each can be congenital or accidental.
2
Give examples from daily life
Think of examples for each category, such as cold due to phlegm (material) vs cold due to ice (simple), and a naturally cold person (congenital) vs someone who became cold later (accidental).
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this classification matter? It helps in understanding how temperaments can be altered and what causes them.

(And if that) which we mentioned) is possible, the inference is invalid) because it is possible that the heat in summer of those lands is due to some of these causes, not merely because of the sun's proximity to their zenith, so it does not follow that the winter of the equator is like it in heat if it is free from the mentioned causes. (Then there is no rational obstacle) that it may cause) in some places that are not on the equator nor in the fourth clime) some of these matters) i.e. in some earthly positions (either singly or combined what is (i.e. a specific temperament that is more balanced than the two) i.e. the temperaments of the inhabitants of the equator and the fourth clime. And when he mentioned the most balanced types and the most balanced kinds, he pointed to the most balanced individuals and the most balanced organs by saying: (And you know) by analogy with the most balanced kinds (that the most balanced individuals) of the species (is the most balanced person from the most balanced kind, and as for) (the most balanced organs) it is (according to them the skin, especially) the skin (of the fingertip, especially) that of the index finger. Therefore they judged the skin of the index finger or the skin of the fingertips. (Naturally, in distinguishing between tangible things, the judge should be equally inclined to both extremes) to judge justly. ) And it is not hidden) from the intelligent (that any of that which they mentioned about the state of the skin (is not certain) because there is no decisive proof for it, and the arbitration argument is persuasive. (And know that each of) the eight temperaments) outside of balance (may be material) by a humor dominating the body and overcoming it with its quality, removing it from the balance which is its right to that quality, such as phlegm dominating it and removing it to cold, or yellow bile removing it to heat. And it may be simple (by being removed from balance not by a humor permeating it but by external causes that necessitate that, like cooling with ice and heating with the sun) and each of them may be (congenital) the body was created upon it) and accidental) occurring after its congenital balance.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the most balanced human individual and the most balanced organ for tactile judgment. It begins by considering the possibility that heat in summer may be due to factors other than direct solar zenith, which undermines a previous inference about the equator's winter. It then argues that there is no rational obstacle to the existence of a place with a temperament more balanced than both the equator and the fourth clime. From this, the author moves to the concept of the most balanced individual and organ: the most balanced individual comes from the most balanced kind, and the most balanced organ is the skin, specifically of the finger tip, especially the index finger. The skin is considered the judge of touch because it should be equally inclined to both extremes to judge justly. However, the author concludes that all this is not certain; there is no decisive proof, and the argument is merely persuasive. The passage ends by noting that the eight temperaments outside balance can be either material (due to a dominant humor) or simple (due to external causes), and each can be congenital or accidental.

Key Terms
الأمزجة الثمانية
مادي
ساذج
جبلي
عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All temperaments are caused by internal humors.
Correction: The passage clearly states that some are simple, caused by external factors.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a material temperament from a simple temperament?
A) Material is congenital, simple is accidental
B) Material is caused by humors, simple is caused by external factors
C) Material is hot, simple is cold
D) Material is always temporary, simple is permanent

Key Takeaway
The eight imbalances are classified along two axes: cause (material vs simple) and origin (congenital vs accidental).

Classification of Minerals: Malleable and Non-Malleable
Defines malleable and non-malleable minerals and lists the seven metals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers classified minerals into malleable and non-malleable, and what the seven malleable bodies are.

Core Idea
Minerals are either malleable (can be hammered) or not; the seven metals are the malleable ones.

Before diving into the formation of metals, Sanusi establishes a basic classification: minerals are either malleable (able to be hammered without breaking) or non-malleable. The first category consists of seven specific bodies (metals) which are listed by name. This lesson introduces that foundational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the definitions.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice that minerals are first divided into malleable and non-malleable. What is the criterion?
Malleable means can be hammered without breaking, but softening and expanding.
3
Memorize the seven metals
The passage lists seven metals: gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini. Remember them.
Asrub is black lead; Kharsini resembles copper or raw gold.

الرديء (غير جيدي المخالطة فالرصاص وإن كانا معاً رديئين فإن قوي التركيب بينهما والالتئام. فهو الحديد (وإلا أي وإن لم يقو التركيب بينهما مع رداءتهما ) فهو الأسرب ) . ويسمى الرصاص الأسود ( وأنت خبير بأن [The second chapter on what has no soul among the composite things] with temperament (and they are called minerals, and they are divided into two divisions: malleable) i.e. capable of being struck by a hammer such that they do not break or scatter but soften and extend into their depth and expand. (and non-malleable) i.e. not accepting that. ( [The first division: The seven bodies] the malleable, and they are the seven bodies) gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini (formed from the mixture of mercury and sulfur, which are formed from vapors and fumes. For mercury is a very pure vapor or water, mixed with fine sulfurous smokiness in a strong mixture such that no surface separates from it without being covered by something of that dryness; therefore it does not stick to the hand and is not confined to a shape of what contains it. Its example is drops of water falling on extremely fine dust, for a dusty surface surrounds the drop, enclosing the water like a cover for it, such that the drop remains in its shape on the face of the dust. And if two drops meet, perhaps it tears and the two waters become in one cover. And the whiteness of mercury is due to the purity of the watery and the whiteness of the earthy, and the mixing of the airy and the sulfur and the smokiness that ferments its vapor intensely with heat until an oiliness occurs in it, then it congeals with cold. And these seven differ (by their mixture according to a temperament prepared for that difference. For if both are pure and cooking is complete) i.e. the cooking of the mercury with the sulfur (and if the sulfur) with its purity and clarity (is white, then the product is silver; and if it is red and has a coloring power) fine, non-burning (then it) i.e. the product (is gold; and if both are pure and in the red sulfur there is coloring power, but (the cold congeals it before complete cooking, then it is Kharsini, as if it were raw gold, i.e. unripe, not reaching full maturity). And if the mercury is pure and the sulfur is inferior and burning, then it is copper; and if both, i.e. the pure mercury and the inferior sulfur, (are not well mixed, then it is lead; and if both are together inferior and the composition and cohesion between them is strong, then it is iron; and otherwise i.e. if the composition between them is not strong along with their inferiority, then it is Asrub). And it is called black lead. (And you are aware that the division is not exhaustive) because it is possible that both are pure with white sulfur and cold congeals it before full maturity, and that the sulfur is pure and the mercury is inferior or vice versa, and the sulfur is not burning, and other such rational possibilities. (And that the formation) i.e. the formation of the bodies from them in this way has no path to certainty, and only conjecture and guesswork are hoped for) with weak indicators like their saying: It indicates that mercury is the element of the malleable bodies that when melted they become like mercury; as for lead it is obvious. And as for others, because when melted it is red mercury; and it also indicates that mercury adheres to these bodies, and that it can be congealed by the smell of sulfur until it becomes like lead, for the alchemists congeal. His saying: (As if it is raw gold, it is said it is a substance resembling copper from which mirrors are made, called Chinese iron. And his saying: The cold congeals it before complete cooking, then it is raw silver struck by coagulating cold. And his saying: Those obsessed with alchemy. 'Al-Haws' with movement is a type of madness, and 'al-Haws' with sukun is pounding, as in al-Sihah. And his saying: The 'spirits' are like mercury, and this is from the terminology of the alchemists. And his saying: 'And in it' i.e. in the Eastern Investigations. And his saying: 'Al-Razana' i.e. heaviness.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies minerals, focusing on the seven malleable bodies. It begins by stating that minerals are divided into malleable (those that can be hammered without breaking) and non-malleable. The first division comprises the seven metals: gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini (possibly zinc or brass). These are formed from a mixture of mercury and sulfur, which themselves come from vapors and fumes. The author describes mercury's properties: it is very pure and does not stick to the hand, like water drops on fine dust forming a dusty coating. The whiteness of mercury is due to its watery and earthy components mixed with airy and sulfurous elements. Through heat, a ferments produces oiliness, then cold congeals it. The differences among the seven metals arise from the mixture and cooking conditions. If both mercury and sulfur are pure and cooking is complete: if sulfur is white, the product is silver; if red with a non-burning coloring power, the product is gold. If both are pure but cold congeals the mixture before cooking is complete, the result is Kharsini (raw gold). If mercury is pure but sulfur is inferior and burning, copper forms. If mixing is poor, lead forms. If both are inferior but cohesively mixed, iron forms; if not, Asrub (black lead) forms. The author then critiques that this division is not exhaustive because many other combinations are rationally possible. Furthermore, the theory of formation is not certain; it relies only on weak evidence and guesswork, such as that melted metals resemble mercury or that mercury can be congealed by sulfur smell. The passage ends with explanatory notes on terms: 'raw gold' resembles copper used for mirrors (Chinese iron); 'cold congelation' produces raw silver; 'al-Haws' means madness; 'spirits' are like mercury in alchemy; 'Eastern Investigations' is a reference; and 'al-Razana' means heaviness.

Key Terms
المعادن
المنطرقة
غير المنطرقة
الأجسام السبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all minerals are malleable or that the classification is the only one.
Correction: The passage clearly states only the first division (seven metals) is malleable; non-malleable minerals exist. Also, later the division is critiqued as non-exhaustive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'malleable' mean?
A) Breaks easily under a hammer
B) Cannot be hammered at all
C) Softens and expands when hammered without breaking
D) Melts when heated

Key Takeaway
Minerals are primarily classified into malleable (the seven metals) and non-malleable, a foundational distinction in classical mineralogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that malleable minerals do not break but spread out under the hammer, consider your own heart when trials strike. Do you become shattered and scattered, or do you soften and expand in reliance on Allah? What one step can you take today to become more malleable to the will of Allah?
?
The passage lists seven specific metals as the only malleable ones. Think about the 'seven' core beliefs or practices you personally cannot live without. Are they as solid and enduring as gold and iron? How can you reinforce them to ensure they are not corrupted?

Formation of the Seven Metals from Mercury and Sulfur
Explains the formation of each of the seven metals from mercury and sulfur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theory of how the seven metals are formed from mercury and sulfur under different conditions of purity, cooking, and cooling.

Core Idea
The seven metals are all made from mercury and sulfur, differing only by purity, cooking time, and quality of mixture.

After listing the seven malleable bodies, Sanusi explains their origin. All metals, he says, are formed from a mixture of mercury (زئبق) and sulfur (كبريت), which themselves arise from vapors and fumes. The properties of each metal depend on the purity of these components, the completeness of the cooking process, and any premature cooling. This lesson details that fascinating process.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic ingredients
Mercury and sulfur are the two components. Note the description of mercury: it is pure, does not stick, and when cooked with sulfur, it gradually congeals.
Mercury's properties explained via analogy to water drops on fine dust.
2
Learn the conditions for each metal
The passage lists seven conditions that produce gold, silver, copper, lead, iron, Kharsini, and Asrub. Pay attention to purity, cooking, and cooling.
For example, pure mercury + pure white sulfur + complete cooking = silver.
3
Identify the pattern
Notice how the outcomes vary based on the quality of the two ingredients and the cooking process. This is a systematic scheme.
4
Reflect on the theory's scope
This theory aims to explain all metals, but as the next lesson will show, it is not exhaustive.

القسمة غير حاصرة ) لجواز أن يكونا صافيين مع بياض الكبريت ويعقده البرد قبل تمام النضج وأن يكون الكبريت صافياً والزئبق ردياً أو بالعكس، ولا يكون الكبريت محرقاً إلى غير ذلك من الاحتمالات العقلية ( وإن التكون ) أي تكون الأجساد منهما على هذا الوجه لا سبيل فيه إلى اليقين، ولا يرجى فيه إلا الحدث والتخمين) بأمارات ضعيفة مثل قولهم : يدل على أن الزئبق عنصر المنطرقات أنها عند الذوبان تكون مثل الزئبق أما الرصاص فظاهر. وأما غيره فلانه عند الذوب زئبق أحمر ويدل عليه أيضاً أن الزئبق يعلق بهذه الأجساد وأنه يمكن أن يعقد برائحة الكبريت حتى يكون مثل الرصاص فإن أصحاب الإكسير يعقدون قوله : (وكأنه ذهب فج قيل هو جوهر يشبه النحاس يتخذ منه المرايا هو المسمى بالحديد الصيني . وقوله : يعقده البرد قبل تمام النضح فحينئذ يكون فضة فجاً أصابها برد عاقد . وقوله المهوسون بالكيمياء. الهوس بالتحريك ضرب من الجنون والهوس بالسكون الدق كذا في الصحاح. وقوله : الأرواح هي كالزئبق وهذا من مصطلحات أهل الإكسير. وقوله : وفيه أي في المباحث المشرقية. وقوله : والرزانة أي الثقل .
[The second chapter on what has no soul among the composite things] with temperament (and they are called minerals, and they are divided into two divisions: malleable) i.e. capable of being struck by a hammer such that they do not break or scatter but soften and extend into their depth and expand. (and non-malleable) i.e. not accepting that. ( [The first division: The seven bodies] the malleable, and they are the seven bodies) gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini (formed from the mixture of mercury and sulfur, which are formed from vapors and fumes. For mercury is a very pure vapor or water, mixed with fine sulfurous smokiness in a strong mixture such that no surface separates from it without being covered by something of that dryness; therefore it does not stick to the hand and is not confined to a shape of what contains it. Its example is drops of water falling on extremely fine dust, for a dusty surface surrounds the drop, enclosing the water like a cover for it, such that the drop remains in its shape on the face of the dust. And if two drops meet, perhaps it tears and the two waters become in one cover. And the whiteness of mercury is due to the purity of the watery and the whiteness of the earthy, and the mixing of the airy and the sulfur and the smokiness that ferments its vapor intensely with heat until an oiliness occurs in it, then it congeals with cold. And these seven differ (by their mixture according to a temperament prepared for that difference. For if both are pure and cooking is complete) i.e. the cooking of the mercury with the sulfur (and if the sulfur) with its purity and clarity (is white, then the product is silver; and if it is red and has a coloring power) fine, non-burning (then it) i.e. the product (is gold; and if both are pure and in the red sulfur there is coloring power, but (the cold congeals it before complete cooking, then it is Kharsini, as if it were raw gold, i.e. unripe, not reaching full maturity). And if the mercury is pure and the sulfur is inferior and burning, then it is copper; and if both, i.e. the pure mercury and the inferior sulfur, (are not well mixed, then it is lead; and if both are together inferior and the composition and cohesion between them is strong, then it is iron; and otherwise i.e. if the composition between them is not strong along with their inferiority, then it is Asrub). And it is called black lead. (And you are aware that the division is not exhaustive) because it is possible that both are pure with white sulfur and cold congeals it before full maturity, and that the sulfur is pure and the mercury is inferior or vice versa, and the sulfur is not burning, and other such rational possibilities. (And that the formation) i.e. the formation of the bodies from them in this way has no path to certainty, and only conjecture and guesswork are hoped for) with weak indicators like their saying: It indicates that mercury is the element of the malleable bodies that when melted they become like mercury; as for lead it is obvious. And as for others, because when melted it is red mercury; and it also indicates that mercury adheres to these bodies, and that it can be congealed by the smell of sulfur until it becomes like lead, for the alchemists congeal. His saying: (As if it is raw gold, it is said it is a substance resembling copper from which mirrors are made, called Chinese iron. And his saying: The cold congeals it before complete cooking, then it is raw silver struck by coagulating cold. And his saying: Those obsessed with alchemy. 'Al-Haws' with movement is a type of madness, and 'al-Haws' with sukun is pounding, as in al-Sihah. And his saying: The 'spirits' are like mercury, and this is from the terminology of the alchemists. And his saying: 'And in it' i.e. in the Eastern Investigations. And his saying: 'Al-Razana' i.e. heaviness.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies minerals, focusing on the seven malleable bodies. It begins by stating that minerals are divided into malleable (those that can be hammered without breaking) and non-malleable. The first division comprises the seven metals: gold, silver, lead, Asrub, iron, copper, and Kharsini (possibly zinc or brass). These are formed from a mixture of mercury and sulfur, which themselves come from vapors and fumes. The author describes mercury's properties: it is very pure and does not stick to the hand, like water drops on fine dust forming a dusty coating. The whiteness of mercury is due to its watery and earthy components mixed with airy and sulfurous elements. Through heat, a ferments produces oiliness, then cold congeals it. The differences among the seven metals arise from the mixture and cooking conditions. If both mercury and sulfur are pure and cooking is complete: if sulfur is white, the product is silver; if red with a non-burning coloring power, the product is gold. If both are pure but cold congeals the mixture before cooking is complete, the result is Kharsini (raw gold). If mercury is pure but sulfur is inferior and burning, copper forms. If mixing is poor, lead forms. If both are inferior but cohesively mixed, iron forms; if not, Asrub (black lead) forms. The author then critiques that this division is not exhaustive because many other combinations are rationally possible. Furthermore, the theory of formation is not certain; it relies only on weak evidence and guesswork, such as that melted metals resemble mercury or that mercury can be congealed by sulfur smell. The passage ends with explanatory notes on terms: 'raw gold' resembles copper used for mirrors (Chinese iron); 'cold congelation' produces raw silver; 'al-Haws' means madness; 'spirits' are like mercury in alchemy; 'Eastern Investigations' is a reference; and 'al-Razana' means heaviness.

الكبريت
الطبخ
الفضة
الذهب
الخارصيني
النحاس
الرصاص
الحديد
الأسرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all metals have the same formation process or that the theory is proven.
Correction: The passage shows each metal has specific conditions, and later it is admitted that the theory is speculative.

Checkpoint
What condition produces gold according to this passage?
A) Pure mercury and pure white sulfur with complete cooking
B) Pure mercury and pure red sulfur with non-burning coloring power and complete cooking
C) Pure mercury and inferior sulfur
D) Both ingredients inferior and poorly mixed

Key Takeaway
The seven metals are distinguished by varying proportions of mercury and sulfur and the completeness of their cooking, a speculative but systematic theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that the same basic ingredients, when mixed with different levels of purity and subjected to different processes, yield vastly different metals. Think about your own soul: are you aware of the 'impurities' in your character that prevent you from becoming 'gold'? What specific steps will you take this week to purify one of those areas?
?
The text warns that premature cooling can stop the formation of a mature metal, resulting in something raw and incomplete. In your spiritual life, where have you allowed the 'cold' of distractions or comfort to stop your growth before reaching maturity? What will you do to resume the process of 'cooking'?
?
If the alchemists were once certain about their theory, but later it was proven false, what does that teach you about the certainty of your own beliefs? Are there any assumptions you hold that you have not examined critically? Name one and commit to investigating it this month.

Critique of the Division and the Uncertain Nature of the Theory
Critiques the division as non-exhaustive and the theory as uncertain, with explanatory notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the metal classification as non-exhaustive and the acknowledgment that the formation theory is uncertain and based on weak evidence.

Core Idea
The classification and formation theory are not certain; they are speculative and open to other possibilities.

After presenting the theory of metal formation, Sanusi steps back to critique it. He points out that the division into the seven metals is not exhaustive — there could be other combinations. Moreover, the entire formation theory is not certain; it relies on conjecture and weak evidence, such as the behavior of metals when melted. This lesson highlights the epistemic humility of the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the passage where Sanusi says 'the division is not exhaustive' and 'no path to certainty' only conjecture.
2
List the rational possibilities
The passage enumerates several possible combinations not covered by the seven categories, such as both pure with white sulfur but cold before complete cooking.
3
Understand the evidence
The passage mentions weak indicators: melted metals resemble mercury, mercury can be congealed by sulfur smell. This shows the theory is based on observation but not certain.
4
Note the terminology
Explanatory notes define terms like 'raw gold', 'al-Haws' (madness), 'spirits' (like mercury), and 'al-Razana' (heaviness).
These notes clarify technical terms from alchemy.

intermediate

The Seven Malleable Bodies: Alchemical Theory and Its Critique
Al-Jurjani explains the alchemical formation of seven metals and critiques its certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alchemical theory of the seven metals' formation from mercury and sulfur, and why al-Jurjani considers such theories uncertain, affirming instead that all causation ultimately belongs to Allah.

Core Idea
Alchemy’s metal classification is speculative; only Allah is the true agent.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the classical alchemical theory of the seven malleable bodies, explaining how they supposedly form from varying mixtures of mercury and sulfur. But he immediately undercuts the certainty of this scheme, pointing to countless rational possibilities and weak evidence. This discussion serves a deeper theological purpose: to remind us that all natural processes are ultimately under the direct control of al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār, the Free Agent, and that human conjectures about necessary causes are baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full Arabic source carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms for the metals.
2
Understand the formation theory
Follow the conditions for each metal: purity of mercury and sulfur, color of sulfur, completeness of cooking, and mixing. Identify the seven metals listed.
The classification is based on a combination of factors.
3
Identify the critique of certainty
Find where al-Jurjani says the division is not exhaustive and the whole theory is based on conjecture. Note the weak indications he mentions.
He uses the phrase 'lā sabīl fīhi ilā al-yaqīn' – no way to certainty.
4
Connect to the theological conclusion
Read the final sentence: all is from the Free Agent. Understand how this aligns with Ashʿari occasionalism and rejection of natural causality.
This is the key takeaway for the lesson.

المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : الأجسام المنطرقة وهي سبعة والأدخنة فإن الزئبق بخارية أو مائية صافية جداً خالطها دخانيا كبريتية لطيفة مخالطة شديدة بحيث لا ينفصل منه سطح إلا ويغشاه من تلك اليبوسة شيء، فلذلك لا يعلق باليد ولا ينحصر انحصاراً بشكل ما يحويه ومثاله قطرات الماء الواقعة على تراب في غاية اللطافة فإنه يحيط بالقطرة سطح ترابي حاصر للماء كالغلاف له بحيث يبقى القطرة على شكلها في وجه التراب وإذا تلاقى قطرتان منها فربما ينخرق ويصير الماءان في غلاف واحد وبياض الزئبق لصفاء المائية وبياض الأرضية وممازجة الهوائية والكبريت ودخانية تخمر بها بخارية تخمراً شديداً بالحر حتى حصل فيها دهنية ثم انعقدت بالبرد وتختلف هذه السبعة ( باختلاطهما على مزاج معد لذلك الاختلاف فإنهما إن كانا صافيين وتم الطبخ) أي انطباخ الزئبق بالكبريت ( فإن كان الكبريت) مع صفائه ونقائه ( أبيض فالحاصل الفضة وإن كان أحمر وفيه قوة صباغة ) لطيفة غير محرقة ( فهو ) أي الحاصل ( الذهب وإن كانا نقيين وفي الكبريت الأحمر قوة صباغة، لكن ( عقده البرد قبل تمام الطبخ فهو الخارصيني وكأنه ذهب فج أي نيء لم يبلغ تمام النضج ) وإن كان الزئبق صافياً والكبريت ردياً محرقاً فهو النحاس، وإن كانا أي الزئبق النقي والكبريت الرديء (غير جيدي المخالطة فالرصاص وإن كانا معاً رديئين فإن قوي التركيب بينهما والالتئام. فهو الحديد (وإلا أي وإن لم يقو التركيب بينهما مع رداءتهما ) فهو الأسرب ) . ويسمى الرصاص الأسود ( وأنت خبير بأن القسمة غير حاصرة ) لجواز أن يكونا صافيين مع بياض الكبريت ويعقده البرد قبل تمام النضج وأن يكون الكبريت صافياً والزئبق ردياً أو بالعكس، ولا يكون الكبريت محرقاً إلى غير ذلك من الاحتمالات العقلية ( وإن التكون ) أي تكون الأجساد منهما على هذا الوجه لا سبيل فيه إلى اليقين، ولا يرجى فيه إلا الحدث والتخمين) بأمارات ضعيفة مثل قولهم : يدل على أن الزئبق عنصر المنطرقات أنها عند الذوبان تكون مثل الزئبق أما الرصاص فظاهر. وأما غيره فلانه عند الذوب زئبق أحمر ويدل عليه أيضاً أن الزئبق يعلق بهذه الأجساد وأنه يمكن أن يعقد برائحة الكبريت حتى يكون مثل الرصاص فإن أصحاب الإكسير يعقدون قوله : (وكأنه ذهب فج قيل هو جوهر يشبه النحاس يتخذ منه المرايا هو المسمى بالحديد الصيني . وقوله : يعقده البرد قبل تمام النضح فحينئذ يكون فضة فجاً أصابها برد عاقد . وقوله المهوسون بالكيمياء. الهوس بالتحريك ضرب من الجنون والهوس بالسكون الدق كذا في الصحاح. وقوله : الأرواح هي كالزئبق وهذا من مصطلحات أهل الإكسير. وقوله : وفيه أي في المباحث المشرقية. وقوله : والرزانة أي الثقل . الزئبق بالكباريت انعقادات محسوسة فيحصل لهم ظن بأن الأمور الطبيعية مقارنة للأحوال الصناعية ) ( وإن سلم تكونها منهما وإنه على هذا الوجه ( فتكونها ) من غيرهما أو منهما ( على غير هذا الوجه مما لم يقم على امتناعه دليل. كيف والمهوسون بالكيمياء لهم في الأجساد) السبعة ( والأرواح) التي تفيد الصورة الذهبية والفضية ( تفنن) لأنهم لا يقتصرون على أخلاط الكبريت والزئبق ) والكل عندنا للفاعل المختار بلا إحالة على شيء مما ذكروه كما مر مراراً .
The First Observatory - Second Chapter: First Section: The malleable bodies, and they are seven. And fumes: mercury is vaporous or very pure watery, mixed with fine sulfurous fumes in an intense mixture such that no surface separates from it without something of that dryness covering it. Therefore it does not adhere to the hand nor confine itself in the shape of its container. Its example is drops of water falling on extremely fine dust, for a dusty surface surrounds the drop confining the water like a wrapper for it, such that the drop remains in its shape on the face of the dust. And when two drops meet, perhaps it ruptures and the two waters become in one wrapper. The whiteness of mercury is due to the purity of its aqueous part and whiteness of its earthy part and admixture of airy and sulfur, and a fumy quality with which its vaporous part fermented intensely by heat until an oiliness occurred in it, then it congealed by cold. And these seven differ (by their mixing on a temperament prepared for that difference. For if they are both pure and cooking is complete — i.e., cooking of mercury with sulfur — then if the sulfur) with its purity and clarity (is white, the product is silver; and if it is red and has a dyeing power) fine non-burning (then it) i.e., the product (is gold. And if they are both pure and the red sulfur has dyeing power, but cold congealed it before complete cooking, then it is tutty (kharsīnī), as if it were raw gold, i.e., unripe not reaching full maturity. And if the mercury is pure and the sulfur is inferior burning, then it is copper. And if both — i.e., the pure mercury and the inferior sulfur — are not well mixed, then it is lead. And if both are inferior and the composition between them and their adhesion is strong, then it is iron. Otherwise — i.e., if the composition is not strong between them despite their inferiority — then it is usrub (lead alloy?). It is called black lead. And you are aware that the division is not exhaustive) because it is possible that both are pure with whiteness of sulfur and cold congeals it before full maturity, and that the sulfur is pure and mercury is inferior or vice versa, and the sulfur is not burning, etc., among rational possibilities. (And that the formation — i.e., the bodies' formation from them in this manner — there is no way to certainty in it, and only conjecture and guesswork are hoped for) by weak indications, such as their saying: It indicates that mercury is the element of the malleable bodies because when they melt they become like mercury. As for lead, it is obvious. As for others, because when melting it is red mercury. It also indicates that mercury adheres to these bodies and that it can congeal by the smell of sulfur until it becomes like lead, for the masters of the elixir congeal. His words: (As if it were raw gold—it is said it is a substance resembling copper, from which mirrors are made, called Chinese iron. And his words: Cold congealed it before full cooking, so then it is raw silver affected by congealing cold. And his words: the ravers (muhawwisūn) in alchemy. Al-hawas (with fatha) is a type of madness, and al-haws (with sukun) is pulverization, as in al-Sihah. And his words: the spirits (al-arwāḥ) are like mercury, and this is from the terminology of the people of the elixir. And his words: 'fīhi' i.e., in the Eastern investigations. And his words: 'al-razāna' i.e., weight. Mercury by sulfurs has sensible congealings, so they obtain an opinion that natural matters are comparable to artificial states. (And even if it is conceded that their formation is from both and in this manner, (then their formation) from other than both or from both in another manner is not proven impossible by evidence. How so, while the ravers in alchemy have diversity in the seven bodies and the spirits that yield the golden and silver forms, because they do not confine themselves to mixtures of sulfur and mercury. And all of it, in our view, is from the Free Agent (al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār) without reference to anything they mentioned, as has passed repeatedly.)
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the seven malleable bodies according to alchemical theory, followed by a critique of the certainty of this classification and a reaffirmation of divine agency. The author begins by identifying the seven malleable bodies (al-ajsām al-munṭariqa) and describes the nature of mercury as a vaporous or watery substance mixed with sulfurous fumes, giving the example of water droplets on dust to illustrate how mercury does not adhere to the hand. He then explains the process of formation: the seven metals differ based on the purity of mercury and sulfur, the completeness of cooking, and the action of cold. The classification lists silver, gold, tutty (kharsīnī), copper, lead, iron, and black lead (usrub) under various conditions of purity and mixing. However, the author immediately notes that this division is not exhaustive (ghayr ḥāṣira), as many other rational possibilities exist. He then states that the entire theory of formation is not certain (lā sabīl fīhi ilā al-yaqīn) and relies only on weak indications, such as the behavior of metals upon melting and the observations of alchemists (aṣḥāb al-iksīr). The passage includes marginal glosses explaining terms like 'raw gold' (dhahab fajj), 'Chinese iron', 'ravers' (muhawwisūn), and 'spirits' (arwāḥ). Finally, the author concludes by reaffirming that all such phenomena are ultimately attributed to the Free Agent (al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār) without necessary reference to natural causes, as has been stated repeatedly. This underscores the Ashʿarī commitment to occasionalism and the rejection of necessary causal connections in nature.

Key Terms
الأجسام المنطرقة (malleable bodies)
الزئبق (mercury)
الكبريت (sulfur)
الأجساد السبعة (the seven bodies)
الخارصيني (tutty)
الأسرب (lead alloy / usrub)
أصحاب الإكسير (masters of the elixir)
الفاعل المختار (the Free Agent)
الحدس والتخمين (conjecture and guesswork)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The alchemical classification of metals is a certain, exhaustive natural law.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states that the division is not exhaustive (ghayr ḥāṣira) and that the formation theory is not certain (lā sabīl fīhi ilā al-yaqīn), leaving room for many other possibilities and attributing all to the Free Agent.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani consider the classification of the seven malleable bodies as non-exhaustive?
A) Because the alchemists disagree among themselves.
B) Because many rational possibilities exist beyond the listed conditions.
C) Because the theory is based on revelation, not reason.
D) Because iron cannot be melted.

Key Takeaway
Alchemy's metal theory is uncertain and non-exhaustive; true causation belongs solely to Allah, the Free Agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the author's dismissal of alchemical certainty, do you feel a tension between trusting scientific explanations and affirming Allah's direct agency? In what area of your life have you instinctively attributed an outcome to 'natural laws' rather than to Allah's moment-by-moment creation? Will you consciously remind yourself this week that every cause is ultimately from Allah?
?
The passage calls alchemical claims 'conjecture and guesswork'. Are there beliefs or assumptions in your own life that you hold with false certainty—perhaps about people, events, or the future—while neglecting the truth that only Allah knows all? Will you repent from such certainty and submit to the Giver of knowledge?
?
If all phenomena, including the formation of metals, are directly from Allah, how does that change the way you view your daily routines, your work, and the world around you? Are you living as if secondary causes matter more than the First Cause? Commit today to see Allah's hand in every detail, no matter how small.

Classification of Non-Malleable Minerals
Al-Jurjani classifies non-malleable minerals and defines the seven bodies' properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani classifies non-malleable minerals based on physical properties such as moisture, solubility, and composition strength, and how the seven bodies are defined by fusibility, endurance, and malleability.

Core Idea
Non-malleable minerals are not a single type—they subdivide by moisture, solubility, and hardness.

Al-Jurjani presents a detailed classification of minerals that cannot be hammered. He begins with a simple division based on the cause of non-malleability, then complements it with a more systematic taxonomy from the Eastern Investigations. This lesson walks through the classification, the shared properties of the seven malleable bodies, and a brief objection about iron.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Let the classification structure emerge naturally.
Notice the two different classification systems presented: one in the opening lines and one from the Eastern Investigations.
2
Identify the main dichotomies
Find the two major divisions: (1) non-malleability due to softness vs. other causes, and (2) among the others, solubility vs. insolubility.
Also note the alternative classification by composition strength from the Eastern Investigations.
3
Examine the three properties of the seven bodies
Focus on the shared characteristics: fusible, enduring, malleable. See how each distinguishes them from other substances.
The objection about iron tests the definition of fusibility.
4
Consider the objection and reply about iron
Understand why iron is considered fusible even though it softens rather than melts. The reply shows the classification is based on potential under artifice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني غير المنطرقة من المعادن ( وعدم انطراقها إما للين وفرط الرطوبة ( كالزئبق أولا، وحينئذ إما أن تنحل بالرطوبات كالأملاح والزاجات (أولا) تنحل ( كالطلق والزرنيخ). وفي المباحث المشرقية الآن أجسام المعدنية إما قوية التركيب وحينئذ إما أن يكون منطرقاً وهو الأجساد السبعة أو غير منطرق إما لغاية رطوبته كالزئبق أو لغاية يبوسته كالياقوت ونظائره، وإما ضعيفة التركيب فإما أن تنحل بالرطوبة وهو الذي يكون ملحي الجوهر كالزاج والنوشادر والشب أولا تنحل وهو الذي يكون دهني التركيب كالكبريت والزرنيخ وفيه أيضاً أن الأجساد السبعة متشاركة في أنها أجسام ذائبة صابرة منطرقة . فالذائب يميزها عن الأكلاس والأحجار التي لا تذوب والصابر عما يذوب ويتنجز كالشمع والقير والمنطرق عما ليس بمنطرق كالزجاج والميناء. فإن قيل : الحديد لا يذوب وإن كان يلين قلنا : يمكن إذابته بالحيلة ويمتاز الذهب عن أخواته بالصفرة والرزانة والفضة بالبياض والرزانة بالقياس إلى ما سوى الذهب .
Second Section: Non-malleable minerals. And their non-malleability is either due to softness and excess moisture (like mercury), or not. Then either they dissolve by moisture like salts and vitriols (zājāt), or they do not dissolve (like talc and arsenic). And in the Eastern investigations (al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya), mineral bodies are either strong in composition—then either malleable, which are the seven bodies, or non-malleable, either due to extreme moisture like mercury, or due to extreme dryness like ruby and its analogues—or weak in composition—then either they dissolve by moisture, which are of salty essence like vitriol, sal ammoniac (nūshādir), and alum, or do not dissolve, which are of fatty composition like sulfur and arsenic. And also in it is that the seven bodies share that they are fusible, enduring (ṣābira), and malleable. Fusibility distinguishes them from alkalis (aklās) and stones that do not melt; endurance distinguishes them from what melts and flows like wax and tar; malleability distinguishes them from what is not malleable like glass and enamel. If it is said: iron does not melt although it softens, we say: it can be melted by artifice. And gold is distinguished from its sisters by yellowness and gravity, and silver by whiteness and gravity relative to what is other than gold.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a classification of non-malleable minerals (al-ajsām ghayr al-munṭariqa) and their properties. The author begins by stating that non-malleability arises either from softness and excess moisture (like mercury) or from other causes. He then divides non-malleable minerals into those that dissolve in moisture (e.g., salts and vitriols) and those that do not (e.g., talc and arsenic). Citing the Eastern investigations, he offers a more detailed classification: mineral bodies are either strong in composition (either malleable—the seven bodies—or non-malleable due to extreme moisture like mercury or extreme dryness like ruby) or weak in composition (either soluble in moisture—salty substances like vitriol, sal ammoniac, alum—or insoluble—fatty substances like sulfur and arsenic). The passage then notes that the seven malleable bodies share three characteristics: they are fusible (dhāʾib), enduring (ṣābir, i.e., they do not quickly tarnish or flow), and malleable (munṭariq). These distinguish them from non-fusible substances (alkalis and stones), from easily flowing substances (wax, tar), and from non-malleable substances (glass, enamel). An objection is raised: iron does not melt though it softens. The reply is that iron can be melted by artifice. Finally, gold and silver are distinguished from the other malleable bodies by color and gravity (al-ṣufra wa al-razāna for gold, al-bayāḍ wa al-razāna for silver).

Key Terms
الأجسام غير المنطرقة (non-malleable bodies)
الذائب (fusible)
الصابر (enduring)
المنطرق (malleable)
الزاجات (vitriols)
النوشادر (sal ammoniac)
الشب (alum)
الطلق (talc)
الزرنيخ (arsenic)
الرزانة (gravity)
المباحث المشرقية (Eastern investigations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The minerals classified here correspond to modern chemical elements.
Correction: The classification is based on observable physical properties (solubility, fusibility, malleability) within an Aristotelian-alchemical framework, not on modern atomic theory.

Checkpoint
How does the passage distinguish gold from other metals?
A) Gold is the only metal that does not tarnish.
B) Gold is distinguished by yellowness and gravity.
C) Gold is the most malleable metal.
D) Gold is the only metal that melts easily.

Key Takeaway
Non-malleable minerals are classified by physical properties such as moisture, solubility, and composition, and the seven bodies share fusibility, endurance, and malleability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth that challenges your current understanding, like the classification of iron as a malleable body despite its resistance to melting, are you quick to object or do you seek the 'artifice' that reveals how it fits? Is your soul more like the rigid non-malleable mineral or the one that can be shaped by careful reasoning?
?
The seven bodies are distinguished by gravity and color—gold by yellowness and weight. What distinguishing marks does your character bear? When others look at you, do they see the weight of wisdom and the color of sincerity, or are you indistinguishable from the base minerals of this world?
