Alchemical Context and Divine Causation
Introduction to non-malleable minerals and critique of alchemical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alchemical context of the discussion about non-malleable minerals and the author's theological stance on causality.

Core Idea
All creation is directly from Allah, not necessitated by natural processes.

This passage opens the discussion of non-malleable minerals by addressing the alchemical claim that minerals are formed from mercury and sulfur. The author acknowledges the empirical observations but asserts that the theological principle of divine causation overrides any natural necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the mention of mercury and sulfur coagulations.
The text starts with the title and then the alchemical observation.
2
Understand the alchemical claim
Alchemists believe that natural processes imitate artificial ones, and they have many mixtures.
The 'seven bodies' and 'spirits' refer to classical metals and substances.
3
Grasp the theological response
The author says that even if such compositions occur, other possibilities are not disproven; ultimately, everything is from the Free Agent.
This reflects the Ash'ari principle of occasionalism.
4
Reflect on the implications
This shows how kalam evaluates natural science within a theological framework.
The author does not deny empirical observations but subordinates them to divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الثاني : الأجسام غير المنطرقة من المعادن الزئبق بالكباريت انعقادات محسوسة فيحصل لهم ظن بأن الأمور الطبيعية مقارنة للأحوال الصناعية ) ( وإن سلم تكونها منهما وإنه على هذا الوجه ( فتكونها ) من غيرهما أو منهما ( على غير هذا الوجه مما لم يقم على امتناعه دليل. كيف والمهوسون بالكيمياء لهم في الأجساد) السبعة ( والأرواح) التي تفيد الصورة الذهبية والفضية ( تفنن) لأنهم لا يقتصرون على أخلاط الكبريت والزئبق ) والكل عندنا للفاعل المختار بلا إحالة على شيء مما ذكروه كما مر مراراً . [ القسم الثاني غير المنطرقة ] من المعادن ( وعدم انطراقها إما للين وفرط الرطوبة ( كالزئبق أولا، وحينئذ إما أن تنحل بالرطوبات كالأملاح والزاجات (أولا) تنحل ( كالطلق والزرنيخ). وفي المباحث المشرقية الآن أجسام المعدنية إما قوية التركيب وحينئذ إما أن يكون منطرقاً وهو الأجساد السبعة أو غير منطرق إما لغاية رطوبته كالزئبق أو لغاية يبوسته كالياقوت ونظائره، وإما ضعيفة التركيب فإما أن تنحل بالرطوبة وهو الذي يكون ملحي الجوهر كالزاج والنوشادر والشب أولا تنحل وهو الذي يكون دهني التركيب کالكبريت والزرنيخ وفيه أيضاً أن الأجساد السبعة متشاركة في أنها أجسام ذائبة صابرة منطرقة . فالذائب يميزها عن الأكلاس والأحجار التي لا تذوب والصابر عما يذوب ويتنجز كالشمع والقير والمنطرق عما ليس بمنطرق كالزجاج والميناء. فإن قيل : الحديد لا يذوب وإن كان يلين قلنا : يمكن إذابته بالحيلة ويمتاز الذهب عن أخواته بالصفرة والرزانة والفضة بالبياض والرزانة بالقياس إلى ما سوى الذهب .
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Second Section: Non-malleable minerals. Mercury with sulfur has perceptible coagulations, so they get the impression that natural matters are comparable to artificial states. And even if it is conceded that they are composed of the two and in this manner, their composition from other than them or from them in a different manner is something for which proof of impossibility has not been established. How so, while those obsessed with chemistry have variety in the seven bodies and the spirits that bestow the gold and silver form, because they do not confine themselves to mixtures of sulfur and mercury. And all, according to us, is from the Free Agent without reference to anything they mentioned, as has been repeated many times. [The Second Section: Non-malleable minerals]. Their non-malleability is either due to softness and excessive moisture, like mercury, or not. In that case, either they dissolve through moisture, like salts and vitriols, or they do not dissolve, like talc and arsenic. In the Eastern Investigations, mineral bodies are either strong in composition: then either malleable, which are the seven bodies, or non-malleable, either due to extreme moisture like mercury or extreme dryness like ruby and its analogues; or weak in composition: then either they dissolve in moisture, which is the salty substance like vitriol, sal ammoniac, and alum, or they do not dissolve, which is the fatty composition like sulfur and arsenic. It also states that the seven bodies share the properties of being fusible, durable, and malleable. Fusibility distinguishes them from alkalis and stones that do not melt; durability distinguishes them from things that melt and change like wax and pitch; malleability distinguishes them from things that are not malleable like glass and enamel. If it is said: Iron does not melt although it becomes soft, we say: It can be melted by artifice. Gold is distinguished from its fellows by yellowness and heaviness, and silver by whiteness and heaviness relative to what is other than gold.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a classification of non-malleable minerals. It begins by mentioning that mercury with sulfur produces perceptible coagulations, leading alchemists to think natural processes mimic artificial ones. The author asserts that even if such compositions occur, other possibilities are not disproven. Then the text defines non-malleable minerals and divides them: non-malleability due to softness/moisture (like mercury) or not. The latter is further subdivided into those that dissolve in moisture (salts, vitriols) and those that do not (talc, arsenic). The passage then quotes 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya' offering another classification: strong-composition minerals (malleable: seven bodies; non-malleable: extremely moist like mercury or extremely dry like ruby) and weak-composition minerals (saline and dissolvable, or fatty and non-dissolvable). The seven bodies (metals) share three properties: fusible, durable, malleable. Each property is explained: fusibility distinguishes from stones; durability from wax/pitch; malleability from glass. An objection about iron not melting is answered by noting it can be melted by artifice. The passage ends with how gold and silver are distinguished.

Key Terms
انعقادات
الكباريت
أجساد سبعة
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Minerals are necessarily formed by specific mixtures of mercury and sulfur.
Correction: The author argues that other compositions are possible and all is from Allah's direct action, rejecting necessary causality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's position on alchemical compositions?
A) They are necessarily true.
B) They are impossible.
C) They are possible but not necessarily the only way, and all is from Allah.
D) They are only artificial.

Key Takeaway
The author insists that all mineral formation is subject to divine causation, not natural necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe natural processes—like the formation of minerals or the growth of plants—do you truly see them as directly from Allah, or do you subconsciously attribute them to fixed natural laws that operate independently? What would change in your daily gratitude if you consciously affirmed 'All is from the Free Agent'?
?
The author critiques the alchemists for assuming necessity in nature. Are there areas in your own life where you assume 'this must happen because of this cause'—forgetting that Allah could bring about the same outcome through a different means or none at all? How does that assumption affect your reliance on Allah?
?
If you truly believed that all phenomena are from the Free Agent with no necessary reference to secondary causes, how would it transform your response to unexpected events—both good and bad? Would you still worry about the 'means' as much as you do?

Classification of Non-Malleable Minerals
Classification of non-malleable minerals by two schemes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classification systems for non-malleable minerals presented in the text.

Core Idea
Non-malleable minerals are classified by moisture, solubility, and composition strength.

This lesson presents two complementary classifications of non-malleable minerals. The first is based on the cause of non-malleability and solubility. The second, from the Eastern Investigations, uses composition strength and additional categories.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first classification
Note the division: non-malleability due to moisture (mercury) or not; if not, then either soluble or not in moisture.
Examples: salts/vitriols (soluble), talc/arsenic (insoluble).
2
Understand the second classification
From al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: strong composition minerals (malleable or non-malleable) and weak composition minerals (soluble or non-soluble).
Non-malleable strong minerals are either very moist (mercury) or very dry (ruby).
3
Compare the two classifications
See how they overlap: e.g., mercury appears in both as non-malleable due to moisture.
The second classification adds the dimension of composition strength and includes gems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الثاني : الأجسام غير المنطرقة من المعادن الزئبق بالكباريت انعقادات محسوسة فيحصل لهم ظن بأن الأمور الطبيعية مقارنة للأحوال الصناعية ) ( وإن سلم تكونها منهما وإنه على هذا الوجه ( فتكونها ) من غيرهما أو منهما ( على غير هذا الوجه مما لم يقم على امتناعه دليل. كيف والمهوسون بالكيمياء لهم في الأجساد) السبعة ( والأرواح) التي تفيد الصورة الذهبية والفضية ( تفنن) لأنهم لا يقتصرون على أخلاط الكبريت والزئبق ) والكل عندنا للفاعل المختار بلا إحالة على شيء مما ذكروه كما مر مراراً . [ القسم الثاني غير المنطرقة ] من المعادن ( وعدم انطراقها إما للين وفرط الرطوبة ( كالزئبق أولا، وحينئذ إما أن تنحل بالرطوبات كالأملاح والزاجات (أولا) تنحل ( كالطلق والزرنيخ). وفي المباحث المشرقية الآن أجسام المعدنية إما قوية التركيب وحينئذ إما أن يكون منطرقاً وهو الأجساد السبعة أو غير منطرق إما لغاية رطوبته كالزئبق أو لغاية يبوسته كالياقوت ونظائره، وإما ضعيفة التركيب فإما أن تنحل بالرطوبة وهو الذي يكون ملحي الجوهر كالزاج والنوشادر والشب أولا تنحل وهو الذي يكون دهني التركيب کالكبريت والزرنيخ وفيه أيضاً أن الأجساد السبعة متشاركة في أنها أجسام ذائبة صابرة منطرقة . فالذائب يميزها عن الأكلاس والأحجار التي لا تذوب والصابر عما يذوب ويتنجز كالشمع والقير والمنطرق عما ليس بمنطرق كالزجاج والميناء. فإن قيل : الحديد لا يذوب وإن كان يلين قلنا : يمكن إذابته بالحيلة ويمتاز الذهب عن أخواته بالصفرة والرزانة والفضة بالبياض والرزانة بالقياس إلى ما سوى الذهب .
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Second Section: Non-malleable minerals. Mercury with sulfur has perceptible coagulations, so they get the impression that natural matters are comparable to artificial states. And even if it is conceded that they are composed of the two and in this manner, their composition from other than them or from them in a different manner is something for which proof of impossibility has not been established. How so, while those obsessed with chemistry have variety in the seven bodies and the spirits that bestow the gold and silver form, because they do not confine themselves to mixtures of sulfur and mercury. And all, according to us, is from the Free Agent without reference to anything they mentioned, as has been repeated many times. [The Second Section: Non-malleable minerals]. Their non-malleability is either due to softness and excessive moisture, like mercury, or not. In that case, either they dissolve through moisture, like salts and vitriols, or they do not dissolve, like talc and arsenic. In the Eastern Investigations, mineral bodies are either strong in composition: then either malleable, which are the seven bodies, or non-malleable, either due to extreme moisture like mercury or extreme dryness like ruby and its analogues; or weak in composition: then either they dissolve in moisture, which is the salty substance like vitriol, sal ammoniac, and alum, or they do not dissolve, which is the fatty composition like sulfur and arsenic. It also states that the seven bodies share the properties of being fusible, durable, and malleable. Fusibility distinguishes them from alkalis and stones that do not melt; durability distinguishes them from things that melt and change like wax and pitch; malleability distinguishes them from things that are not malleable like glass and enamel. If it is said: Iron does not melt although it becomes soft, we say: It can be melted by artifice. Gold is distinguished from its fellows by yellowness and heaviness, and silver by whiteness and heaviness relative to what is other than gold.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a classification of non-malleable minerals. It begins by mentioning that mercury with sulfur produces perceptible coagulations, leading alchemists to think natural processes mimic artificial ones. The author asserts that even if such compositions occur, other possibilities are not disproven. Then the text defines non-malleable minerals and divides them: non-malleability due to softness/moisture (like mercury) or not. The latter is further subdivided into those that dissolve in moisture (salts, vitriols) and those that do not (talc, arsenic). The passage then quotes 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya' offering another classification: strong-composition minerals (malleable: seven bodies; non-malleable: extremely moist like mercury or extremely dry like ruby) and weak-composition minerals (saline and dissolvable, or fatty and non-dissolvable). The seven bodies (metals) share three properties: fusible, durable, malleable. Each property is explained: fusibility distinguishes from stones; durability from wax/pitch; malleability from glass. An objection about iron not melting is answered by noting it can be melted by artifice. The passage ends with how gold and silver are distinguished.

Key Terms
منطرق
الكباريت
الأملاح
الزاجات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Eastern Investigations, weak-composition minerals are either...
A) malleable or non-malleable
B) soluble in moisture or non-soluble
C) moist or dry
D) fusible or durable

Key Takeaway
Non-malleable minerals are systematically classified based on moisture, solubility, and composition strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a piece of knowledge as contradictory without examining it closely, like the two classifications here? What assumption are you holding onto that might actually be complementary to another view if you looked deeper?
?
The author carefully distinguishes causes of non-malleability. Where in your own beliefs about Allah's creation have you confused cause and effect or oversimplified? Commit to studying one aspect of nature this week to see its true hikmah.
?
The Eastern Investigations classification distinguishes between strong and weak composition. Consider your own iman: is it strong or weak? And if weak, does it dissolve easily with worldly moisture or does it resist like oily substances? What does this passage teach you about strengthening your spiritual composition?

Properties of the Seven Bodies and the Iron Objection
Properties of the seven metals and defense of their malleability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining properties of the seven metals (bodies) and the response to the objection about iron's fusibility.

Core Idea
The seven metals are defined by being fusible, durable, and malleable.

This lesson focuses on the seven bodies (metals) and their shared properties: fusibility, durability, and malleability. The text explains how each property distinguishes them from other substances, and addresses a potential objection regarding iron.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three properties
Read the description: 'ذائبة صابرة منطرقة' (fusible, durable, malleable).
These are the common attributes of the seven metals.
2
Understand the distinctions
Fusibility distinguishes from stones; durability from wax/pitch; malleability from glass/enamel.
Each property excludes a class of non-metals.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Objection: iron does not melt (though it softens). Answer: it can be melted by artifice.
The author maintains that iron still qualifies as fusible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الثاني : الأجسام غير المنطرقة من المعادن الزئبق بالكباريت انعقادات محسوسة فيحصل لهم ظن بأن الأمور الطبيعية مقارنة للأحوال الصناعية ) ( وإن سلم تكونها منهما وإنه على هذا الوجه ( فتكونها ) من غيرهما أو منهما ( على غير هذا الوجه مما لم يقم على امتناعه دليل. كيف والمهوسون بالكيمياء لهم في الأجساد) السبعة ( والأرواح) التي تفيد الصورة الذهبية والفضية ( تفنن) لأنهم لا يقتصرون على أخلاط الكبريت والزئبق ) والكل عندنا للفاعل المختار بلا إحالة على شيء مما ذكروه كما مر مراراً . [ القسم الثاني غير المنطرقة ] من المعادن ( وعدم انطراقها إما للين وفرط الرطوبة ( كالزئبق أولا، وحينئذ إما أن تنحل بالرطوبات كالأملاح والزاجات (أولا) تنحل ( كالطلق والزرنيخ). وفي المباحث المشرقية الآن أجسام المعدنية إما قوية التركيب وحينئذ إما أن يكون منطرقاً وهو الأجساد السبعة أو غير منطرق إما لغاية رطوبته كالزئبق أو لغاية يبوسته كالياقوت ونظائره، وإما ضعيفة التركيب فإما أن تنحل بالرطوبة وهو الذي يكون ملحي الجوهر كالزاج والنوشادر والشب أولا تنحل وهو الذي يكون دهني التركيب کالكبريت والزرنيخ وفيه أيضاً أن الأجساد السبعة متشاركة في أنها أجسام ذائبة صابرة منطرقة . فالذائب يميزها عن الأكلاس والأحجار التي لا تذوب والصابر عما يذوب ويتنجز كالشمع والقير والمنطرق عما ليس بمنطرق كالزجاج والميناء. فإن قيل : الحديد لا يذوب وإن كان يلين قلنا : يمكن إذابته بالحيلة ويمتاز الذهب عن أخواته بالصفرة والرزانة والفضة بالبياض والرزانة بالقياس إلى ما سوى الذهب .

Definition of the Vegetative Soul and Its Components
Defines the vegetative soul and analyzes each term in its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the vegetative soul, the meaning of each qualifier, and how it differs from other perfections and temperaments.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul is a first perfection — it completes the essence, not just an attribute.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif lays out the definition of the vegetative soul with remarkable precision. Before proceeding to the other souls, al-Jurjani ensures that every term in the definition is carefully delimited. Pay close attention to the distinction between first and second perfection — it is the key to understanding what kind of perfection the soul is.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the definition: 'a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of being nourished and growing.' Observe that each qualifier has a purpose.
The word 'first' is crucial.
2
Understand the two types of perfection
The author explains that 'perfection' (al-kamal) is a genus. First perfection completes the species in its essence (e.g., the form of a bed). Second perfection completes it in its attributes (e.g., whiteness). The qualifier 'first' excludes second perfections.
The vegetative soul is a first perfection because it is part of the essence of the living body.
3
Trace the exclusions of each qualifier
Walk through how 'body' excludes immaterial entities, 'natural' excludes artificial bodies, and 'organic' excludes elements and minerals. Notice the grammatical note on 'organic' as an attribute of either 'first perfection' or 'body'.
The objection regarding temperaments will test your understanding.
4
Examine the objection and its resolution
Read the objection: if first perfection is simultaneous with the species, then temperament would also be a first perfection, yet it is not called a soul. Study the response: first perfection must be internal to the constitution (qiwam), while temperament is only a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تعريف النفس وهي ثلاث الأولى : النفس النباتية وهي كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي من حيث يتغذى وينمو، فالكمال جنس يتناول المحدود وغيره لأنه عبارة عما يتم به النوع إما في ذاته ويسمى كمالاً أولاً ومنوعاً كصورة السرير مثلاً فإنها كمال للخشب السريري لا يتم السرير في حد ذاته إلا بها. وإما في صفاته كالبياض فإنه كمال للجسم الأبيض لا يكمل في صفته إلا به ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً. وبأول يخرج عن الحد الكمالات الثانية المتأخرة عن تحصل النوع في نفسه كتوابع الكمال الأول المحصل للنوع من العلم والقدرة وغيرهما من الصفات المتفرعة على تحصل الأنواع في ذواتها وبالجسم يخرج عنه كمال المجردات أي منوعها، وبالطبيعي يخرج الجسم الصناعي أي يخرج صور الأجسام الصناعية كالسرير والكرسي فإن صورتهما لا تسمى نفساً وبالآلي يخرج العناصر أي صورها إذ لا يصدر عنها أفعالها بواسطة الآلات، وكذلك الصور المعدنية فلفظ آلي يجوز رفعه على أنه صفة لكمال أول أي كمال ذو آلة ويجوز جره على أنه صفة لجسم أي جسم مشتمل على الآلة، وهذا أظهر وعلى قوله : ( المتأخرة عن تحصل النوع فإن قيل : فعلى هذا يلزم أن تكون الأمزجة النباتية والحيوانية والإنسانية كمالات أول لعدم تأخرها عن تحصل تلك الأنواع مع أن المزاج لا يسمى نفسا . قلنا : المراد بالكمال الأول للشيء هو ما كان محصلاً في نفسه وداخلاً في قوامه. كما أشار إليه بقوله : ما يتم به النوع في ذاته، وظاهر أن الأمزجة المذكورة ليست بداخلة في قوام تلك الأنواع. وإنما هي شروط لتحصلها في أنفسها.
On the definition of the soul. They are three. The first: the vegetative soul, and it is a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of what it is nourished and grows. So perfection (al-kamal) is a genus covering the defined and others, because it is an expression of that by which the species is completed: either in its essence, and it is called first perfection and specific difference, like the form of a bed for example, for it is a perfection for the bed-wood, the bed is not completed in itself except by it; or in its attributes, like whiteness, for it is a perfection for the white body, it is not completed in its attribute except by it, and it is called second perfection. And by 'first' are excluded from the definition the second perfections that are posterior to the attainment of the species in itself, like the appendages of the first perfection that realizes the species: knowledge, power, and other attributes that branch off from the attainment of the species in their essences. And by 'body' is excluded from it the perfection of the immaterial entities, i.e., their specific differences. And by 'natural' is excluded the artificial body, i.e., the forms of artificial bodies like the bed and the chair, for their forms are not called soul. And by 'organic' are excluded the elements, i.e., their forms, since their acts do not proceed from them by means of instruments, and likewise the mineral forms. So the word 'organic' (ali) may be raised (in i'rab) as an attribute of 'first perfection', i.e., a perfection possessing an instrument, and may be declined as an attribute of 'body', i.e., a body comprising the instrument, and this is more apparent. And on his saying: (posterior to the attainment of the species). For it is said: on this basis it would follow that the plant, animal, and human temperaments (amzija) are first perfections because they are not posterior to the attainment of those species, despite the temperament not being called a soul. We say: what is meant by the first perfection of a thing is what is realizing in itself and entering into its constitution, as he indicated by his saying: that by which the species is completed in its essence, and it is apparent that the mentioned temperaments are not entering into the constitution of those species, but rather they are conditions for their attainment in themselves.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the vegetative soul and meticulously explains each qualifier in its definition. First, it states that the souls are three and defines the vegetative soul as 'a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of being nourished and growing'. It then analyzes the genus 'perfection' (al-kamal), which is a genus covering the defined and other things, because it is that by which a species is completed. Perfection is of two kinds: first perfection, which completes the species in its essence (e.g., the form of a bed for a bed), and second perfection, which completes the species in its attributes (e.g., whiteness). The word 'first' excludes second perfections. 'Body' excludes perfections of immaterial entities like the intellects. 'Natural' excludes artificial bodies like a bed or chair. 'Organic' excludes elements and minerals, which lack instruments for their acts. The passage then notes two possible grammatical assignments for 'organic': as an attribute of 'first perfection' (a perfection with instruments) or of 'body' (a body with instruments), considering the latter more apparent. Finally, it addresses an objection: if first perfection is that which is realized simultaneously with the species, then temperament (mizaj) would also be a first perfection, but temperament is not called soul. The answer clarifies that first perfection must be internal to the constitution of the species, while temperament is merely a condition for its attainment.

Key Terms
النفس النباتية
كمال أول
جسم طبيعي آلي
حيثية التغذي والنمو
كمال ثان
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temperament (mizaj) is a first perfection and thus should be considered a soul.
Correction: First perfection must be internal to the essence (qiwam), while temperament is a condition, not part of the constitution.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, the vegetative soul is a first perfection. What does 'first' exclude?
A) Immaterial entities
B) Second perfections like knowledge and power
C) Artificial bodies like beds
D) Elements and minerals

Key Takeaway
The vegetative soul is a first perfection that enters into the essence of a natural organic body, not merely a condition like temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson distinguishes between what is essential to your very nature (first perfection) and what is merely a condition for it (like temperament). What parts of your daily life — your habits, your identity, your routines — are you treating as if they are essential to your soul, when they may actually be only conditions? Will you dare to examine which one is truly foundational?
?
The vegetative soul is shared with plants — a reminder that your physical life is not your ultimate distinction. If you stripped away everything merely vegetative from your existence, what remains that is uniquely human and uniquely yours? Are you feeding that higher part, or just nourishing the plant within? Commit to one action today that serves your 'first perfection' rather than your vegetative needs.
?
The author insists that a 'first perfection' must enter into the very constitution of a thing, not just be a prerequisite. What in your relationship with Allah and your spiritual life is actually part of your 'constitution' — non-negotiable and defining — versus what is merely a helpful condition like temperament? Be honest: could you lose your prayer, your charity, or your community ties and still remain the same person in essence? If not, what does that say about your soul's true perfection?

Definitions of Animal and Human Souls and Unified Definition
Defines animal and human souls and offers unified definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of animal and human souls and the unified definition of the soul (nafs) that covers all three types, including the use of disjunction and the alternative using 'possessing life potentially'.

Core Idea
The soul is a first perfection of a natural organic body; its type is determined by its capacities.

In this passage, Sanusi defines the animal and human souls individually, then offers a unified definition that encompasses all three souls (vegetative, animal, human). He presents two approaches: a disjunctive definition and a single essential attribute definition. The passage also discusses how celestial souls are excluded and explains the purpose of key restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the individual definitions
Read the definitions of the animal soul and human soul separately. Note the restrictions (first perfection, natural, organic) and the specific capacities (sensing/voluntary motion vs. intellecting universals/deduction).
These definitions build on the earlier vegetative soul definition.
2
Examine the unified disjunctive definition
The text then gives a unified definition using disjunction (tardedid), covering all three soul types in one concise expression. Understand that this is three definitions in one, not a single definition.
The verification is that it is three definitions with concise expression.
3
Understand the alternative unified definition
A second unified approach uses the single attribute 'possessing life potentially' to cover all three. See how this works and how it excludes celestial souls and non-organic forms.
The restriction 'organic' excludes elements and minerals; 'potentially' excludes celestial souls.
4
Consider the discussion on celestial souls
The passage addresses celestial souls, noting that some hold each sphere is an instrument of the universal sphere, so they don't have separate souls. Also compare with Imam al-Razi's interpretation.
The phrase 'potentially' is key to excluding spheres on Sanusi's definition.

The second: the animal soul, and it is a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of what it senses and moves voluntarily. The third: the human soul, and it is a first perfection for a natural organic body that intellects universals and deduces by opinion. And the benefits of the restrictions in these two definitions have appeared from what has passed. This is if we define each of the three souls separately. And if we want to define the soul absolutely, i.e., so that it encompasses all that we have mentioned, we say: the soul is a first perfection for a natural organic body that nourishes and grows, or senses and moves voluntarily, or intellects universals and deduces by opinion. For this disjunction refers back to the divisions of the defined and encompasses them. And the verification is that it is, in meaning, three definitions for those divisions with conciseness of expression. And sometimes it is expressed about them, i.e., about the mentioned respects by way of disjunction, with a single necessary attribute that includes them, which is from the perspective that it is possessing life potentially. So it is said: the soul is a first perfection for a natural organic body possessing life potentially. The restriction 'organic' is a precaution against the forms of the elements and minerals, for although they are first perfections of natural bodies, they are not organic as has passed. And by it are also excluded the celestial souls according to the view of one who held that each sphere of the spheres is like instruments, meaning that each individual celestial sphere does not have a soul on its own. Rather, the rational soul belongs only to the universal spheres, since voluntary motion is part of the acts of life. And the sphere is excluded by his saying: 'potentially,' unlike the explanation of the Imam (may God have mercy on him). For the sphere on his explanation is excluded first by his saying: 'possessing life,' since growth and nutrition are not conceivable in the sphere. And his saying: the Imam interpreted the two mentioned meanings, or he interpreted the two mentioned sayings, and the result is one.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the animal and human souls and then provides a unified definition of the soul (nafs) that covers all three types. First, the animal soul is defined as a first perfection for a natural organic body in respect of sensing and voluntary movement. The human soul is defined as a first perfection for a natural organic body that intellects universals and deduces by opinion. The benefits of the restrictions are said to have already appeared. Then it offers a unified definition using a disjunction (tardedid): 'the soul is a first perfection for a natural organic body that nourishes and grows, or senses and moves voluntarily, or intellects universals and deduces by opinion.' This is actually three definitions in one concise expression. An alternative unified definition uses a common essential property: the soul is a first perfection for a natural organic body possessing life potentially (dhi hayaatin bil quwwa). The passage explains that the restriction 'organic' excludes elemental and mineral forms. It also discusses celestial souls: according to some, celestial spheres do not have separate souls because they are mere instruments of the universal spheres; the rational soul belongs only to universal spheres. The phrase 'potentially' excludes spheres because they do not have life potentially in the sense of requiring growth, etc. Unlike the interpretation of Imam al-Razi who first excludes spheres by 'possessing life' simpliciter. The passage concludes by noting that the Imam interpreted the two meanings or sayings, and the outcome is one.

The Soul's Names Based on Relation to Body
The author states that the soul detaches from the body and is then called intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same entity can have different names depending on its relation to other things, specifically the soul being called 'intellect' when separated from the body.

Core Idea
The soul is called 'soul' only when related to the body; detached, it is 'intellect'.

This lesson examines a subtle but important point about the soul's naming. The author clarifies that the term 'soul' is used only when the soul is connected to the body. Once that connection is severed, the proper name becomes 'intellect'. This distinction helps avoid confusion in discussions about the soul's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses the phrase 'التنبيه الثاني' to mark this as a second notice.
2
Identify the two states of the soul
The passage describes the soul in relation to the body and when detached. Find where the author speaks of the soul being 'مجردة' (separate) and when the name changes.
3
Understand the naming principle
The author concludes with a general principle: a thing can have a name based on its essence and another based on its relation. Reflect on why this matters for theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه ( الثاني : النفس في بعض الأشياء كالإنسان ( قد تتبرأ عن البدن ) بأن تكون مجردة غير حالة فيه (لكن لا يتناوله اسم النفس إلا باعتبار تعلقها به ) حتى إذا انقطع ذلك التعلق أو قطع النظر عنه لم يتناوله اسم النفس إلا باشتراك اللفظ بل الاسم الخاص بها حينئذ هو العقل، ( وقد يكون للشيء باعتبار ذاته وجوهره (اسم، وباعتبار تعلقه ) وإضافته إلى غيره (اسم
The second notice: The soul in some things, like the human, may detach from the body by being a separate substance not immanent in it. But the name 'soul' (nafs) does not apply to it except by virtue of its relation to it. So if that relation is severed or disregarded, the name 'soul' does not apply to it except by homonymy; rather, its specific name at that time is 'intellect' ('aql). And a thing may have a name by virtue of its essence and substance, and a name by virtue of its relation and addition to something else.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a notice (tanbih) about the soul in some things, such as the human. The soul may detach from the body and be a separate substance (mujarrada) not immanent (ghayr hālla) in it. However, the term 'soul' (nafs) does not apply to it except by virtue of its relation (ta'allaq) to the body. If that relation is cut off or not considered, the term 'soul' does not apply except homonymously (ishtirak lafzi); rather, its proper name at that point is 'intellect' ('aql). The passage also notes that a thing can have one name by virtue of its essence (dhat) and substance (jawhar) and another name by virtue of its relation (ta'allaq) and addition (idafa) to something else.

Why the Soul Definition Fails for Celestial Souls
Showing why the standard definition does not include celestial souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the standard definitions of the soul cannot be applied to celestial souls, and why no single definition can cover all three types of souls (vegetative, animal, celestial).

Core Idea
No single definition can cover vegetative, animal, and celestial souls.

This passage examines the widely accepted definition of the soul and shows its limitations when applied to celestial souls. The author demonstrates that every proposed definition either fails to include celestial souls or accidentally includes non-souls. This forces us to reconsider what the soul truly is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the two conditions of the standard definition: 'using instruments' and 'possessing life potentially'.
2
Identify why celestial souls fail the standard definition
Notice that if celestial souls act without instruments, they fail the 'using instruments' condition; if they act with instruments, they fail the 'possessing life potentially' condition because they are always actual.
3
Examine the alternative definition
The alternative definition based on differing actions also fails because celestial souls have uniform actions. Observed differences are due to composition, not the soul itself.
4
Consider the attempts at a universal definition
The passage tests two simple definitions: 'principle of actions' (too broad, includes minerals) and 'principle of actions with intention' (excludes vegetative soul). Neither works.
5
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that no correct definition (rasm) can encompass all three types of souls. This highlights the difficulty of defining the soul universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه ( الثالث : هذا الحد ) الذي ذكروه للنفس على الإطلاق ( لا يتناول النفوس الفلكية لأن أفعالها إن لم تكن بالآلات كما المشهور فقد خرجت عن التعريف بقيد الآلي، وإن كانت بالآلات كما ذهب إليه جمع فقد خرجت عنه بقيد ذي حياة بالقوة على ما مر . وكذا لا يتناولها الحد المستفاد مما ذكرناه في التنبيه الأول ) لما عرفت أنا أعطيناها اسم النفس من حيث ( يختلف أفعالها والنفوس ( الفلكية ليست كذلك فإن أفعالها غير مختلفة بل هي على نهج واحد، والاختلافات المشاهدة فيها مستندة إلى تركب حركات كل واحدة منها على وتيرة واحدة ) ولا تعلم رسما يتناولها ) أي ويتناول النفوس الثلاث معا عن النباتية والحيوانية والفلكية . ( فإنا لو قلنا : ( النفس ما يكون ( مبدأ للأفعال ) أي ما يصدر عنه فعل ( كان كل قوة كالطبيعة العنصرية والصورة المعدنية (نفساً. ولو شرطنا ) مع صدور الفعل ( القصد خرجت ) النفس ( النباتية ) ، والحاصل أن الاكتفاء بصدور الفعل يبطل طرد الحد واعتبار اختلاف الأفعال يخرج النفوس الفلكية. واعتبار القصد يخرج النباتية فلم يتحقق عندنا رسم صحيح يتناول النفوس الثلاث. قوله : ( وكذا لا يتناولها أي لا يتناول النفوس الفلكية الحد المستفاد إلخ. وذلك الحد . وهو ما ذكره بقوله : ما يصدر عنها آثار لا على نهج واحد .
The third notice: This definition which they have given for the soul absolutely does not encompass the celestial souls, because if their actions are not by means of instruments (as is well-known), then they have left the definition by the condition 'using instruments'. And if they are by means of instruments (as a group held), then they have left the definition by the condition 'possessing life potentially' (dhi hayatin bil-quwwa), as previously stated. Likewise, it does not encompass them the definition derived from what we mentioned in the first notice, because you know that we gave the name 'soul' to them insofar as their actions differ, but the celestial souls are not like that; their actions are not different but are uniform, and the observed differences in them are due to the composition of the motions of each one of them on a single pattern. And we do not know a definition (rasm) that encompasses them, i.e., encompasses the three souls together: vegetative, animal, and celestial. For if we say: 'The soul is that which is a principle of actions', i.e., that from which an act proceeds, then every power like elemental nature and mineral form would be a soul. And if we add the condition of intention (qasd) along with the issuance of action, then the vegetative soul is excluded. The upshot is that being satisfied with the issuance of action invalidates the comprehensiveness of the definition, and considering differences in actions excludes the celestial souls, and considering intention excludes the vegetative soul. Therefore, we have not realized a correct definition (rasm) that encompasses the three souls. His saying: (And likewise it does not encompass them) meaning the definition derived from what we mentioned in the first notice does not encompass the celestial souls. And that definition is what he mentioned in his saying: 'that from which effects issue not on a uniform pattern.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a notice (tanbih) explaining why the commonly given definition of the soul does not encompass celestial souls (al-nufus al-falakiyya). The definition includes the condition of being a perfection for a natural body possessing life potentially (hayat bil-quwwa) and using instruments (alati). Celestial souls either do not use instruments (if they act directly, as is well-known) — then they are excluded by the condition 'using instruments', or if they do use instruments (as some hold), then they are not 'possessing life potentially' because they are actual. Furthermore, the alternative definition derived from the first notice (actions differing from uniform pattern) also fails because celestial souls have uniform actions; observed differences come from the composition of movements. The passage then experiments with possible definitions: if we say the soul is a principle of actions, then any power (like elemental nature or mineral form) would be a soul. If we add 'with intention' (qasd), then the vegetative soul is excluded. Thus, no correct definition (rasm) can encompass all three types (vegetative, animal, celestial). The commentator clarifies that the 'definition from the first notice' means 'that from which effects issue not on a uniform pattern.'

Ibn Sina's Definition of the Soul and Its Universal Application
Ibn Sina's definition of soul and its application to all souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of the soul as he presented it in al-Shifa', and how it applies to both earthly and celestial souls despite the term being used homonymously.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's soul definition is inclusive: it covers all souls by focusing on actions devoid of will, not uniform pattern.

This lesson explores a key passage from al-Shifa' where Ibn Sina defines the soul. The author concludes that the term 'soul' is used homonymously for earthly and celestial souls, yet Ibn Sina provides a definition that encompasses both. We'll unpack the definition and the grammatical clarification that ensures celestial souls are included.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the homonymous usage
Understand that the term 'soul' (nafs) is applied to different types of souls only by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). This means there is no single essence shared across all souls, but the term is used for different realities.
This is crucial for avoiding confusion about the unity of the concept of soul.
2
Read Ibn Sina's definition
Read the Arabic definition from al-Shifa': 'everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will'. Consider what this definition includes.
Notice the two conditions: actions not uniform AND devoid of will.
3
Analyze the pronoun reference
The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'devoid of will' (adimat al-irada) refers to the pattern (watira), not the actions. This is to include celestial souls whose actions are uniform but devoid of will.
If it referred to actions, celestial souls would be excluded because their actions are uniform.
4
Understand the inclusive effect
By referring to the pattern, the definition covers any soul that is a principle of actions that either are not uniform or, if uniform, lack will. Thus all souls (earthly and celestial) are included.
This shows the sophistication of Ibn Sina's definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإطلاق النفس على النفوس الأرضية والسماوية ليس إلا بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي هذا. وقد صرح ابن سينا في الشفاء بأن كل ما يكون مبدأ لصدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة عادمة للإرادة فإنا نسميه نفسا وهذا المعنى مشترك بين النفوس كلها لأن قوله : ( عادمة للإرادة) الظاهر أن قوله : عادمة مجرور، وأن الضمير المستتر فيه راجع إلى الوتيرة لا إلى الأفاعيل كما يوهمه ظاهر عبارته في حاشية شرح التجريد حيث قال : أو يكون مبدأ الأفاعيل تكون على وتيرة واحدة لكن لا يكون عادمة للإرادة وإنما قلنا : لا إلى الأفاعيل لأنه حينئذ يلزم خروج النفوس الفلكية عن هذا الرسم، وعلى تقدير رجوع الضمير إلى الوتيرة كما هو الظاهر. يكون إسناد عادمة إلى الضمير فيه إسناداً مجازيا ويكون المقصود ونفي المجموع. ثم إن
Therefore, the application of the term 'soul' (nafs) to earthly and celestial souls is only according to homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). And Ibn Sina has explicitly stated in al-Shifa' that everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will (al-irada), then we call it a soul. And this meaning is common to all souls, because – His saying: (devoid of will) – it is apparent that his saying 'devoid of will' (adimat al-irada) is in the genitive case, and that the implicit pronoun in it refers to the pattern (al-watira), not to the actions (al-afa'il), as is suggested by the apparent meaning of his expression in the gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid, where he said: 'Or it is a principle of actions that are on a uniform pattern but not devoid of will.' And we only said: not to the actions, because then it would necessitate the exclusion of celestial souls from this definition. And on the assumption that the pronoun refers to the pattern, as is apparent, the attribution of 'devoid' to the pronoun is a metaphorical attribution, and the intended meaning is the negation of the combination. Then,
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage concludes that the term 'soul' (nafs) is applied to earthly and celestial souls only by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). It then cites Ibn Sina in al-Shifa', who defines soul as 'everything that is a principle for the issuance of actions that are not on a uniform pattern and are devoid of will (al-irada)'. This meaning is common to all souls. The commentator then explains the phrase 'devoid of will' ('adimat al-irada'), clarifying that the pronoun in 'devoid' refers to the 'pattern' (watira) not to the 'actions' (afa'il), because returning it to actions would exclude celestial souls (since their actions are uniform but they lack will? Actually, the commentator argues that if the pronoun referred to actions, then the definition would require actions not uniform and devoid of will, but celestial souls have uniform actions but are devoid of will, so they would be excluded. Instead, the intended meaning is the negation of the combination of uniformity and will: i.e., actions are either not on a uniform pattern or, if uniform, then devoid of will. Thus celestial souls, being devoid of will, are included. The commentator notes that this interpretation is a metaphorical attribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definition excludes celestial souls because their actions are uniform.
Correction: The definition includes celestial souls because 'devoid of will' refers to the pattern, not the actions; so actions that are uniform but devoid of will still qualify.

D) The instrument (al-ala)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of the soul, when correctly understood, applies to both earthly and celestial souls by focusing on the absence of will rather than the uniformity of actions.

Ibn Sina says that souls are classed by whether their actions involve will. When you act, is your will submitting to your intellect or is your intellect enslaved to your will? Which of these describes the state of your soul right now?
?
This lesson clarifies that celestial souls lack will. If you were stripped of your will, what would remain of your actions? Would your soul still be a principle of good actions, or would it be empty? Let this question drive you to purify your intentions.

Cessation of the Nutritive Power with Death
Commentary on the cessation of the nutritive power with death and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument for the cessation of the nutritive power with death and its basis in the corruption of temperament.

Core Idea
The nutritive power ceases with death because it requires a balanced temperament.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains why the nutritive power stops functioning at death. It argues that the cessation is necessary due to the corruption of the temperament, which undermines the bodily powers. The author provides a simile of a lamp to clarify how innate heat is extinguished.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: the passage begins with a mention of spoiling by disease, then makes the main claim, gives a reason, and ends with an explanatory simile.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ضرورة الموت' which indicates necessity.
2
Identify the two causes mentioned
The passage gives two interconnected causes for the cessation: the corruption of the temperament (فساد المزاج) and the finitude of bodily powers. Understand that these are not separate but related.
The variant reading emphasizes 'ضرورة الموت' as a proof.
3
Understand the role of innate moisture and heat
The author explains that the innate moisture decreases because of the cooperation of internal and external heat and movements. This leads to the extinguishing of innate heat.
Innate moisture is like oil; innate heat is like the flame.
4
Reflect on the lamp simile
The simile of the lamp (انطفاء المصباح) illustrates how the loss of innate moisture and the predominance of foreign moisture cause death, just as a lamp goes out when oil is gone and water prevails.
This simile makes the abstract process tangible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوامه ولونه وقد يخل به كما في البرص والبهق ( وقد يثبت وقوفها أي وقوف الغاذية عن فعلها ( ضرورة الموت)، وحينئذ لفساد المزاج ( بأن القوى الجسمانية متناهية ) في آثارها ( كما تقدم ) . وفي بعض النسخ وقد يثبت وقوفها بضرورة الموت وبأن القوى الجسمانية يعني أن ضرورة الموت تدل على وقوفها أيضاً. وإنما كان ضرورياً لأن الرطوبة الغريزية تنتقص بعد بنفس الوقوف وذلك أن الحرارة الغريزية والحرارة الخارجة والحركات النفسانية والبدنية تتعاضد في تحليلها حتى تنحل بالكلية فتغلب اليبوسة والرطوبة الغريبة وتنطفئ الحرارة الغريزية كانطفاء المصباح عند انتفاء الدهن وغلبة الماء ويحل الموت
Its consistency and color, and it may spoil it as in leprosy and vitiligo. And it may be established its stopping, i.e., the stopping of the nutritive power from its action, necessarily with death, and then due to corruption of the temperament, because the bodily powers are finite in their effects, as previously. And in some copies: 'And it may be established its stopping by the necessity of death and because the bodily powers' meaning that the necessity of death indicates its stopping also. And it was necessary because the innate moisture decreases after the stopping itself, and that is because the innate heat, the external heat, and the psychic and bodily movements cooperate in analyzing it until it is completely dissolved, then dryness and foreign moisture prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished like the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is gone and water prevails, and death occurs.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the cessation of the nutritive (غاذية) power with death. It begins by noting that certain bodily features like consistency and color can be spoiled by diseases such as leprosy and vitiligo, which indicates the role of the nutritive power. The main argument is that the nutritive power necessarily stops with death. The proof is that death results from the corruption of the temperament, and the bodily powers are finite in their effects. The author mentions a variant reading that emphasizes the necessity of death indicating its stoppage. Then he explains why it is necessary: the innate moisture (رطوبة غريزية) decreases after the stopping itself, because the innate heat (حرارة غريزية), external heat, and psychic and bodily movements all cooperate in analyzing the moisture until it is completely dissolved. Dryness and foreign moisture then prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished, just as a lamp goes out when its oil is exhausted and water overcomes. Thus death occurs, and with it the nutritive power ceases.

Key Terms
الغاذية
القوى الجسمانية
الرطوبة الغريزية
الحرارة الغريزية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the cessation of the nutritive power is merely a consequence of death rather than a necessary simultaneous occurrence.
Correction: The passage argues that the nutritive power stops necessarily with death due to the corruption of the temperament and the finitude of bodily powers. It is not a separate consequence but an integral part of the death process.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason for the cessation of the nutritive power with death?
A) The finitude of bodily powers
B) The corruption of the temperament
C) The exhaustion of innate moisture
D) The extinguishing of innate heat

Key Takeaway
The nutritive power ceases with death necessarily because the corruption of the temperament ends the balanced conditions required for its function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your bodily powers are finite and cease at death. What are you investing your energy in that will outlast this body? Name one concrete change you need to make to shift from temporal nourishment to eternal nourishment.
?
This lesson says death is a necessary corruption of temperament. What 'corruptions' in your spiritual temperament—such as arrogance, negligence, or love of dunya—are slowly extinguishing your spiritual life? What is one step you will take this week to correct that imbalance?

The Cessation of the Growth Power: Its Proof and Implications
Definition, distinctions, proof of cessation, and aftermath of the growth power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why and how the growth power ceases, based on the natural drying of the body, and the distinction between normal growth, tumors, and obesity.

Core Idea
Growth stops when the body dries completely, preventing the expansion needed for nutrient absorption.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif explains the mechanism behind the cessation of the growth power. The author defines the growth power, distinguishes it from abnormal conditions like tumors and obesity, and provides a physiological proof based on the body's natural drying. He also raises a subtle question about whether the power becomes completely null or persists in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the growth power
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to understand how the growth power is defined: it interlaces food among parts and increases dimensions naturally.
Note that the definition ties growth to a natural proportion specific to each person.
2
Identify the distinctions from tumor and obesity
The passage contrasts natural growth with tumors (unnatural proportion) and obesity (post-growth, limited to two dimensions and flesh). Understand why these are not considered growth.
This clarifies what is not meant by the growth power.
3
Trace the proof of cessation via drying
Follow the argument: the body starts moist, allowing easy nutrient penetration. As it dries gradually, penetration becomes difficult. Since penetration requires organ expansion, when organs are completely dry, expansion is impossible, so growth stops necessarily.
The proof is a causal chain from moisture to expansion to growth.
4
Consider the hesitation and relation to nutritive power
The passage ends with a hesitation: does the growth power become null or remain in essence? Also, note how the nutritive power (غاذية) ceases to provide material for growth when food only replaces what is lost.
This shows the complexity of the topic and the limits of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والنامية) التي لا بد منها في وصول الشخص إلى كماله ) تداخل الغذاء بين الأجزاء فتضمه إليها فتزيد في الأقطار الثلاثة بنسبة طبيعية ) أي تزيد في تلك الأقطار بنسبة تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص الذي له تلك القوة ( إلى غاية ما هي غاية النشو في ذلك الشخص ( ثم تقف ) عن فعلها ) لا كالورم فإنه ليس على النسبة الطبيعية بل خارج عن المجرى الطبيعي. ( والسمن) فإنه قد يكون بعد حال النشو أيضاً كالورم وقد مر ما قيل من أن السمن لا يكون إلا في قطرين ومن أنه مخصوص باللحم وما في حكمه دون الأعضاء الأصلية كالعظم ونظائره ( وذلك ) أي بيان وقوف النامية أنه لما كان البدن متولداً من الدم والمني ( فهو في الأول رطب ) في الغاية فيتأتى حينئذ نفوذ الغذاء بين أجزائه بسهولة (ثم يجف يسيراً يسيراً) ويتعسر النفوذ قليلاً ( ونفوذ الغذاء لا يكون إلا بتمدد الأعضاء فإذا جفت ) الأعضاء جفافاً كاملاً ( لم تقبل ذلك ) التمدد فلم يتصور نفوذ الغذاء فيها ( فوقفت ( النامية عن فعلها ( ضرورة ) وهل تبطل حينئذ بالكلية أو تبقى ذاتها فيه تردد والغاذية عدم النامية بتحصيل ما يتعلق به فعلها وهو ما زاد من على بدل مايتحلل فإذا ساواه الغذاء أو نقص عنه فات محل فعل النامية .
And the growth power, which is necessary for the person to reach his perfection. It interlocks the food among the parts, then attaches it to them, and increases in the three dimensions in a natural proportion, i.e., it increases in those dimensions in a proportion required by the nature of that person who has that power, up to the limit which is the limit of growth in that person, then it stops from its action – not like a tumor, for it is not upon the natural proportion but outside the natural course. And obesity, for it may occur after the state of growth also like a tumor, and what was said that obesity only occurs in two dimensions, and that it is specific to flesh and what is in its ruling, not the original organs like bone and its parallels. And that, i.e., the explanation of the cessation of the growth power: since the body is generated from blood and semen, it is in the beginning extremely moist, so then the penetration of food among its parts occurs easily. Then it dries little by little, and the penetration becomes slightly difficult. The penetration of food only occurs by the expansion of the organs. So when the organs dry completely, they do not accept that expansion, so the penetration of food in them is not conceivable, and the growth power stops necessarily. And whether it then becomes null completely or its essence remains is a matter of hesitation. And the nutritive power is the absence of the growth power by acquiring what its action attaches to, which is what is added over the replacement of what dissolves. So if the food equals it or falls short, the locus of the growth power's action is lost.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the cessation of the growth (نامية) power. The author defines the growth power as that which allows a person to reach his full development. It interlaces food among the body's parts and increases them in three dimensions naturally, until the person reaches his full growth, then it stops. The author distinguishes this from a tumor, which is unnatural, and from obesity, which can occur after growth and is limited to two dimensions and to flesh. The proof of its cessation is that the body is initially very moist (from blood and semen), allowing easy nutrient penetration. As the body dries, penetration becomes harder. Since nutrient penetration requires organ expansion, when the organs become completely dry, they cannot expand, and growth stops. The author then raises a question: does the growth power become completely null at this point, or does its essence remain? This is left as a matter of hesitation (تردد). He further explains the relationship between the nutritive (غاذية) and growth powers: the nutritive power ceases to provide the material for growth, so when the food only replaces what is dissolved or less, the growth power has no locus to act.

Key Terms
النامية
الغاذية
الأقطار الثلاثة
الرطوبة
التمدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the growth power stops because of old age rather than because of the complete drying of the organs preventing expansion.
Correction: The passage explains that cessation is due to the physical drying and loss of ability to expand, not merely the passage of time.

Checkpoint
What causes the growth power to cease according to the passage?
A) The body reaches a certain age
B) The organs dry completely and cannot expand
C) The food supply diminishes
D) The nutritive power stops completely

Key Takeaway
The growth power ceases necessarily when the body dries completely, but whether it becomes null or remains in essence is debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body has stopped growing physically. Are you content with the limits Allah has placed on your physical form, or do you secretly wish you were different? What does this lesson's emphasis on a 'natural proportion' teach you about accepting His decree for your life?
?
The growth power stops necessarily when the body dries and cannot expand. In your spiritual life, have you allowed your heart to become dry—unwilling to expand in obedience or love for Allah? What would it take for you to become 'moist' again, soft and receptive to growth?
?
The author hesitates whether the growth power becomes null or remains in essence after cessation. Consider a good deed or virtue you once practiced but have now stopped. Have you abandoned it completely, or does its capacity remain within you, waiting to be activated again? What will you do this week to revive that capacity?

Generative and Formative Powers for Species Preservation
Description of generative and formative powers for species preservation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the roles of the generative and formative powers in species preservation and the differences between the views of Hippocrates and Aristotle regarding their nature.

Core Idea
Two powers preserve species by shaping and generating from food.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the biological and philosophical underpinnings of how species continue through individuals. It focuses on two of the four served powers—the generative and formative—and explains their functions, with insights from Hippocrates and Aristotle. Understanding these powers helps appreciate the Islamic philosophical synthesis of medicine and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the technical terms and the two different opinions of Hippocrates and Aristotle.
The passage also references the Kulliyat al-Qanun, a famous medical text.
2
Identify the two powers and their functions
Find where the generative and formative powers are defined. What does each do? The generative separates material from food; the formative gives forms and shapes in the womb.
3
Compare the views of Hippocrates and Aristotle
Note the difference: Hippocrates says the generative power is in all organs, so semen is diverse in reality; Aristotle says it does not separate from the other powers, so semen is similar in reality.
This difference impacts how one understands the nature of semen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنها ) أي من الأربع المخدومة ) اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء النوع ) فقط مع كون بقائه محتاجاً إلى الأولين أيضاً بتوسط الشخص. ( وهما المولدة والمصورة فالمولدة تفصل من الغذاء) بعد الهضم الأخير. (ما يصلح أن يكون مادة للمثل) أي لمثل ذلك الشخص الذي فصلت منه البذر ) وهي في كل البدن كما ذهب إليه بقراط وأتباعه فإن المني عندهم يخرج من جميع الأعضاء فيخرج من العظم مثله ومن اللحم مثله . وعلى هذا فالمني متخالف الحقيقة متشابه الامتزاج لأن الحس لا يميز بين الأجزاء. وعند أرسطو أن تلك القوة لا تفارق الاثنتين فيكون المني المتولد هناك متشابه الحقيقة وفي كليات القانون أن المولدة نوعان نوع يولد المني في الذكر والأنثى . ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المني أي الكيفيات المزاجية لأن أجزاءه متخالفة الأمزجة فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو فيخص للعصب مزاجاً خاصاً وكذا للعظم والشريان وغيرهما وذلك من مني متشابه الأجزاء أو متشابه الامتزاج ) والمصورة وهي توجد في المني عند كونه ( في الرحم خاصة تفيد تلك الأجزاء ) أي الأجزاء المتخالفة الحقيقة أو الاستعداد التي في المني ( الصور والقوى والأشكال والمقادير ( التي بها تصير مثلاً بالفعل بعد ما كانت مثلاً بالقوة وهاتان القوتان أعني المولدة والمصورة تخدمهما الغاذية وهو ظاهر، والنامية أيضا وذلك بأن تعظم الأعضاء وتوسع مجاريها حتى تصير إلى الهيئة الصالحة للتوليد. ولذلك لا يتكون المني إلا بعد عظم الأعضاء
And from them, i.e., from the four served powers, two are needed for the preservation of the species only, while its preservation also needs the first two through the intermediary of the individual. They are the generative and the formative. The generative separates from the food after the final digestion what is suitable to be material for the like, i.e., for the like of that person from whom the seed is separated. And it is in the whole body, as Hippocrates and his followers held, for semen in their view comes out from all organs: from bone its like, from flesh its like. And on this basis, semen is diverse in reality but similar in mixture because the senses do not distinguish between the parts. According to Aristotle, that power does not separate from the two, so the semen generated there is similar in reality. And in the Kulliyat al-Qanun, the generative is of two types: a type that generates semen in male and female, and a type that separates the powers that are in the semen, i.e., the temperamental qualities, because its parts are diverse in temperaments, so it mixes them in mixtures according to each organ, and allocates to the nerve a specific temperament, and likewise to bone, artery, and others, and that from semen similar in parts or similar in mixture. And the formative, which exists in the semen when it is in the womb specifically, gives to those parts, i.e., the parts diverse in reality or disposition that are in the semen, the forms, powers, shapes, and measurements by which they become an actual likeness after they were a potential likeness. And these two powers, I mean the generative and the formative, are served by the nutritive, which is evident, and also by the growth, by making the organs grow and expanding their channels until they reach the suitable state for generation. Therefore, semen is not produced except after the growth of the organs.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses two powers among the four served powers: the generative (مولدة) and the formative (مصورة), which are needed for the preservation of the species, though the first two powers (nutritive and growth) are also necessary via the individual. The generative power separates from the food, after final digestion, the material suitable to become seed for a similar individual. The author presents two medical views: Hippocrates believed the generative power exists in all organs, so semen comes from every part of the body; thus semen is diverse in reality (متخالف الحقيقة) but similar in mixture because the senses cannot distinguish the parts. Aristotle held that this power does not separate from the two (probably the nutritive and growth), so the semen is similar in reality. Then the author references the Kulliyat al-Qanun, which divides the generative into two types: one that generates the semen itself in male and female, and another that separates the powers (i.e., temperamental qualities) within the semen, because the semen's parts have diverse temperaments, and it mixes them appropriately for each organ. The formative power, present in the semen specifically in the womb, gives those parts their forms, powers, shapes, and measurements, actualizing them from potentiality to actuality. Both the generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive (obviously) and by the growth power, which enlarges organs and expands channels until they are fit for generation. Hence, semen is not produced until after the organs have grown.

Key Terms
المولدة
المصورة
المني
بقراط
أرسطو
كليات القانون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the generative and formative powers are independent of the nutritive and growth powers.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive and growth powers; semen does not form until the organs have fully grown.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the view of Hippocrates and Aristotle regarding the generative power?
A) Hippocrates said it is in all organs; Aristotle said it does not separate from the two
B) Hippocrates said it is only in the womb; Aristotle said it is in all organs
C) Hippocrates said semen is similar in reality; Aristotle said it is diverse
D) Hippocrates said the generative power has two types; Aristotle said one type

Key Takeaway
The generative and formative powers work together to produce offspring, drawing on materials processed by the nutritive and growth powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passages describes how your physical existence—every cell, every organ—depends on a coordinated sequence of powers working together. If you were to trace the creativity behind this design back to its Origin, what would you say to Him right now? Do not just think: take a moment to actually express gratitude for the miracle of your own formation, and commit to not taking your body for granted.
?
The text shows that even great physicians like Hippocrates and Aristotle differed on the nature of semen. In matters of your own spiritual formation—like how your heart is shaped by faith—do you actively seek knowledge from the Qur'an and Sunnah, or do you rely on assumptions and hearsay? Identify one belief you hold about Allah's creation that you have not verified from revelation, and make a plan to verify it.
?
The generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive and growth powers; nothing in your physical creation operates alone. What about your spiritual life? Are you feeding your soul with the 'nutritive' of prayer and remembrance so that spiritual fruits (good deeds) may be generated? List one spiritual practice you will increase this week to ensure your soul grows and becomes ready to produce lasting good.

Three Proofs for the Attractive Power in the Stomach
The author presents three proofs for the attractive power in the stomach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three empirical proofs from involuntary swallowing, vomiting patterns, and animal behavior that demonstrate the stomach possesses an attractive power.

Core Idea
The stomach attracts food even against one's will, proving a built-in attractive power.

Before diving into the proofs, note that they rely on common experiences: food sometimes goes down involuntarily when hungry, sweet substances linger in vomit, and some animals' stomachs actively reach for food. The author uses these to argue that a pulling force must exist in the stomach.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the first proof about food moving involuntarily from mouth to stomach when the need is severe. Notice how the author argues that this must be forced, and since it's not a push, it must be a pull from the stomach.
Focus on the logical structure: forced movement requires a force, and the alternatives are push or pull.
2
Examine the second proof
Read the second proof about sweet and bitter substances. The sweet is last to come out in vomit, indicating the stomach attracts it; bitter substances are expelled reluctantly. What does this suggest about the stomach's preferences?
This proof uses the stomach's 'natural love' for sweetness.
3
Review the third proof
Read the third proof about the crocodile's stomach rising to meet food. This active movement shows a desire to attract food.
This proof extends to animals with short esophagi, making the attraction more visible.
