⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه فيغلبه الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال: الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني: أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس ذلك إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعاً. وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدرردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره. الوجه الثالث: قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع: الرحم بعد انقطاع الطمث عن قريب إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيواناً مشتاقاً للمني. فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم. الوجه الخامس: الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ثم تتمايز تلك الأمور المختلطة وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة اللائقة به لامتنع ذلك التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء.
And [the first proof is] from the one who is nourished: food may be turned from the mouth to the stomach at the time of severe need for it without the will of the one nourished, rather the human may intend to prevent it to chew it, but the food overcomes him and is drawn inside. So it must be forced, and there must be a force. It is either a push from above by saying the animal pushes it voluntarily—and its falsity has become clear—or a pull from below, which is that the stomach pulls it by an attractive power in it, and that is what is desired. The second proof: when a human nourishes himself with food, then takes something sweet afterwards and induces vomiting, he finds the last thing that comes out with the vomit to be sweet, and that is only because the stomach pulls it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And if a human takes a bitter, unpleasant medicine, the esophagus and stomach try to expel and eject it and only swallow it with difficulty, so perhaps it is expelled by vomiting involuntarily. The third proof: the stomach may rise to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, so that it comes out at the time of feeding until it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract the food. So these three proofs indicate that the stomach possesses an attractive power. The fourth proof: the uterus, soon after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free of residues, its desire for semen intensifies so that it is felt pulling the urethra into its interior like a cupping glass pulls blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth proof: the blood that is in the liver is mixed with the three residues, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile; then those mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable to it. So were there not in each organ an attractive power for that moisture suitable to it, that differentiation and the pouring of each moisture into its specific organ constantly or for the most part would be impossible. And this is a clear proof for the existence of the attractive power in all the organs.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (القوة الجاذبة) in the body. The author first gives three proofs specifically for the stomach: (1) food moves involuntarily from mouth to stomach when needed, requiring a pulling force from below; (2) when vomiting after eating something sweet, the sweet substance is the last to come out, indicating the stomach attracts it due to natural affinity, while bitter substances are expelled; (3) in animals with short esophagi like the crocodile, the stomach rises to meet the food, showing desire to attract. The fourth proof is for the uterus: after menstruation, it attracts semen like a cupping glass, demonstrating an attractive power. The fifth proof is general: blood mixed with residues differentiates in the liver, and each organ attracts its suitable humor; otherwise this constant separation would be impossible. The passage concludes that these proofs clearly establish the existence of the attractive power in all organs.

Key Terms
قوة جاذبة
جذب
معدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Swallowing is always a voluntary act.
Correction: The first proof shows that in times of intense hunger, food can be drawn into the stomach involuntarily, implying an attractive force in the stomach.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof about sweet substances demonstrate?
A) The stomach pushes sweet substances out last.
B) The stomach attracts sweet substances due to natural love.
C) Sweet substances are not affected by the stomach.
D) The stomach has no preference for sweet or bitter.

Key Takeaway
Three empirical observations—involuntary ingestion, selective retention of sweet, and active reaching in crocodiles—prove the stomach has an attractive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that your stomach pulls food even when you try to prevent it. Reflect on a time you neglected your body's need for proper nutrition—perhaps by skipping meals or eating poorly. How does the stomach's relentless yet designed drive to nourish you make you feel about the One who built that drive into your very nature? Will you from now on honor that design by choosing foods that sustain you well?
?
The stomach has a natural love for sweet things, and even in vomiting, it clings to what is sweet. What are the 'sweet' desires in your own soul that you cling to, maybe even to your own harm? Are you willing to submit those desires to the wisdom of your Creator, trusting that His guidance is better for you than your natural inclinations?
?
Consider the crocodile's stomach rising to meet its food—an almost desperate desire for sustenance. Your own soul has a built-in longing for something beyond itself. What are you truly hungry for? Are you feeding that hunger with the eternal, nourishing words of your Lord, or with temporary distractions that leave you empty? What will you change today to satisfy your deepest need?

Proof for the Attractive Power in the Uterus
The author gives a single proof for the attractive power in the uterus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the behavior of the uterus during menstruation and its attraction for semen serves as a proof for the existence of an attractive power in the uterus.

Core Idea
The uterus actively attracts semen like a cupping glass draws blood, proving its attractive power.

The fourth proof shifts focus to the uterus, which after menstruation becomes empty and intensely desires semen. The author compares this to a cupping glass that pulls blood, suggesting a similar attractive force at work.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the description of the uterus after menstruation. Notice the language: 'its desire for semen intensifies' and it 'pulls the urethra into its interior.'
The author uses a medical analogy with cupping to explain the attraction.
2
Understand the analogy
Compare the uterus's action to that of a cupping glass. Just as the glass creates a vacuum to draw blood, the uterus actively draws semen inward. This implies an attractive power.
The phrase 'some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen' emphasizes its active role.
3
Consider the significance
Ask yourself: why does this prove an attractive power? Because the movement is not voluntary but instinctual, and it requires a force. The author concludes that this proof establishes the attractive power in the uterus.
This proof is gender-specific but logically parallel to the stomach proofs.

What does the fourth proof compare the uterus's action to?
A) A magnet attracting iron.

?

?

?
The proof shows that even the most intimate bodily functions are meticulously designed. Are you living as if your body is a trust from Allah, or do you misuse it? What one change can you make today to honor that trust?

Proof for the Attractive Power in All Organs
The author presents a general proof for the attractive power in all organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differentiation of blood components in the liver and their distribution to specific organs proves that each organ has an attractive power for its suitable humor.

Core Idea
Each organ must attract its specific fluid from mixed blood, otherwise separation would be impossible.

The fifth and final proof is the most general, covering all organs. It observes that blood in the liver is mixed with phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile, yet each organ receives its own specific fluid. This cannot happen without an attractive power in each organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth proof
Read how blood mixed with three residues in the liver becomes differentiated, and each organ receives a fluid suitable to it. Notice the logical conclusion: without an attractive power, this would be impossible.
The proof is based on the constant and consistent distribution over time.
2
Understand the implications
If there were no attractive power, the fluids would stay mixed randomly. The fact that each organ consistently gets its appropriate humor shows there must be a selective pulling force.
This proof is similar to final cause arguments: the regular outcome implies a cause.
3
Appreciate the generality
Unlike previous proofs focusing on specific organs, this proof applies to all organs, establishing the attractive power as a general physiological principle.
This is considered a 'clear proof' by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه فيغلبه الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال: الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني: أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس ذلك إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعاً. وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدرردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره. الوجه الثالث: قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع: الرحم بعد انقطاع الطمث عن قريب إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيواناً مشتاقاً للمني. فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم. الوجه الخامس: الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ثم تتمايز تلك الأمور المختلطة وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة اللائقة به لامتنع ذلك التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof cite as evidence for the attractive power in each organ?
A) The liver separates blood into components.
B) Each organ consistently receives its suitable moisture from mixed blood.
C) The stomach attracts food.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body separates nutrients from waste every time you eat, without any conscious effort. Does this automatic precision make you feel more self-reliant or more dependent on the One who designed it? How would your attitude toward health change if you saw each organ's attraction as a direct act of divine sustenance?
?
The proof says each organ 'constantly or for the most part' receives its suitable moisture. When was the last time you thanked Allah for the reliability of your bodily functions? What would a day be like if just one organ failed to attract what it needs—and how does that thought make you feel about your current state of health?
?
You have likely taken your body's coordination for granted. If you truly believed that every organ has a God-given attractive power, how would that affect your reverence for your own body and your responsibility to care for it—starting from this very moment?

Definition of the Digestive Power and Its Distinction from the Nutritive Power
The author defines the digestive power and explains its role in preparing food for nutrition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the digestive power prepares food for nutrition and how it differs from the nutritive power.

Core Idea
The digestive power prepares; the nutritive power actualizes.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies the role of the digestive power as one of the four serving powers. The author distinguishes it clearly from the nutritive power by analyzing the two stages of food transformation. Read on to grasp this essential distinction in Islamic medicine and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation. Let the author's flow come through before you analyze.
Notice the technical terms: هاضمة (digestive), غاذية (nutritive).
2
Identify the definitions
Find the definitions of the digestive and nutritive powers. The digestive prepares food until it becomes a part in actuality; the nutritive requires foodstuffs to become a part in actuality.
3
Note the two states described
The author describes two states: (1) increase of readiness for the organic form (digestion), and (2) actual obtaining of the organic form (nutrition).
This is the core distinction.
4
Understand the role of 'cooking' (طبخ)
The transformation occurs through a cooking process that decreases readiness for the blood form and increases readiness for the organic form.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself why distinguishing these powers matters for the overall understanding of bodily functions and the hierarchy of powers.
This connects to the broader cosmological framework.

الثانية من الأربع الخادمة الهاضمة وهي تعد الغذاء إلى أن يصير جزءاً بالفعل من العضو فهي غير الغاذية أعني القوة التي تقتضي صيرورة الأغذية جزءاً بالفعل. وفي كليات القانون: وأما الهاضمة فهي التي تحيل ما جذبته الجاذبة وأمسكته الماسكة إلى قوام مهيئاً لفعل القوة المغيرة فيه وإلى مزاج صالح للاستحالة إلى الغذائية بالفعل. قال الإمام الرازي: هذا الكلام نص في أن القوة الهاضمة غير القوة الغاذية ويؤيده أنه جعل الغاذية مخدومة للقوى الأربع التي منها الهاضمة فلنتكلم في الفرق. فنقول: إذا جذبت جاذبة عضو شيئاً من الدم وأمسكته ماسكته فللدم صورة نوعية وإذا صار شبيهاً بالعضو فقد بطلت عنه هذه الصورة وحدثت صورة أخرى عضوية، فهناك كون للصورة العضوية، وفساد للصورة الدموية وإنما يحصلان إذا كان من الطبخ ما لأجله ينتقص استعداد المادة للصورة الدموية ويشتد استعدادها للصورة العضوية إلى أن تزول عنها الأولى وتحدث فيها الأخرى، فهاهنا حالتان إحداهما سابقة وهي تزايد استعداد قبول الصورة العضوية، والأخرى لاحقة حصول هذه الصورة فالحالة الأولى فعل القوة الهاضمة، والثانية فعل القوة الغاذية. وهذا معنى قوله: ( وهي ( أي الهضم الذي هو فعل الهاضمة ) استحالات ما واقعة بين تمام فعل الجاذبة وابتداء حصول فعل الغاذية التي هي كون ما أعني حصول الصورة العضوية).
The second of the four serving [powers] is the digestive (al-hāḍima), and it prepares the food until it becomes a part in actuality of the organ. So it is other than the nutritive (al-ghādhīya), I mean the power that requires the foodstuffs to become a part in actuality. And in the Kulliyāt al-Qānūn: As for the digestive, it is that which transforms what the attractive power attracted and the retentive power retained into a consistency prepared for the action of the altering power therein, and into a temperament suitable for transformation into actual nutrition. Imām al-Rāzī said: This speech is explicit that the digestive power is other than the nutritive power, and it is supported by his making the nutritive power served by the four powers, among which is the digestive. So let us speak about the difference. We say: When an organ's attractive power attracts something of blood and its retentive power retains it, the blood has a specific form. When it becomes similar to the organ, this form becomes invalid and another organic form occurs. So there is a coming-into-being of the organic form and a corruption of the blood form. These two only occur if there is a cooking [process] by which the readiness of the matter for the blood form decreases and its readiness for the organic form intensifies, until the first form ceases and the other occurs in it. So here there are two states: one prior, which is the increase of readiness to receive the organic form, and the other subsequent, which is the actual obtaining of this form. So the first state is the act of the digestive power, and the second is the act of the nutritive power. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And it (i.e., digestion which is the act of the digestive power) is certain transformations occurring between the completion of the act of the attractive power and the beginning of the actualization of the act of the nutritive power, which is the coming-into-being, i.e., the obtaining of the organic form.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the digestive power (الهاضمة) as one of the four serving powers (الخادمة) that prepare food for nutrition. It distinguishes it from the nutritive power (الغاذية). The author quotes from Kulliyāt al-Qānūn, describing the digestive power as transforming what the attractive and retentive powers have handled into a proper consistency and temperament for nutrition. Imām al-Rāzī affirms this distinction, noting that the nutritive power is served by the four powers, including the digestive. The author then explains the difference through an analysis of two states: first, the matter's readiness for the organic form increases (due to cooking/digestion) — this is the act of the digestive power; second, the organic form actually obtains — this is the act of the nutritive power. Thus digestion is a preparatory transformation between attraction and nutrition.

Misconception: One might think the digestive and nutritive powers are the same because both relate to food.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: the digestive prepares (first state), while the nutritive actualizes (second state).

Checkpoint

Two Objections to the Digestive/Nutritive Distinction
Two objections to the separation of digestive and nutritive powers with supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections Imam al-Razi raised against the separate existence of digestive and nutritive powers, and how they challenge a foundational distinction in Aristotelian biology.

Core Idea
Objections that make digestion and nutrition one power challenge a foundational distinction in Aristotelian biology.

Imam al-Razi, a formidable critic, did not simply accept the standard division of faculties. In this passage, he levels two powerful objections against separating the digestive and nutritive powers. Both objections aim to collapse the distinction by showing that the same power does the work. Let's see how he argues and what the responses imply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two objections, each followed by supporting arguments and commentary.
The passage includes a commentary after the objections; don't skip it.
2
Identify the first objection
Find how al-Razi argues that the digestive power both moves food to similarity with the organ and conveys it, making it one with the nutritive power.
He uses the principle: whatever moves something to something else is the conveyor to it.
3
Understand the support for the first objection
Al-Razi cites Ibn Sina's argument about motion and rest to show that the cause removing food from its first state must also bring it to its final state, implying the digestive power alone suffices.
This actually strengthens the objection rather than refuting it.
4
Identify the second objection
The digestive power prepares the matter for the influx of the organic form; when readiness is complete, nutrition is done. Thus no separate nutritive power is needed.
Al-Razi notes that Galen did not list the nutritive power among the four serving powers.
5
Reflect on the debate
Consider how these objections challenge traditional faculty psychology and what counter-arguments might be offered.
The passage ends with a commentary clarifying textual points about 'thinning the thick' and 'thickening the thin'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعترض الإمام عليه أولا بما أشار إليه المنصف بقوله: ( ويمكن أن يقال: المحرك إلى مشابه العضو هو القوة الموصلة إليه ). وتقريره على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن القوة الهاضمة محركة للغذاء في الكيف إلى الصورة المشابهة لصورة العضو وكل ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء آخر فهو الموصل إلى ذلك الآخر، فيكون الفاعل للفعلين قوة واحدة. أما الصغرى فظاهرة إذ لا معنى للهضم إلا التحريك عن الصورة الغذائية إلى الصورة العضوية وأما الكبرى فظاهرة أيضاً لأن ما حرك شيئاً إلى شيء كان المتوجه إليه غاية للمحرك والمعنى بكونه غاية أن المقصود الأصلي هو فعل ذلك الشيء. وقد اعترف ابن سينا بذلك حيث احتج على أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً، فقال: محال أن يكون الواصل إلى حد ما واصلاً إليه بلا علة موجودة موصلة. ومحال أن تكون هذه العلة غير التي أزالت عن المستقر الأول، هذا كلامه وهو يقتضي أنه لما كان المزيل عن الصورة الدموية هو الهاضمة وجب أن يكون الموصل إلى العضوية أيضاً الهاضمة، فهي الغاذية لا غير. واعترض ثانياً بما ذكره المصنف بقوله: ( كيف والمراد بالقوة هنا المعدة للمادة لفيضان الصورة عليها والمفيض لها وهو واهب الصور والقوة الهاضمة هي المفيدة بطبخها ونضجها للاستعدادات المختلفة بالقوة أي الشدة والضعف التي من جملتها ما يعد المادة لفيضان الصورة العضوية وتلك القوة المفيدة لهذه الاستعدادات مغنية عن قوى أخرى في الأعضاء ) لأنه إذا تم الإعداد وكمل الاستعداد فاضت الصورة وتمت التغذية، فإذن لا فرق بين الهاضمة والغاذية ولذلك لم يذكر جالينوس في شيء من كتبه الغاذية. وقال ابن سينا: بل المسيحي على ما في سوى قوله: ( واعترض ثانياً ) أي اعترض الإمام ثانياً. وقوله: وقال ابن سينا عطف على قوله: لم يذكر أي، ولذلك قال ابن سينا وقوله: والأظهر وإنما قال: والأظهر ولم يقل: والظاهر أو الصواب لأن عد الأربعة من الغاذية يتضمن عد الهاضمة من الغاذية أيضاً لكن مع انضمام زيادة لا يحتاج إليها هاهنا وتلك الزيادة هي عد الثلاثة للأخرى من الغاذية. وقوله: بترقيق الغليظ متعلق بقوله: تعد الفضل أما ترقيق الغليظ فكما في البول والعرق وأما تغليظ الرقيق فكما في الغائط والقيء.
Then Imam [al-Razi] objected to him first by what the author indicated by his statement: 'It can be said: The mover to similarity with the organ is the conveying power [to it].' Its exposition according to what is in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya is that the digestive power moves the food in quality toward the form similar to the organ's form, and everything that moves something to something else is the conveyor to that other, so the agent of both acts is one power. As for the minor premise, it is obvious, for there is no meaning of digestion except moving from the food form to the organic form. And as for the major premise, it is also obvious, because what moves something to something has the destination as its end, and the meaning of its being an end is that the primary intention is the act of that thing. And Ibn Sina admitted this when he argued that between every two motions there is rest, saying: It is impossible for that which reaches a certain limit to reach it without an existing conveying cause. And it is impossible for this cause to be other than that which removed it from its first resting place. This is his speech, and it necessitates that since the remover from the blood form is the digestive power, then the conveyor to the organic form must also be the digestive power; thus it is the nutritive power, no other. And he objected secondly by what the author mentioned in his statement: 'How? The intended [power] here is the one that prepares the matter for the influx of form upon it, and the effuser of it is the Giver of Forms. The digestive power is the one that, by its cooking and ripening, produces the various preparations in potentiality (i.e., intensity and weakness), among which is that which prepares the matter for the influx of the organic form. And that power which produces these preparations suffices for other powers in the organs.' For when the preparation is complete and the readiness is perfect, the form flows and nutrition is completed. So then there is no difference between the digestive and the nutritive. Therefore Galen did not mention the nutritive power in any of his books [among] these four which we called the serving [powers]. And Ibn Sina said: ... (the rest is garbled).
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents two objections by Imam al-Razi against the distinction between the digestive and nutritive powers, along with responses. The first objection argues that the digestive power moves food towards similarity with the organ, and whatever moves something to something else is the conveyor to it; hence the same power that moves (digestive) is the one that conveys (nutritive), making them one. The response uses Ibn Sina's argument about motion and rest to argue that the cause that removes the food from its first state (the digestive) must also be the cause that brings it to the final state (the nutritive), implying they are the same. The second objection argues that the digestive power prepares the matter by cooking and ripening, producing the necessary readiness for the influx of the organic form; when readiness is complete, the form flows and nutrition is complete, so no separate nutritive power is needed. It notes that Galen did not list the nutritive power among the four serving powers. The passage then includes a commentary explaining the text, including the meaning of 'thinning the thick' and 'thickening the thin' in relation to excreta.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Razi's objections are quickly dismissed as weak or irrelevant.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Razi's objections are serious and supported by arguments from Ibn Sina and Galen, challenging a foundational distinction.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's objections challenge the independence of the nutritive power, arguing that the digestive alone suffices for both transformation and delivery.

The Two States of Nutrition and the Roles of Digestive and Nutritive Powers
Defines two states of nutrition and attributes them to digestive and nutritive powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two sequential states in the process of nutrition and how the digestive and nutritive powers are responsible for each state.

Core Idea
Digestion prepares the body; nutrition completes it — two distinct powers.

This passage from a classical text on natural philosophy explains the process of nutrition by distinguishing two distinct states. It clarifies the roles of the digestive and nutritive powers in transforming food into the organic form. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion between these related but different functions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author introduces two states.
Pay attention to the terms for the two powers.
2
Identify the two states
In your own words, describe the first state (increase of preparedness) and the second state (attainment of the organic form).
These are sequential.
3
Match states to powers
Notice which power is active in each state: digestive power for the first, nutritive power for the second.
The passage also defines digestion as transformations between powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي للصورة العضوية إلى أن تزول عنها الأولى وتحدث فيها الأخرى، فهاهنا حالتان إحداهما سابقة وهي تزايد استعداد قبول الصورة العضوية، والأخرى لاحقة حصول هذه الصورة فالحالة الأولى فعل القوة الهاضمة، والثانية فعل القوة الغاذية وهذا معنى قوله : ( وهي ( أي الهضم الذي هو فعل الهاضمة (استحالات ما) واقعة بين تمام فعل الجاذبة وابتداء حصول فعل الغاذية التي هي كون ما أعني حصول الصورة العضوية
And it is for the organic form until the first form departs and another occurs in it. So here are two states: one preceding which is the increase of preparedness to accept the organic form, and the other subsequent which is the attainment of this form. The first state is the action of the digestive power, and the second is the action of the nutritive power. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'And it (i.e. digestion which is the action of the digestive power) is (some transformations) occurring between the completion of the action of the attractive power and the beginning of the occurrence of the action of the nutritive power, which is the becoming of what I mean by the attainment of the organic form.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines two states in the process of nutrition: first, the increase of preparedness to receive the organic form, and second, the actual attainment of that form. The first state is attributed to the digestive power, the second to the nutritive power. The author then explains that digestion (action of the digestive power) consists of transformations that take place between the completion of the attractive power's action and the beginning of the nutritive power's action, which results in the organic form.

The Four Servants of the Nutritive Power
Ibn Sina's four powers of the nutritive soul enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's classification of the four powers serving the nutritive power and how it differs from Galen's view.

Core Idea
The nutritive power has four servants: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive.

This lesson examines a key classification in Islamic philosophical physiology. The discussion centers on the powers that serve the nutritive faculty. Ibn Sina, following Aristotle, distinguishes four specific powers, whereas Galen did not treat them as separate. The passage clarifies the proper enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between Galen and Ibn Sina.
Galen did not mention the nutritive power separately; he called these four 'servants'.
2
Identify the four powers
Find where Ibn Sina enumerates the four powers: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive. Notice how the digestive power is defined as changing food to resemble the organ.
The digestive power is explicitly included as one of the four.
3
Understand the commentary's role
The phrase 'الأظهر أن يقال' indicates a preference in interpretation: the digestive power is indeed part of the nutritive power.
This resolves any ambiguity about the classification.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters in the broader context of the soul's faculties and how it impacts the understanding of nutrition in the body.
This ties into the larger theory of the soul's powers.

ولذلك لم يذكر جالينوس في شيء من كتبه الغاذية هذه الأربع التي سميناها الخوادم وقال ابن سينا : بل المسيحي على ما في المباحث ( الغاذية أربع ) وعد هذه ( الأربع منها ) . والأظهر أن يقال : وعد الهاضمة منها حيث قال في باب القوى والأفعال والأرواح من كتاب المائة الغاذية : أربع الجاذبة والماسكة والهاضمة وهي التي تغير الغذاء وتجعله شبيهاً بالعضو المغتذى، والرابعة الدافعة
And therefore Galen did not mention in any of his books the nutritive power; these four which we called the servants. And Ibn Sina said: 'Rather, according to what is in the Investigations: the nutritive power is four, and he enumerated these four of them.' And the most apparent is to say: and he enumerated the digestive power among them, when he said in the chapter on powers, actions, and spirits from the Book of the Hundred: 'The nutritive power has four: the attractive, the retentive, the digestive which changes food and makes it similar to the nourished organ, and the fourth is the expulsive.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of powers. Galen did not treat the nutritive power separately but considered these four as servants. Ibn Sina, on the other hand, explicitly includes the digestive power as one of the four powers under the nutritive power: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive. The passage clarifies that the digestive power is properly enumerated as part of the nutritive power.

Preparatory Actions of the Digestive Power: Thinning, Thickening, Cutting
Explains the three preparatory actions of the digestive power and its two modes of operation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three preparatory actions of the digestive power (thinning, thickening, cutting) and the two modes of preparation (by itself or with water).

Core Idea
The digestive power prepares both food and waste through three physical processes.

The digestive power does more than break down food; it also prepares waste for expulsion. This passage explains three key actions—thinning, thickening, and cutting—that ensure waste can be eliminated efficiently. It then notes that these actions may be carried out by the digestive power alone or with the help of moisture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's description sink in before interpreting.
Focus on the three actions and the examples of animals.
2
Identify the three preparatory actions
The passage lists three actions: thinning the thick, thickening the thin, and cutting the viscous. Note the purpose of each: to make waste expellable.
3
Understand the two modes of preparation
The digestive power can prepare food either by its own heat alone (as in falcons, snakes, camels) or by mixing with moisture (as in humans).
This shows variation in animal physiology.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider why different animals have different digestive processes. This illustrates the wisdom in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And that the digestive power, just as it prepares the food suitable for becoming part of the organ, as previously mentioned, it also prepares the excess that is not suitable for assimilation, from it (i.e., from the food) for expulsion, by thinning the thick so that it can be expelled, and thickening the thin, for the body of the organ may absorb it due to its thinness, so those absorbed parts cannot be expelled. But if it is thickened, the organ does not absorb it and it is expelled completely. And cutting the viscous, for it sticks to the organ and cannot be expelled unless it is cut. And the preparation coming from the digestive power is either by itself, as in some animals like the falcon, whose heat dissolves the incoming food without needing water, and in the snake, which may eat dirt and make it into chyme without using water, and in the camel, which eats dry plants for days and does not drink water; or by mixing with water moisture, as in humans and most animals.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage details the preparatory actions of the digestive power. It not only prepares the food for assimilation but also processes the waste for expulsion. It does this by three operations: thinning the thick, thickening the thin, and cutting the viscous. The purpose is to ensure that waste can be expelled. The passage then describes two modes of preparation: the digestive power may operate by its own heat alone (as in some animals like falcons, snakes, and camels) or by mixing with water (as in humans and most animals).

First Stage of Digestion: In the Stomach
Describes the first stage of digestion in the stomach, beginning in the mouth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first stage of digestion as described in classical Islamic medicine, including its location and the transformation of food into chyme.

Core Idea
Digestion begins in the mouth, not the stomach, due to the continuous surface between them.

In this passage, the author explains the first of four stages of digestion, which occurs in the stomach but actually begins in the mouth. The description uses the analogy of a gourd with a long neck to illustrate the connection between the mouth and stomach. The author also provides a practical example involving chewed wheat to demonstrate the transformative effect of chewing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Understand the four stages context
Note that digestion has four stages; this lesson covers only the first. This sets the scope.
3
Examine the analogy of the gourd
The author compares the mouth-stomach connection to a gourd with a long neck to show how their surfaces are continuous.
This analogy highlights that the mouth is not separate from the stomach in the digestive process.
4
Analyze the example of chewed wheat
Chewed wheat applied to pustules is more effective than cooked or crushed wheat mixed with saliva, proving that chewing transforms the food's quality.
This example demonstrates that digestion starts in the mouth through the act of chewing.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why understanding the beginning of digestion is important for the overall process and for medical knowledge.

Then digestion, which is the action of the digestive power, has four stages. The first is in the stomach, in that it makes the food into chyme, which is a substance like thick kashk water in its whiteness and consistency. This stage begins in the mouth due to the connection of its surface with the surface of the stomach, until they become like one surface, similar to the inner surface of a gourd that has a long neck and a round head. For this reason, chewed wheat in ripening pustules does what cooked wheat does not, nor does crushed wheat mixed with saliva; so that indicates the transformation of its quality by chewing.

Commentary
This passage describes the first stage of digestion, which occurs in the stomach and turns food into chyme. It emphasizes that this stage begins in the mouth because the surface of the mouth is continuous with the surface of the stomach, analogous to a gourd with a long neck. The author gives an example: chewed wheat applied to a pustule is more effective than cooked or crushed wheat mixed with saliva, proving that chewing transforms the food's quality. This transformation is part of the digestion that starts in the mouth.

The Four Faculties of the Vegetative Soul
Enumerates the four faculties of the vegetative soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four faculties of the vegetative (nutritive) soul as enumerated by classical Islamic philosophers.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul has four distinct faculties: attraction, retention, digestion, and expulsion.

Before delving into the higher faculties of the animal and rational souls, it is essential to understand the foundational vegetative faculties that govern nutrition. This passage, drawn from a classical philosophical text, enumerates and briefly defines the four powers of the nutritive soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Note the key terms for the faculties.
The passage is concise; each faculty is named briefly.
2
Identify the four faculties

The digestive faculty is described as changing food to resemble the organ.
3
Understand the function of each
Based on the passage and commentary, explain what each faculty does: attraction draws food, retention holds it, digestion transforms it, expulsion expels waste.
These functions work together in the process of nutrition.
4
Reflect on the source
The passage references 'Kitab al-Mi'a al-Ghadhiya'. Consider why the author quotes this source and how it fits into the broader discussion of the soul's faculties.

Commentary

He quotes from 'Kitab al-Mi'a al-Ghadhiya' that they are: the attractive force (al-jadhiba), which draws food; the retentive force (al-masika), which holds it; the digestive force (al-hadima), which changes and assimilates food to the body; and the expulsive force (al-dafi'a), which expels waste.

The Digestive Faculty: Function and Modes
Explains the digestive faculty's function and its two modes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the digestive faculty prepares food and waste for assimilation and expulsion, and its two modes of action (by innate heat or by moisture).

Core Idea
The digestive faculty processes food by thinning, thickening, and cutting waste, acting either by its own heat or with moisture.

In this passage, the author explains the dual role of the digestive faculty: it not only prepares the nutritious part of food for assimilation but also prepares the waste for expulsion through mechanical and chemical processes. The passage also reveals that different animals employ different methods—some rely on innate heat alone, while others require additional moisture. This lesson will unpack these concepts and illustrate them with examples from nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the composition of food
Recognize that food has two components: one suitable for nourishment and one that is waste.
2
Learn the three processes for waste expulsion
The digestive faculty thins thick waste, thickens thin waste, and cuts viscous waste to facilitate expulsion.
These processes prevent waste from sticking to organs.
3
Distinguish the two modes of digestion
Compare digestion by innate heat (in falcons, snakes, camels) vs. digestion aided by moisture (in humans and most animals).
This shows the diversity of physiological mechanisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الغذاء مركب من جوهرين أحدهما صالح لأن يشبه بالمغتذي، والثاني غير صالح له و ( أن الهاضمة كما تعد الغذاء الصالح للجزئية ) على ما مر . ( تعد الفضل) الذي لا يصلح للتشبيه (منه) أي من الغذاء للدفع بترقيق الغليظ ) حتى يندفع ( وتغليظ الرقيق فإنه قد يتشربه جرم العضو لرقته فلا تندفع تلك الأجزاء المتشربة فيه. فإذا غلط لم يتشربه العضو واندفع بالكلية ) وتقطيع اللزج ) فإنه يلتزق بالعضو فلا يندفع إلا إذا قطع والإعداد الصادر من الهاضمة ) إما بذاتها كما في الجوارح مثل البازي فإن حرارتها تذيب الغداء الوارد عليها بلا احتياج إلى ماء وفي الحية فإنها ربما تأكل التراب وتجعله كيلوساً من غير استعانة بماء، وفي الجمل فإنه يأكل أياماً نباتاً يابساً ولا يشرب ماء (أو بمخالطة رطوبة ( مائية ( كما في الآدمي وأكثر الحيوانات
Know that food is composed of two substances: one suitable to become similar to the nourished body, and the other not suitable for it. And that the digestive faculty, just as it prepares the food suitable for becoming a component (as mentioned before), it also prepares the waste which is not suitable for assimilation from the food for expulsion by thinning the thick so that it is expelled, and thickening the thin – for the bodily organ might absorb it due to its thinness, so those absorbed parts would not be expelled; but if it becomes thick, the organ does not absorb it and it is expelled entirely – and cutting the viscous, for it sticks to the organ and is not expelled unless it is cut. And the preparation produced by the digestive faculty is either by its own essence, as in raptors such as the falcon, whose heat melts the food that comes to it without needing water; and in the snake, which may eat soil and turn it into chyme without the help of water; and in the camel, which eats dry plants for days and does not drink water – or by admixture with liquid moisture, as in humans and most animals.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains the operation of the digestive faculty. It begins by noting that food has two components: a good part that can be assimilated and a waste part that is expelled. The digestive faculty not only prepares the good part for assimilation but also prepares the waste for expulsion by thinning, thickening, or cutting it. Then it describes two modes of action: either by its own innate heat (as in falcons, snakes, and camels) or by the help of added moisture (as in humans and most animals).

The First Two Degrees of Digestion (Stomach and Liver)
Enumerates the first two degrees of digestion in the stomach and liver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two degrees of digestion: in the stomach (forming chyle) and in the liver (forming chymus and distinguishing the four humors), including the roles of the mouth, mesenteric veins, and portal vein.

Core Idea
Digestion proceeds from stomach chyle to liver chymus and humors.

In this passage, the author enumerates the four degrees of digestion and then elaborates on the first two. The first degree occurs in the stomach, where food becomes chyle, beginning in the mouth. The second degree occurs in the liver, where the subtle part of chyle is transported via the mesenteric veins and portal vein to be cooked into chymus, with the four humors becoming distinct. This reflects the Galenic medical understanding integrated into Islamic philosophical texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic and translation once without interruption. Notice the enumeration and the detailed descriptions.
2
Identify the two degrees
Locate the sections for the first degree (المعدة) and second degree (الكبد). Note the transition point.
3
Understand the first degree details
Focus on the formation of chyle, the role of the mouth, and the evidence from chewed wheat. Also note the marginal glosses.
4
Understand the second degree details
Trace the path of the subtle part of chyle through mesenteric veins to portal vein, and the cooking in the liver.
5
Reflect on the overall picture
Consider how these two stages connect to produce the fundamental substances (humors) for the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم للهضم مراتب أربع الأولى في المعدة بأن تجعل الغذاء كيلوسا وهو جوهر كماء الكشك الثخين في بياضه وقوامه قوله : (الثخين) صفة ماء الكشك وليس صفة الكشك كما يتوهم. وقوله : في بياضه متعلق بالتشبة في قوله : كماء أي هو شبيه في بياضه بماء الكشك والدماميل جمع دمل بتشديد الميم وهو القرح، وقوله : كالمصفاة وهي آلة التصفية. وقوله : طرفه الخارجي أي الخارج من الكبد ويتضاءل يقال : رجل متضائل أي شخت دقيق . وقوله : هذا العرق أي العرق الكبير. وهذه المرتبة تبتدئ في الفم لاتصال سطحه بسطح المعدة حتى كأنهما سطح واحد على طريقة السطح الباطن من القرع الذي له عنق طويل ورأس مدور ولذلك تفعل الحنطة الممضوغة في إنضاج الدماميل ما لا تفعله المطبوخة منها ولا المدقوقة المخلوطة بالريق فدل ذلك على استحالة كيفيتها المضغ المرتبة الثانية : في الكبد فإن الغذاء بعد ما صار كيلوسا إذا اندفع كثيفه إلى الأمعاء للدفع انجذب لطيفه من المعدة ومنها أي ومن تلك الأمعاء التي اندفع إليها الكثيف مختلطاً باللطيف إلى الكبد بطريق ماساريقا وهي عروق دقاق صلبة ضيقة تجاويفها واصلة بين الكبد وآخر المعدة وجميع الأمعاء كالمصفاة، قالوا : وإذا اندفع إلى ماساريقا صار إلى العرق المسمى بالكبد، وهو عرق كبير يتشعب كل واحد من طرفيه إلى شعب كثيرة دقيقة . فشعب طرفه الخارجي يتصل فوهاتها بفوهات الماساريقا وشعب طرفه الآخر تتصغر وتتضاءل وتدق جداً في الانشعاب والانقسام وتنفذ في الكبد بحيث لا يخلو شيء من أجزائه عن شعب هذا العرق فإذا نفذ لطيف الكيلوس فيها صار كل الكبد ملاقياً لكله فينطبخ فيها أي في الكبد انطباخاً تاماً ويصير كيموساً وتتميز الأخلاط الأربعة المتولدة هناك بعضها عن بعض
Then digestion has four degrees. The first is in the stomach, making food into chyle, which is a substance like thick porridge water in its whiteness and consistency. (Marginal note: 'الثخين' is a description of the water of porridge, not of the porridge as supposed... etc.) And this degree begins in the mouth due to the connection of its surface with the stomach's surface so that they are like one surface in the manner of the inner surface of a gourd that has a long neck and a round head. Therefore, chewed wheat does in ripening abscesses what cooked wheat does not, nor crushed wheat mixed with saliva. That indicates the transformation of its quality by chewing. The second degree is in the liver: after the food becomes chyle, when its thick part is pushed to the intestines for expulsion, its subtle part is attracted from the stomach and from the intestines to which the thick part was pushed, mixed with the subtle, to the liver via the mesentery veins. These are delicate, hard, narrow-cavity veins connecting the liver to the end of the stomach and all the intestines, like a filter. They said: when it pushes into the mesentery, it goes to the vein called the portal vein, which is a large vein branching from each of its two ends into many delicate branches. The branches of its outer end connect their openings with the openings of the mesentery, and the branches of its other end become smaller, more slender, and very thin in branching and division, and penetrate the liver such that no part of the liver is free from the branches of this vein. So when the subtle part of the chyle penetrates into them, the entire liver comes into contact with all of it, and it cooks in the liver a complete cooking and becomes chymus, and the four humors generated there become distinguished from each other.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four degrees of digestion. The first degree occurs in the stomach, where food is turned into chyle, resembling thick porridge water. This process begins in the mouth due to the continuity of the oral cavity with the stomach. The proof is that chewed wheat helps ripen abscesses better than cooked or crushed wheat. The second degree occurs in the liver: after the chyle's thick part goes to the intestines, the subtle part is attracted via the mesenteric veins to the liver. The portal vein branches throughout the liver so that the chyle is thoroughly cooked, becoming chymus, and the four humors are distinguished from each other. The marginal notes clarify terms like 'thick' (الثخين) and 'filter' (المصفاة).

Key Terms
الهضم
الكيلوس
الكيموس
ماساريقا
الأخلاط الأربعة
العرق المسمى بالكبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Digestion is a simple single-stage process.
Correction: The passage shows it involves multiple stages: first in the mouth/stomach forming chyle, then in the liver forming chymus and humors.

Checkpoint
What is the name given to the substance formed in the liver after cooking?
A) Chyle
B) Chymus
C) Humor
D) Blood

Key Takeaway
Digestion involves sequential transformations: from food to chyle in the stomach, then to chymus and humors in the liver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat your next meal, will you remember that your liver must 'cook' the chyle into humors? How does that awareness change your sense of gratitude for the hidden processes that sustain you every moment?
?
The passage describes the mesenteric veins as a filter, separating what is nourishing from what is waste. What 'filters' has Allah placed in your life to separate harmful influences from beneficial ones? Are you paying attention to them?
?
In your own life, do you rush from one degree of understanding to the next without letting the second degree—the deeper digestion—occur? What would it look like to allow your heart and mind to fully 'cook' a truth before moving on?

The Formation of the Four Humors
Explains the formation and classification of the four humors from cooked chymus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four humors (yellow bile, black bile, blood, and phlegm) are formed from cooked chymus and the distinction between their natural and unnatural states.

Core Idea
The four humors are born from cooked chymus; each has a natural balanced state.

In classical Islamic medicine and cosmology, the four humors are fundamental substances that constitute the body. This passage from the sharh explains how the liver cooks chymus into three categories of parts—fiery, earthy, and intermediate—which give rise to the four humors with specific qualities. Understanding this process is key to grasping the humoral theory of health and disease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see how the humors are derived from cooked chymus. Note the specific qualities (pungency, acidity, sweetness) assigned to each.
2
Identify the four humors
The passage lists yellow bile, black bile, blood, and phlegm. Notice how each humor corresponds to a type of part from the chymus: fiery, earthy, or intermediate.
Blood and phlegm come from intermediate parts, with blood being fully cooked and phlegm undercooked.
3
Understand natural vs unnatural
Each humor can be natural or unnatural due to temperament change or admixture. This distinction is crucial for understanding health and disease.
The passage explains both the material and efficient causes for each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأن الأجزاء اللطيفة النارية (منه أي ما كان من أجزائه لطيفاً فيه نارية ) أي حرارة ويبس ( تتجاوز نضجه ) وتميل إلى الاحتراق ) ولخفته يعلوها ) أي ولخفة ما يجاوز نضجه يعلو سائر الأجزاء الغذائية ( كالرغوة وهي الصفراء فيها حرافة ) لما مر أن فاعل الحرافة الحرارة المفرطة وحاملها الجسم اللطيف . قالوا : والطبيعي من الصفراء رغوة الدم وسببه الفاعلي هو الحرارة ،المعتدلة وأما المحترق منها ففاعله الحرارة النارية في الغاذية (و) الأجزاء ( الكثيفة الأرضية ) أي التي فيها برودة ويبس. إما لطبعها وإما لشدة احتراقها وصيرورتها إلى طبيعة الرماد يرسب فيها ) أي في الأجزاء الغذائية ) كالعكر وهي السوداء وفيها حموضة) . قالوا : والطبيعي من السوداء عكر الدم وطعمه بين الحلاوة والعفوصة. وما ينصب منها إلى فم المعدة ليدغدعها وينبه على الجوع حامض أعفص، وسببه الفاعلي حرارة معتدلة وأما المحترق فيها ففاعله حرارة مجاوزة عن الاعتدال والسبب المادي للسوداء هو الشديد الغليظ القليل الرطوبة من الأغذية ( وما يبقى بينهما ) أي بين الرغوة والعكر ( منه ما قدتم نضجه وهو الدم وهو حلو أي مائل إلى الحلاوة فيكون حلواً بالقياس إلى المرتين ( ومنه ما هو فج) أي نيء لم يطبخ انطباخاً تاماً (بعد كأنه دم غير تام النضج وهو البلغم وفيه حلاوة ما لكونها دماً غير نضيج ( وكلما كان ) البلغم ( أقرب إلى النضج كان أحلى ) لزيادة قربه حينئذ من الدم ) وكل واحد من هذه الأربعة إما طبيعي وإما طبيعي، وذلك أعني كونه غير طبيعي ( إما لتغير مزاجه في نفسه عن الاعتدال الواجب له الذي به يصلح لأن يصير جزءا من الأعضاء. ( وإما لمخالطة مخالط ) أخلاط أخر غير طبيعية أو رطوبة غريبة ترد عليه من خارج (ولها) أي منه من إياه من قوله: (ويصير كيموساً) قيل : هذا اللفظ سرياني بمعني الخلط سواء كان صالحاً لأن يحصل منه ما ينبغي للبدن أو لم يكن صالحاً لذلك بل كان فاسداً في نفسه. وقوله : منه أي من الغذاء، وكلمة من هاهنا تبعيضية. وقوله : وأما المحترق منها أي من الصفراء. وقوله : ترسب فيها أي تسفل فيها والعكر دردى الزيت وغیره و دردى الزيت ما يبقى في أسفله كذا في الصحاح. وقوله : إلى المرتين أي إلى الصفراء والسوداء وهو يروى بضم الميم وتشديد الراء على أن يكون فيهما مرارة في الجملة لأن الحرافة أو العفوصة لا تخلو عن مرارة ما وقد يروى بكسر الميم وتشديد الراء أيضاً على تغليب الصفراء على السوداء إذ المرة هي الصفراء. وقوله : الواجب له الضمير في له راجع إلى مزاجه أي مزاج كل واحد ، والضمير في يصلح راجع إلى كل واحد أيضاً. وقوله : من جانبه المحدب أي الجانب المحدب للكبد
That is because the subtle fiery parts (from it, i.e., what is subtle with fieriness (heat and dryness) exceed its cooking and tend toward burning) and due to their lightness they rise above (i.e., due to the lightness of what exceeds its cooking, it rises above the other nutritive parts) like froth, and that is the yellow bile, which has pungency. ... (marginal note: 'ويصير كيموساً' is said to be Syriac meaning mixture... etc.) ... And every one of these four is either natural or unnatural; that is, its being unnatural: either due to change of its own temperament from the required moderation by which it becomes fit to be part of the organs, or due to admixture with admixtures: other unnatural humors or foreign moisture coming from outside. And it has... (marginal note about 'من جانبه المحدب' meaning the convex side of the liver).
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage explains how the four humors originate from the cooking of chymus in the liver. It first discusses the fine fiery parts that become yellow bile (الصفراء) with pungency, the coarse earthly parts that become black bile (السوداء) with acidity, and the intermediate parts that become blood (الدم) which is sweet, and phlegm (البلغم) which is undercooked and slightly sweet. Each humor has both natural and unnatural states; unnatural states arise from temperament change or admixture with foreign substances. Marginal notes clarify the Syriac origin of 'kymus' and other terms like 'المرتين' referring to the two biles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All humors are inherently pathological and harmful to the body.
Correction: Each humor has a natural balanced state that is necessary for health; only unnatural states are harmful.

Key Takeaway
The four humors arise from different parts of cooked chymus in the liver, each having a natural balanced state essential for health and an unnatural state causing disease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The natural black bile stimulates hunger—a sign that even what seems like 'waste' has purpose. What 'waste' in your life (time, habits, emotions) might Allah have designed for a beneficial purpose that you have ignored? Will you reconsider before dismissing it?
?
You have a natural temperament (mizaj) that is a balance of humors. Do you see your physical constitution as a trust from Allah, with its own wisdom, or do you constantly fight against it? What would it look like to honor your body's natural balance this week?
?
The passage distinguishes natural from unnatural humors. In your spiritual life, what desires or inclinations are natural (fitrah) and what have become unnatural through excess or neglect? How will you seek to restore balance starting today?

The Third and Fourth Ranks of Digestion: Description and Correction
Describes the third and fourth digestion stages and corrects defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth stages of digestion in classical Islamic medicine, including the pathway of nutrients from the liver to organs and the three types of assimilation defects.

Core Idea
Digestion ends as nutrients seep into organs, where each organ must attract, assimilate, and adhere.

This passage continues the examination of digestion by describing the final two stages. After the liver produces humors, they travel through veins and are further refined. In the fourth stage, nutrients seep into organs, where each organ actively attracts and assimilates them. The passage also identifies three defects that can occur in this final assimilation and corrects them according to the Eastern Investigations.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structured description of the third and fourth ranks.
The passage uses hierarchical anatomical terms.
2
Trace the pathway of nutrients
Identify the sequence of vessels: from the hollow vein (al-ajwaf) to its branches, then to streams (jadawil), channels (sawaqi), suckling vessels (rawadi'), and finally fibrous capillaries (liyafiyya). Understand that nutrients seep out of these capillaries onto the organs.
This shows the sophistication of medieval anatomy.
3
Identify the three defects
The passage lists three defects in assimilation: atrophy (quantity), vitiligo/leprosy (color), and fleshy dropsy (consistency). Note how these are corrected in the next step.
Defects are not in the food but in the organ's ability.
4
Understand the correction
Read the correction: dropsy is a defect in adhesion, atrophy in replacement of dissolved substances, and vitiligo/leprosy in assimilation of consistency and essence. This aligns with the Eastern Investigations.
This reflects the integration of theory and pathology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتبة الثالثة : في العروق فإن الأخلاط الأربعة بعد تولدها في الكبد تنصب إلى العرق الثابت من جانبه المحدب المسمى بالأجوف المقابل للعرق النابت من مقعره المسمى بالباب ثم تندفع الأخلاط في العروق المتشعبة من الأجوف مختلطة بعضها ببعض وفيها تنهضم الأخلاط انهضاماً تاماً فوق ما كان لها في الكبد . وهناك يتميز ما يصلح غذاء لكل عضو فيصر مستعداً لأن تجذبه جاذبة العضو . المرتبة الرابعة : في الأعضاء فإن الغذاء إذا سلك في العروق الكبار إلى الجداول ثم منها إلى السواقي ثم إلى الرواضع ثم إلى العروق الليفية ترشح الغذاء من فوهاتها أي فوهات الليفية الشعرية على الأعضاء وحصل لها في الأعضاء كل عضو حصل غاذية كل للأغذية المترشحة عليها التشبه به التصاقا وقد يخل به كفي الذبول ولونا وقد يخل به كفى البرص والبهق وفي القوام، وقد يخل به كفى الاستسقاء اللحمي . والصواب الموافق للمباحث المشرقية ما قدمناه من أن الإخلال في الاستسقاء اللحمي بالالتصاق، وفي الذبول في تحصيل بدل ما يتحلل وفي البرص والبهق في التشبه من حيث القوام والماهية.
The third rank: concerning the veins. Indeed the four humors after their generation in the liver pour into the fixed vein from its convex side called the hollow vein opposite the vein growing from its concave side called the portal vein. Then the humors rush into the veins branching from the hollow vein mixed with one another, and in them the humors are completely digested beyond what they had in the liver. And there what is suitable as nourishment for each organ is distinguished, so it becomes ready to be attracted by the attracting force of the organ. The fourth rank: concerning the organs. Indeed when food travels in the large veins to the streams, then from them to the channels, then to the suckling vessels, then to the fibrous veins, the food seeps from their openings—i.e., the openings of the fibrous capillaries—onto the organs. And for them, in the organs, each organ acquires an attractor for the foods seeping onto it, assimilation to it by adhesion, and it may be defective in it, like in atrophy in terms of what replaces what dissolves, and in color like in vitiligo and leprosy, and in consistency like in fleshy dropsy. And the correct view in accordance with the Eastern Investigations is what we presented: that the defect in fleshy dropsy is in adhesion, and in atrophy in acquiring a replacement for what dissolves, and in vitiligo and leprosy in assimilation in terms of consistency and essence.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the third and fourth stages of digestion. It first describes how the four humors, after being produced in the liver, move through the vein system (third stage) and undergo further digestion. Then it explains the fourth stage: the suitable food is carried through a hierarchical network of vessels—large veins, streams, channels, suckling vessels, and finally fibrous capillaries—from which it seeps onto the organs. Each organ then attracts and assimilates the food by adhesion. The passage identifies three possible defects in this process: in quantity (atrophy), in color (vitiligo and leprosy), and in consistency (fleshy dropsy). It then corrects these according to the Eastern Investigations, specifying that dropsy involves a defect in adhesion, atrophy in the replacement of what dissolves, and vitiligo/leprosy in the assimilation in terms of consistency and essence.

الليفية
التشبه
الالتصاق
الذبول
البرص
البهق
الاستسقاء
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The defects are simply in the food substance itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that defects are in the organ's ability to attract, assimilate, and adhere, not in the food. For example, dropsy is a defect in adhesion, not in the quality of lymph.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three types of defects in the assimilation of nutrients?
A) Defects in adhesion, replacement, and assimilation
B) Defects in quantity (atrophy), color (vitiligo/leprosy), and consistency (dropsy)
C) Defects in the veins, liver, and organs
D) Defects in the humors, vessels, and tissues

Key Takeaway
The movement of nutrients from the liver to organs involves multiple stages and three types of assimilation defects that are corrected by understanding the underlying mechanisms of adhesion, replacement, and assimilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken in 'nutrients'—like knowledge, advice, or worship—but failed to truly adhere to them? What defect (adhesion, replacement, or assimilation) might you have experienced, and how can you seek correction?
?
The organs must attract, assimilate, and adhere. Which of these three steps do you think you struggle with most in your spiritual life? What concrete change can you make today to move from assimilation to adhesion?

The Four Wastes of Digestion
Lists the waste products of the four digestion stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the waste products associated with each of the four stages of digestion according to classical medical theory.

Core Idea
Digestion leaves four wastes, each affecting the body differently.

In this passage, the author enumerates the residues produced at each stage of digestion. These wastes must be expelled because they cannot become part of the nourished substance. The list includes a note of disagreement about the origin of urine, and concludes with a remark on the particular weakening effect of semen evacuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the four stages and their wastes.
2
Identify each waste
For each stage, note the waste product(s) mentioned. Pay attention to the author's comment about urine and the controversy.
3
Reflect on the effect of semen
Consider why evacuating a small amount of semen is more weakening than larger amounts of other wastes.
This connects to the concept of original organic substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان ( الأول : إن لكل مرتبة من مراتب الهضم فضلاً لا يصلح أن يصير جزءاً من المغتذي فيحتاج إلى دفعة. فللأولى التي في معدة الثفل الذي يندفع من طريق الأمعاء. وللثانية التي في الكبد البول وهو الأكثر والمرتان السوداء والصفراء المندفعتان من الطحال والمرارة. وللثالثة التي في العروق الرطوبة المائية المندفعة بالبول والأبخرة التي تصير عرقاً. وجعل البول فضلة للمرتبة الثالثة مخالف لما في المباحث المشرقية والمشهور الأطباء. وللرابعة المني ولذلك يضعف استفراغ القليل منه ما لا يضعف مثله استفراغ أضعافه من الدم أو سائر الأخلاط، وذلك لأن استفراغه يورث وهناً في جواهر الأعضاء الأصلية المتولدة من المني دون غيره من الأخلاط.
Two notes: The first: Indeed for each stage of digestion there is a waste that cannot become a part of the nourished substance, so it needs to be expelled. For the first stage (in the stomach): the feces which is expelled through the intestines. For the second (in the liver): urine, which is the most, and the two biles—black and yellow—expelled from the spleen and gallbladder. For the third (in the veins): the aqueous moisture expelled with urine and the vapors that become sweat. And making urine a waste of the third stage contradicts what is in the Eastern Investigations and the famous opinion of physicians. For the fourth: the semen, and therefore evacuating a small amount of it weakens the body in a way that evacuating multiples of it from blood or other humors does not weaken. That is because its evacuation causes weakness in the substances of the original organs generated from semen, unlike other humors.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a note listing the waste products of each digestion stage. It begins by stating that each stage produces a residue that cannot be assimilated. The first stage (stomach) produces feces; the second (liver) produces urine and two biles; the third (veins) produces aqueous moisture (urine) and vapors (sweat) — but it notes a disagreement with the Eastern Investigations and common medical opinion, which consider urine a waste of the second stage, not the third. The fourth stage (reproduction) produces semen, and evacuating even a small amount is particularly weakening because it affects the core organic substances generated from semen, unlike other humors.

المرة الصفراء
الرطوبة المائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Urine is a waste of the third stage of digestion.
Correction: According to the author, considering urine a waste of the third stage contradicts the Eastern Investigations and famous medical opinion; it is correctly a waste of the second stage (liver).

Checkpoint
Which waste product is associated with the fourth stage of digestion?
A) Urine
B) Feces
C) Semen
D) Bile

Key Takeaway
The waste products of the four digestion stages are feces, urine/biles, sweat/moisture, and semen, with semen evacuation being the most weakening because it depletes the original organic substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that evacuating a small amount of semen weakens you far more than losing much blood. Most people dismiss this as mere physiology. But if you truly believe this, what must change in how you guard your body? Will you continue to neglect this vulnerability?
?
The passage highlights that urine is a waste of the liver (second stage), not the veins. This detail was debated by scholars. How often do you accept a piece of knowledge without questioning its source? After today, pick one belief you hold about your body and verify it against authoritative texts.
?
Every waste product in your body, from feces to sweat, is a sign of Allah's design in purification. But you probably never thank Allah for your waste elimination. Starting now, what specific du'a or reflection will you attach to these daily functions?

The Retaining Force: Definition and Proof
Defines the retaining force and proves its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the retaining force (al-masika) and the rational proof for its existence in the stomach.

Core Idea
The retaining force actively holds food, not passive stomach fullness.

This lesson introduces the third of the four serving forces: the retaining force, which holds food in the stomach until digestion begins. The author defines it and then offers a simple yet clever proof for its existence, distinguishing it from mere physical fullness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition of the retaining force: it holds food until the digestive force acts. Notice the author's comment about its order relative to the digestive force.
The author notes it should logically come before the digestive force, but he delayed it for a reason.
2
Analyze the proof
The author proves the retaining force by saying the stomach contains food from all sides. He then argues that this is not due to fullness because a full stomach would make sounds (qaraqir). So there must be an active force retaining the food.
The sounds indicate emptiness, not fullness.
3
Identify the common misunderstanding
Some might think the stomach simply holds food passively. The passage corrects this: it's an active force, not just the stomach's capacity.
This misconception is explicitly addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة من الأربع الخادمة الماسكة وهي القوة التي تمسك الغذاء ريثما تفعل فيه الهاضمة فعلها فالأنسب أن يقدم ذكرها على الهاضمة كما فعله الإمام الرازي وابن سينا، وكأنه إنما أخرها لأخذه الهاضمة في تفسيرها. ويثبتها أي يثبت وجود الماسكة في المعدة احتواءها على الغذاء من كل الجوانب وليس ذلك لامتلاء المعدة فإنها لو كانت ممتلئة لكان منها أصوات القراقر والقراقرة كالإحساء.
The third of the four serving forces: the retaining force, which is the force that holds the food until the digestive force acts upon it. So it is more appropriate to mention it before the digestive force, as Imam al-Razi and Ibn Sina did. It is as if he only delayed it because he included the digestive force in its definition. And it is proven, i.e., the existence of the retaining force is proven in the stomach by its containing the food from all sides, and that is not due to the fullness of the stomach, because if it were full, there would be from it sounds of 'qaraqir' and 'qaraqira' like 'ihsa'.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines the third of the four serving forces: the retaining force (al-masika), which holds food in the stomach until the digestive force acts. It notes that logically it should be mentioned before the digestive force, as al-Razi and Ibn Sina did, but the author delayed it because he used the digestive force in the definition. The proof of its existence is that the stomach holds food from all sides, and this is not due to the stomach being full, because if it were full, it would produce sounds (like gurgling) indicating emptiness. Thus, there must be a force actively retaining the food.

Key Terms
الماسكة
الهاضمة
المعدة
القراقر
الإحساء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The retaining force is simply the physical capacity of the stomach to hold food.
Correction: The passage shows that the stomach's containment is not passive fullness, but an active force—otherwise a full stomach would make sounds.

Checkpoint
How is the existence of the retaining force (al-masika) proven in the stomach?
A) By the stomach making sounds when full
B) By the stomach containing food from all sides, and this is not due to fullness
C) By the authority of al-Razi and Ibn Sina
D) By the digestive force requiring prior retention

Key Takeaway
The retaining force is an active power that holds food in the stomach, proven by the fact that the stomach retains food without being passively full.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that food is actively held in the stomach until digestion can occur. Consider the beneficial knowledge or deeds you have 'ingested' recently—are you passively letting them sit, or are you actively retaining them until they bear fruit? What specific practice can you adopt today to actively 'hold' one piece of beneficial knowledge until it transforms your heart or actions?
?
The proof of the retaining force is the absence of gurgling sounds; if the stomach were merely full, it would make noise. Think about your own spiritual state: what 'gurgling' signs—like internal restlessness, distraction, or lack of peace—indicate that you are only superficially 'full' of faith but not actively retaining it? What one step will you take this week to move from passive fullness to active retention of iman?
?
In the passage, the retaining force is defined by its purpose: holding food until the digestive force acts. Identify one area of your life (a habit, a relationship, a commitment) where you are trying to 'digest' or process something too quickly, without allowing an active holding period. How can you intentionally 'retain' that matter longer, trusting the process before forcing results?
